And I have placed there chapters from head to foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same path so that the seeker may find what they are looking for.
And before I begin that, my sons, you must learn that what is in this chapter regarding fear differs from the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of this it is necessary for there to be greater caution, since in the operation which is described in this chapter, there frequently occurs an evacuation with blood, together with which stands life at the opening of a vein or incision on a boil, abscess, wound, arrow extraction, or stone cutting, and those similar: from all of which arises trembling, fear, and death falls upon many.
And I advise you not to fall into uncertainty over that which is doubtful, for sometimes such kinds of people come before you with such kinds of ailments. For amongst them are those who become angry when suffering from illness and for whom death is a light matter because of the severity of what they experience through their ailment and the long duration of its evil. And when illness arises from fear that signifies death.
And amongst them is he who offers you his flute-player, enriching you with him in the hope of health, while his sickness is destructive.
Therefore, it should not be that you agree with him who comes to you from amongst them whose manner is such. And let your caution be more severe than your greed and avarice. And do not presume about anything concerning him unless after certain knowledge which verifies to you that for which his worthy lineage has arrived.
And use for the treatment of all your patients prognosis and indication regarding that which is appropriate for health.
For in it lies your aid regarding the achievement of fame and glory, memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding and not withhold from you guidance and inspiration, for that is in His hand.
And indeed I have arranged this Chapter by dividing it into chapters as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization from the neck down to the feet so that your search for what you wish and need out of it may be lightened.
The treatment for water accumulation on children's heads.
Chapter 1.
You should know that this condition often happens to children at birth or when the midwife grips the child's head without caution.
And whenever it happens, there is also an unknown hidden cause that cannot be known.
Indeed, I have not seen this sickness anywhere but in children, and among those whom I saw all rushed into death; for that reason, it seemed to me best to abandon any treatment of such a condition.
And I have seen a child among them whose head was filled with water and his head kept growing every day until he could not sit upright due to the size of his head; moisture kept accumulating until death.
And this moisture may gather either between the skin and bone or beneath the bone above the skull.
And for the treatment of this condition, if there is moisture between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make an incision on top of the head running across its width; let the length of the incision be about two finger widths until the moisture drains out.
And this is the shape of a spatula.
If then the moisture is more accumulated, and the abscess is larger, you should make two such incisions.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, and its sign is that you see openings on top of the head from every side; and water is pressed down when you press it with your hand towards the inside, but it does not hide itself: then you should make three incisions on top of the head like this.
And after making an incision, extract all moisture. Then close the wounds with cloths and plugs. After that, anoint it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with sutures and ointments.
And do not fail to keep the head bound tightly with its counterpart. Feed the patient dry food of little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
Another method of incision is to note where the swelling appears large and water collects.
For sometimes it is greater at the back of the head, or on the forehead, or to the right, or left.
Thus ascend with your incision to where the swelling and water collection appear, then split it as seems best to you.
Take care not to cut an artery, lest the patient bleed out and die from that evacuation of moisture.
### On Incision of the arteries found behind the ears.
Chapter two.
When someone has catarrh to the eyes or chest and it is not relieved by medicinal treatments, then this ultimate cure remains only for them - incision of these two arteries.
When you wish to make such an incision then the patient should lean their head back. Then rub the spot with a rough cloth to make the arteries visible. Then the patient should tighten their neck with each of their cloths. Then consider where the vein pulses. And the places of both (veins) are the sunken areas behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then mark with encaustic on both (veins): and cut both (veins) with spatums on either side using a needle, the incision reaching to the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatums under the artery, and cut it above with an upward incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood flows out in a pulsating manner, which jumps into the interiors with frequent leaps.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you should measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with encaustic, and find (the artery) until you reach the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let out is six ounces, and perhaps you emit more or less from it. All of that according to the amount which appears to you of the patient's strength and the fullness of their artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything of it remains reaching to the bone, then cut that, so that a hot abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with linen cloths.
Treat it then with ointment until it is healed.
On the extraction of arteries which are in temples.
Chapter 3.
When a person suffers from an old headache or acute catarrh due to acrid moistness and heat in the arteries of the temples, or from old, severe, or similar sodden humours: And if it is treated with medical remedies but does not yield to them: Then we have found in these ailments that the extraction of arteries from the temples or their cauterisation is efficacious as we have described.
And the method of operating on such an extraction is for the patient to shave off the hair which is on his temples and concentrate on the artery which appears therein. For it shows itself to you by its pulse; rarely is it hidden except in very few people or severe coldness.
If it is hidden from you then the patient should bind his head with any cloth; afterwards rub that place with a rough cloth. Or bathe the place with warm water until it appears to you clearly. Then take the spatula which I formed just now. Afterwards scrape with it the skin gently until you reach that artery. Then throw into it a hook and extract it upwards until you have extracted it from the skin; then make it escape out of any garments which are underneath.
If however the artery is thin; then wrap it with the tip of the hook. Then cut away a portion of it according to the extent by which its ends extend, and they are pulled back without causing any loss of blood. If it is not cut, nor if it is severed, the blood does not stop flowing entirely.
Then extract six ounces of blood from it until three remain.
If the artery is large, you should tie it in two places using double-threaded silk string or strong chords of aloe wood so that corruption does not quickly set into its substance before wound healing and blood loss occurs.
Then cut away the superfluity which is between the two ligatures. Do this at that hour or one hour later.
And if you wish, cauterise it with the knife-shaped cautery until reaching its bone, so that its ends may be excised and it will remain at this same place.
And the outcome is better unless the patient has fever or they have a hot constitution. Because the cautery is among those things which help to end moistness. Therefore it helps more effectively.
And after the removal of arteries, the place should be filled with old tallow and decently made crumbled poultices placed over it.
And after the discharge, it should be treated with drying medicines which promote flesh generation as well as dressings until healing occurs.
If in the meantime while operating a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to sever it with the cautery or fill up its place with egg white and squeeze your hand until the blood is severed. If none of these are present to you at this moment, then place your finger on the wound until the blood freezes. Wet the place with ice-cold water until the sharpness subsides and it tightens as needed.
And for that which is lighter and easier to remove by means of the artery, you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives; and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to that of almagdate. However, they are much less sharp than two knives. Because if they were sharp like two knives, it would hasten both the cold; and they wouldn't cut into the flesh quickly. Since there is a bit of thickness to them, some heat from the fire is retained within them, and they cut through flesh quickly; with this it's best to use this approach for every operation that requires speed, ease, and gentleness.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be a thickness equal to that of your finger, after you mark both places with encaustic; and place each one red hot until they reach the mouth, and cut into the vein in two spots: so as to lengthen what is between its ends. For it doesn't fully consolidate with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment for frequent sharp pain in the eyes.
When tears are frequent and their course is from veins that are on the outside of the head; and you have not tried any cure with medicine or remedies, and see that the patient's face has now turned red, and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead like ants crawling: and their eyes are exhausted, moist, so much that already their lashes are corroded by corrosion: and their eyelids are heated from the sharpness of tears; then the ancients required this operation for their cure, and named it *a cura cum ensi*, or "treatment with a sword".
And it is essential to advise the patient to shave the hair from their forehead. Then make three incisions on the forehead, evenly spaced according to the length of the forehead; let the length of each incision be about two fingers long, and have one incision equidistant from the nose in the center of the forehead, a second along the length slightly away from the movement of the eyebrows, and a third on the other side. And beware that you do not cut any arteries on either side, and stretch your hand out from the connection of the two jaws, and let the entire space of the incision be equal to three fingers put together: also have for yourself a piece of prepared sponge or many dry cloths with which to dry up the blood.
Then insert a spatula of two sharp ends, whose subsequent shape is as follows: [diagram not included]. From the first incision near the temple to the second incision, scrape away all skin that lies between the two incisions, along with the bone above the eye socket. Then do likewise from the middle incision to the second one. Then withdraw the spatula, and reinsert another tool into the first incision, which is called a sharp knife, blunt on one side but sharp on the other. And this is its form: [diagram not included]
Position the sharp side of its edge towards the attached flesh and skin, and the blunt side towards the bone; push it in until you reach the middle incision, and sever with it all those vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except for what continues with the incision up to where skin is visible.
Then do likewise at the middle incision down to the other one.
After some quantity of blood has drained out evenly, squeeze those places from pieces of clotted blood.
Then put linen rags soaked in old wine on every crack, and place over them little pads moistened with wine or oil, or vinegar. See to it that no hot abscess occurs.
On the third day, loosen the bandage and apply plenty of wash with warm water. Then treat it with healing ointment of basil and other things that are used to heal wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the treatment of sharp pains in the eyes, also of catarrh from an internal head cold.
When someone has severe, sharp and persistent catarrh and you see their eyes tired, small, and the sight of them already weakened; and the eyelids ulcerated, with hairs falling out from them, and deep in the head there is a sharp, painful sensation followed by sneezing: then take from these symptoms that those substances [the discharges], and catarrhs do not come except from numerous veins.
And the best treatment for them is this: And it is that you prescribe to the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make in it one crack in the middle of the forehead or slightly across its width and start with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple, and let the incision be up to the bone; and stretch out your hand from the muscles of both temples which move when chewing until you feel the bone: and dry all blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the wound with a swab of old linen, or with strips of new linen. Then compress from above with swabs; and moisten the swabs with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you uncover it, and see that the hot abscess is now diminished; then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that makes flesh grow; for example, take two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the birthplace of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment for that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral substance, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, like chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid, such as water and vinegar, and things like them; or it is an animal.
When therefore a stone or stony substance falls into the ear from among those which are not added to and do not grow within the ear, expose the ear to the sun; if you then see that it is a stone, then instil into the ear some of either violative oil or sesame oil. Then apply the procedure for extracting it and make them sneeze with mustard seed; plug their nostrils as they sneeze, after you place around the ear a pad made of cloth or wool, and stretch out the ear upwards.
For often it comes out with this treatment.
If it does not come out, then perform its removal with a gesture, that is, with delicate pincers; whose form is this.
If it therefore comes out with the gesture, it is good.
And if not, then perform its removal with a delicate blind hook of little curvature.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a copper tube, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole deeply, and apply what is in the circumference of the tube with soft wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for the wind except through the tube; then extract it with your strong attracting breath.
For many times indeed it comes out with that which we have narrated. If however it does not come out this way: then take from helkalimbat, or from viscum, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and place it at the end of a stick after you wrap around it a piece of skin positioned eccentrically: then insert it into the ear hole easily, after you dry the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to cut it open, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the mode of the cut is such that a weak flea first bites on the head: and extract from it blood according to the measure of its strength. Then make the weak one sit between your hands, and turn its ear upwards, and find a small cut in the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed place on it: and let the cut be moon-shaped until you reach the bone: then remove it with what is possible for you from instruments.
Then sew up your incision with speed and tend it until it is healed.
If an object has fallen into the ear due to one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its removal.
But if it does not yield to your extraction, then take a thin, fine spatula like this:
And administer with it the removal of that type of grain which falls into the ear and do not attempt it when you think that the grain has already been softened by the vapour of the ear until you have tried many times; then remove them with hooks, or with a thin probe, or with suction, as we have said, for their extraction is facilitated.
In the case of water which enters the ear, the patient must use sneezing as treatment at first; and already he has filled his ear with some old oil; and he is lying on that ear in which there is water. Therefore if it comes out by itself, it is good.
And if it does not, then take many pebbles the length of a finger, thin and soft, and warm them with fire for a while, and let the patient introduce one of these into his ear-hole: and let him jump on one foot from that side, striking with another pebble against the pebble which is in the ear. So do not cease doing this with pebble and pebble until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out to such an extent that you may take a straw or a reed and introduce one end of it into the ear; and light the other end with fire until most of it is burnt. Repeat with another reed and do so many times until all the water comes out; then extract it along with the tube as mentioned before for the stone.
When extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is a small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, take care of it as I said in the division.
But if it is a large body that can be felt: then carry out its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all those things which are held in the ear.
When extracting a worm born in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division and in the treatise of distillations, and your treatment does not succeed; then you must look at the ear in the sun. If something of the worm appears there, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks.
But if nothing of it appears there, then take a tube whose form is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top; and introduce the fine end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate; then suck hard with it. Do so many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then apply some wax around the tube with a wick, as I said for the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then carry out the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms; and you will find it in the treatise of distillations.
And your infusion which you pour the oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. You can see that it should be made from silver or bronze, with the lower part narrow and having a small hole, the upper part wide.
Let it therefore be made of bronze, off-centre to the part that is inside the cavity of the tube. And if you wish, take a stick and wrap around its end a little cotton with an appropriate number of windings; then pour oil or juice or whatever remedy you like from among these medicines into the tube; let it be in the ear; next insert the stick with the cotton above it, and express it by compressing it with your hand using moderate pressure until oil is forced into the cavity of the ear and the patient feels it inside; and let what is poured into the ear already have been warmed up a little with fire: and beware that nothing which is distilled into it be very cold: for remedies do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
### Treatment of the Accident of Earwax ###
Sometimes children come out of their mothers' wombs unpierced.
And it also happens that earwax accumulates in some people due to injury or the growth of flesh there.
And this earwax sometimes forms deep inside the hole of the ear, beyond sight; and it occasionally happens near its outer edge where vision can reach it.
That which occurs deep inside the ear, beyond sight's reach, is difficult to cure in many cases.
And for that which vision can reach, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and look inside; then if you see any visible wax, open it with a fine probe of this shape, whose end has some width with one part sharp; and let the rest of the probe be two smooth sides: so as not to hurt the ear.
If the obstruction is born of flesh within the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook and cut it away easily until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a fine, soft rod and heat it slightly over a flame; then insert it into the ear canal.
If you perceive the obstruction along with what the patient describes as a heaviness of hearing, attempt to open it easily; and take care not to injure the ear's nerves.
Then place in the ear a linen plug according to the size of the opening you wrapped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure the wound is healed.
Or take linen and soak it in rosewater, then sprinkle over it some powdered frankincense, and use this.
If you see the ear starting to fester, then remove the linen and leave it with another which you wrap in a poultice made from oil and rosewater until the abscess cools; then treat it until it heals.
If there is bleeding, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it over the ear; and use the remaining treatment which stops blood flow as described previously in the section on distillations.
On treating sore eyelids.
Chapter 8.
These sore eyelids which occur on the eye, sometimes are wet and sometimes dry.
So you must hold them with forceps; or with a hook, and cut them away with a spatule from their roots. Then place ground galbanum on the wound.
If any of them ooze blood, then cauterize them with a fine lens-shaped cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after the operation. For they often recur when some of their roots remain. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not recur.
On the treatment of eyelid cysts.
Chapter 9.
It often happens that something occurs in the eyelids which resembles a cyst in its strength and hardness, and on account of this it is called a cyst; and it is a collection of thick moisture in the upper or lower eyelid.
The operation for them is as follows: you should then consider whether the cyst which appears from the eyelid and moves in all directions is proceeding in it; and its consistency is soft.
Therefore, make an incision above it across the width [of the eyelid]. Then scrape it out from all sides with ease until it comes out. Then snatch it away with a hook, and tear it off.
But if its tearing away is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with an incision to some extent, then it harms the patient in some way.
But if the incision is large, then gather it with a suture; and treat it until it is healed.
If it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it and fleshes it out.
And if the hailstone is sloped towards the inner surface of the ear, then turn over the eyelid and hook out the hailstone with a crochet-hook; besides which you must make an incision and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is stretched by means of an incision, that will not do any harm.
Then wash the eye after removal of the hailstone with salt water; and treat the place with something that fleshes it out until the patient is healed.
On the treatment of eyelid swelling.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is the fat that forms under the eyelids; it happens most often to children. It weighs down their eyes and causes them to have morning cataracts, so they cannot look at sunlight because tears run into their eyes. That's why you see them lying on their faces all the time and sleeping with their faces downwards: the moisture from their eyelids runs beneath their eyebrows, which already are swollen; Axirnath is a protuberance that appears in the eye, and when you press it with your fingers you feel Axirnath between your fingers.
The mode of treatment is that the patient should put his head on your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare some lint within it; make out of this lint a circle to the size that matches Axirnath from every side. Place him on it and press with your fingers from all sides so that the navel gathers at the center of the circle.
Then find at the center of that moisture with a spatula striking cracks across its width, and let no crack be larger than that which forms during deep phlebotomy.
Or you should split the skin entirely until you reach the navel because in many conditions the navel proceeds from a crack according to the shape of a white piece of fat.
Extract it therefore with a cloth that you have moistened between your fingers towards the outside and rotate your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed: take care not to add any into the crack lest you reach the eye with the spatula and injure it.
But if the navel does not appear to you at the beginning of the crack then you should add a little gently into the crack until the navel proceeds from it. Then extract it as we have said.
Submerge the cloths in vinegar and water then place them on the site and compress it with pads.
There are people who rub salt into it and place it in the hollow of the crack so that what remains of that moisture melts away.
Then treat it until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should occur then treat it with soothing poultices until it heals.
On the ways of eyelid drooping, that is when the eyelid softens so it creases improperly.
Chapter Eleven.
When there grow on the eyelids of the eye extra hairs beyond the normal course underneath the natural hair, and they become too long: then these harm the eye, and cause ailments to occur such as frequent tears, softening of the eyelids, and whiteness, until it becomes a cause for destruction of the eye.
Ataxmir's eye indeed is cured according to four methods. Or by fire-cauterization. Or with an acute medicine, as mentioned previously in the chapter on cauterization. Ataxmir is also cured by incision and suture. Or with tubes, as I will explain.
It is necessary to place the patient's head on your lap. Then turn the eye's eyelid with your left hand.
If it is turned then that is good. And if it is not turned: introduce the needle into it with a thread attached from its lower part of the eyelid and make the needle penetrate together with the thread from above, and have that be near the hair itself, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hair grows; then pull the thread upwards along with the eyelid, and turn it back using a rod.
Then find the inner part of the eyelid underneath the extra hair using an incision from the larger lacrimal to the smaller one.
Then extract the thread and place a small piece of cotton or cloth underneath the eyelid; then mark over the eyelid with cauterizing iron in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid; for this varies already among men.
For some of them require that a large portion be cut from the eyelid according to the size which is softened by the eyelid.
Then when you have placed the spatulate end over the two lines which you marked, begin from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct and let there be one incision near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a spoke.
Then introduce the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape away all of it. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, using a needle and fine woolen thread; wipe away any blood, and make adhere that which is superfluous of the threads over both eyebrows, with some sticky substances if you wish to do so; but if not, take no care.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days; afterward tend to it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and tend to it with something that dries and contracts. For the eyelid is raised at the wound's sealing and its bringing together; yet it will be better still.
This method of treatment ancient physicians taught to Ataxmir.
However there is concern for the patient; and it is of good skill; nor is fear within him.
And there is another method to Ataxmir also; it is that you mark upon the eyelid a shape which is like the form of a myrtle leaf, as we have described.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or combined hooks, following this form, then cut away the excess with small scissors, following this shape, with equal cutting.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not even out: then take a needle in which there is thread, and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger lachrymal, and a second thread near the smaller lachrymal: and gather together between your fingers the threads with equal force.
Then raise your hand with an equal elevation; and lift the skin over which the tumor is marked, as we have described.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture; and take care of it until it heals.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or your opening, subdue that abscess with a poultice and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid as well, that its hairs turn inward.
It is therefore necessary that you use on it what we have said about incision; but suture is more of an art.  On the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds.
And it is necessary that you turn the eyelid back, and split open the opening which is inside, as I have described. Then make two reeds or two slender sticks with art, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than a spatula; and you have already cut at each end from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then gather each [part] with precision over that which protrudes from the eyelid of the eye, and tighten the two tubes from both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for several days. For the skin will die, darken, and rot until it falls off by itself.
Therefore if you delay, then cut it with scissors. Then treat it until it heals.
When therefore it is strengthened, lift the eyelid: and do not pluck out the hairs.
And this is the form of the two tubes, which you will make according to this figure, and size in length and width.
Chapter 12.
On removing hairs that irritate the eye with a needle.
The operation in this case is that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a thin, soft silk thread. Then gather its ends, and tie each one with a very fine knot; and let the length of the threads be one palm's width.
Then tie underneath it another fine thread that is short, and knot its ends if you like.
Then have the patient place their head in your lap, and let it be close to the sun so that the operation may be revealed; for this is a precise or delicate operation.
Then insert the needle with its thread into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract from above a natural hair.
Then draw out the needle above the splint; and introduce into the splint that needle; be it one or two or three, no more than this.
Then draw out your hand with it until the needle emerges from the eyelid together with the natural eyelash.
But if the splint is drawn and the needle does not emerge with it; draw out the splint downwards, with the thread you have attached to it, until the splint comes out of the upper opening, and see it with your eye: then draw out the splint and the thread, close up the eye and leave it shut for two or three days, until the eyelash adheres in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the eyelash is short then either attach to it a long artificial hair and raise that with it; or leave it alone until it grows longer, and only then lift it up.
Chapter 13.
When this abscess happens to the eye, it is called leporina.
And this abscess is either natural or accidental.
An accidental one forms from the congealing of a wound or incision, or a burn, or something similar to those.
The method of treating it is to open up the congealed material and separate its lips; you should then put between them a little flaxen wick and bind it until it heals.
And you must not use in its treatment things that dry and constrict; for if you do so the abscess will return worse than it was before: rather use things which soften it, such as fenugreek; and an ointment mixed with water in which marshmallow has been cooked, flax-seed and diaquilaon which has been liquefied with some oil: bind this on with a wick and treat with it.
The treasure of your treatment lies in ensuring that you consider every type of mind so that it does not solidify into the same form as before.
This operation we described is simply a correction of the Alxatrata so that the form of the place does not return to what it was entirely.
On treating Alxatrata which occurs on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter 14.
This condition which occurs on the lower eyelid is truly called 'Alxatrata'. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
It occurs accidentally from a wound or tear, by burning with a hot iron, or similar things.
The way to operate on it is to take a needle with double thread and dip it into the flesh; then make it pierce from left to right of the tear duct until the thread comes out at both ends of the flesh.
Then raise the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it through lengthwise with a wide lancet. If then the form of the eyelid returns as it should be, that is good.
If not, take a splint and place it in the tear; then turn the eyelid with it. Make two incisions on the inside of the eyelid between its two corners so that they meet to form an angle like this: [draws ]. When joined together, their shape is the Greek letter lambda ().
Then remove that flesh in an amount equal to the acute inferior edge of his eye from what follows the eyelid. Then join together the separated parts, suturing them with woolen thread.
Then treat them with what we have said about softening medications and linen dressings until they are healed.
And if an abscess occurs from a split or suture, or from cauterization, then you must open the split with a plain scalpel under the eyelashes, likewise what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with linen as we have said.
And the main point about treating an abscess when it is above or below is to carry out that operation as prepared according to the form of the abscess.
For it has many varieties of form: and a prudent practitioner prepares his skill according to whichever mode is possible for him until he restores the figure to its natural shape or close to it, and himself being infirm as befits him from the operation and the instruments in most arrangements.
Chapter 15.
On adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or the cornea.
Sometimes this adhesion occurs to many people such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural movements.
You should therefore introduce the end of a needle under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut through the adhesion with a blunt spatula that is not sharp like spatulas usually are but rather somewhat dull: so as not to disturb the patient during the operation, and for the spatula not to be removed and injure the eye with its sharpness; and let your incision be like you would scrape off that adhesion gently until when the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the whole adhesion is healed.
Pour some saltwater into the eye and wash it out with water; or drip onto it a mixture of saffron dissolved in water distilled from sieved henbane.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye using a strip of linen; place over the eye some wool soaked in the white of an egg; after three days use henbane and poultices until it is healed.
On the excision of ingrown nails and flesh mounds on the eyelids...
Chapter 16.
Know that an ingrown nail is of two types. It may be horn-like; like hard, thin potsherd. Or it may be non-horny, like white frozen moisture; when this touches iron or you try to pick it up with a hook, it breaks and is not pierced by the hook.
And neither type begins except from the larger eyelid, gradually growing until it covers sight and hinders movement of the eye.
The method for excising it is as follows: have the patient put their head in your lap. Then open his eyes and lift up the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the nail using a double-pronged hook, extending it upwards. Next, take a needle and insert into it some horsehair, cattle hair or goat hair; let the thread be strong, double over the needle's end somewhat, then fasten it to the middle of the nail; pierce through with the needle, tie up the nail with the thread, extending it upwards: scrape away the side of the nail that follows after the pupil, as if you were plucking it with hair all the way back.
Then make an incision into the remaining part at the root of the lacrimal sac with a thin spatula or with small scissors; and leave the natural lacrimal flesh untouched so that you do not cause, as a result of its incision, a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the claw is extended with only one hook; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it with a gentle spatula that looks like this [shape].
And be careful not to let the spatula touch the corneal layer so that you do not cause a rupture in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some ground salt or something made from zinjar and leave it there until the next day. Then take care of it with what is required until it heals.
If the claw is not nervous and you cannot insert a needle into it or hook it, then this type of claw is often treated with medicines and heals as I have described above.
If you want to incise it, then open the sick eye and take a thin spatula that is gentle and blunt, with this shape [shape]. And scrape away the claw above with a razor very carefully. If you see that it becomes thinner and breaks under the razor, then drop into the eye from your vessel something made from zinjar or red medicine or ground salt; and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat this procedure on it until all of it is removed. If there is no pus in your eye and it is warm, then leave it alone and treat the inflammation until it heals.
Then repeat the procedure on it with the same method until it is healed.
However, if the bulging tear duct flesh is causing significant harm to the eye, it should be suspended with a hook and cut from it its portion; do not go beyond this incision lest there be damage to the tear ducts.
Then put drops into the tear duct of red saffron, or made from cornflower, until it is healed.
On the incision for Al-Guaradi's disease and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter 17.
Sometimes a red flesh grows or forms in certain human eyes until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or contracts above the eyelids; and is like a wild pomegranate.
And there is also an operation for it: the patient must put their head on your lap. Then open his eyes and gather all that flesh with appropriate hooks; hold it with a pair of forceps or pincers.
Then cut the flesh first until its entirety is finished with a scalpel. Beware of the eye during this operation so that you do not harm it; let your cut be either with a knife or a chisel, which we mentioned in the nail incision: or with small scissors like those used to trim hoofs according to what will come after this chapter.
When your incision is complete and all of the cataract has been removed, fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder, and similar corrosive medicines; then place on top of the eye a fresh sheep's bladder (inside out), so that it may be sealed from the hot pus.
If there is any cataract remaining and you have hot blood and fear a hot pus, then leave the eye alone, and treat it with something that will ease the swelling.
Next, operate on it again until it is healed.
Do likewise with additional flesh that may happen to occur in the eye from this kind of injury.
However, when it comes to treating any eye disease that occurs naturally or accidentally and requires an iron instrument for surgery: do not put your hand into any of these operations until you have seen whether the member can endure such a procedure.
On extracting Sebel from the eye.
Chapter 18.
Sebel are red veins that grow on the eye, impeding its sight and weaken it over time.
You must first determine whether the eye with Sebel is strong and otherwise healthy apart from these veins; then collect its Sebel.
And it is right to instruct the patient to lay their head on your lap.
Then lift the veins with one hook or two according to your judgment; let the hooks be fine and doubled over like this form, or two joined on a single body as here.
Then carefully collect with fine scissors those veins, and wipe away the blood every hour until you see those veins move out of sight and dissolve; be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the forceps.
And let your procedure be done at midday in the sun, and be very firm in your operation; so that you cut only those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye either green or red ointment so that its sharpness may dissolve what remains of the sebaceous matter.
But if you cannot collect all of it in that hour, then apply to the eye a plaster with something in it to relieve the pain; and leave it there for several days until its pain is settled; and let it be bound by a warm poultice; afterwards repeat on it the same procedure until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors.
On reducing arietta to the nose.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call Arietta a fistula which you treat with the cautery or with caustic medicine according as its history may have preceded.
And if it is not healed then there is no skill in it unless you make an incision over the abscess at its origin and extract all of the moisture that is in it along with the pus until bone is exposed.
When therefore bone is exposed and you see on it signs of decay or blackness then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this:
And it is called asperation of the bone.
The tool should be made of hardened iron, and its head should be round like a whetstone, marked with fine grooves either by a file or an engraver's tool; place it on the damaged bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and press your hand for a moment until you feel that the damage has been smoothed away; do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying, astringent medicines.
If the area becomes firm and flesh grows there, and the course of the infection is cut off, and it remains so for forty days without any renewal or swelling, nor does anything untoward happen to it; then you may know that it has now healed.
And if not, then there is no other treatment except to bring the fistula back out through the nostril, following this pattern.
And it is done by exposing the second bone with a sharp iron or medicine; and when you have exposed that bone, take an auger like this one, its tip being of iron, triangular in shape; and its wood should be like a pyramid, tapering to the end as shown here.
Then place it over that bone, close to the tear duct; and extend your hand a little away from the eye. Then turn your hand with the auger until it penetrates the bone and feels weak; through this you will know that air is escaping from it when they hold their hand over their nose.
So then restore the area with astringent drying medicines as I have taught you.
When flesh grows and the area hardens, then any matter that used to escape outwards now returns to the nose; so it is lighter over the weak spot.
The treatment for a protruding eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye bulges with all its parts and there is no damage to vision or diminution of sight, then one must cure the weak person by first bleeding them; afterwards place cups on their neck without scarring but using suction.
Then apply to the eye a poultice made of acacia, aloe, frankincense, and storax. After that compress the eye from above with many soft feathers.
And the method of applying the feathers is as follows: take many soft cloths and fold each one four times; make a stack of these then place them on top of each other according to how much the eye requires; finally, secure them firmly with a hard cloth bandage.
And repeat the cups with suction without scarring.
After that remove the cups and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
But if the bandage softens again then it must be tightened repeatedly; afterwards remove the bandage after a day and night as I've said.
When you see the eye has returned to its proper skin it's good; if not then repeat the poultice and feathers and bandage and cups until cured.
The treatment for a cut of the skin's membrane.
Chapter 21.
When there is a rupture of the membrane of the skin and it protrudes outside the eyelids like a grain of wheat, disfiguring the appearance of the person and requiring an incision to be made then surgery on that is as follows, which I will describe: you should insert a needle at the base of the membrane from below towards above.
Then insert another needle into which is placed double thread from the tear duct side, and make it penetrate; and remove the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of double thread and tie it to a portion of the eyelid around the needle; then tighten it greatly.
Then extract the needle and place over the eye wool soaked in white of egg; then leave it until the thread falls out with the eyelid.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it until the wound heals.
And sometimes the eyelid is tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth until the eye becomes greatly prominent.
Then the eye should be pierced with a fine spatula that reaches down to the depth of the eye; let moisture run into the white of the eye and the eye shall return in immediately; then tighten it until the wound heals.
On the cure of alcomania.
Chapter 22.
This illness which is called alcomania is nothing but pus that accumulates in the eye, like water running down, and it itself is not water.
And the manner of operating on it is to make the patient sit on a prepared seat or upright.
Then take his head in both your hands and move it until you see the pus reach below his eyes. Then it is tied up so that it does not fall and the eye is cleared; and the patient sees things as he used to see them: and if it has not fallen below, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and scrape with it over the hard crust for a little while near where the connective tissue joins the hard crust until pus appears.
When it appears, then drop into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of water that descends into the eye or cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have already spoken about the types of water in the section, and what is suitable for boring; now take that same according to health.
Then you must make the patient sit square between your hands opposite a light near the sun; and tie his healthy eye shut, pulling it tight; then with your right hand hold down the eyelid of his eye if it is the left eye that has water in it; or with your left hand if it is the right eye.
Then take in your right hand an almond if it is the left eye; or in your left hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the end of the almond near the edge where the white of the eye appears around the size of a spoke; then push with force on the almond; and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates the white of the eye: and feel that the almond has now reached an empty space.
And it is necessary for the length of the needle to reach the depth [of the wound], and its width should be from the pupil to the end of the black part, which is the iris. You will see them in the pupil itself due to the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the needle up to the spot where the water is. Then press it downwards with force, one after another.
If the water then descends immediately, the patient will see what is opened before his sight and the needle in his eye.
Then rest for a short while. If the water descends again, then remove it for a second time, except that the needle is withdrawn.
When it stops moving, but doesn't rise, then extract the needle with ease, and you turn your hand little by little.
Then dissolve some clear rock salt in the water, and wash the eye thoroughly with it. Then place over the eye a whole piece of linen cloth or woolen material steeped in rose oil and white of egg; bind the sound eye with it.
We, however, and those of our time, place over it powdered cumin with white of egg.
If the needle doesn't yield to you in entering the eye because of its hardness; for it is made of iron, whose eye [i.e., sight] is very hard; then you must take a spatula called an *alberid*, which has this form.
Pierce therefore only its joint, and do not push your hand into the hole; it is enough to make a path towards the almond-shaped spot.
Then introduce the almond-shaped instrument as we have described.
And when your work is complete, prepare for the patient a decently made bedroom in which they can sleep on their back in a dark room; prohibit any movement or coughing; provide them with food that soothes their nature; and ensure they do not move their head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in place according to its arrangement for three days.
Then loosen it in the dark room and test his vision; make him see things. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
It should not be done at the time of treatment or immediately after the incision; instead, it must be avoided because water rises quickly through a strong current.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should loosen the eye before the seventh day; and treat it with something that will calm the abscess until it is calmed; then restore his vision and place an eye mask over his face to regulate his sight for several days while he remains in the dark room.
Then they should leave the house gradually and return to their homes.
And you should know that a student is not exempt from vision training at the zaltea; for then he will dare to perform operations.
And now a certain person from Alayrach has reached us who said that there was a woman in Alayrach with a perforated stomach from which water is drawn.
But therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of appearances from a woman, as she stands upon it who is ignorant of it.
Let them be made from pure brass; and their ends should have the same triangular delicacy, with an acute point.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of nasal polyps.
Sometimes there grow in the nose various polyps, one of which resembles a many-legged scorpion; and from this arises a polyp that is hard like a stone, dark coloured, cancerous; and from it also arises one that is soft, not dark coloured.
For the soft polyp, which is neither deceitful nor cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands facing the sun, and open his nostrils; then throw a hook into those polyps; next draw them out, and cut what you have seized of them with a thin sharp scalpel on one side until you know that all the flesh is now removed.
But if anything of it should remain which cannot be removed; then shave it with one of the delicate instruments with ease until nothing remains of it. If blood dominates you or a hot abscess develops, then oppose it with what is necessary: if it is one of the malignant abscesses, proceed to cauterize it until the blood stops flowing and all flesh is removed.
Then after the incision, vinegar should be thrown into the nostril followed by water or wine. If the nostril opens up and moisture begins to come out of it all the way to the palate, then you will know that they are already healthy again.
If the moisture does not penetrate as it should then you will know that flesh has grown within its depths around the upper nasal bones which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
You should then introduce a piece of linen thread of a certain thickness with many knots along it and leave between each knot the length of a finger or less: and have the sick person put one end of the thread in their nose as far as possible after you make it similar to an arrow: and they pull on its smell until it comes out at their throat.
And many children do something similar to this as a trick in school: and it is an easy thing for whoever wants to do it.
Then take the two ends of the thread: one that comes out over the nose and another which remains within it. Then take the flesh clamp and fix its teeth to the knots on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now attached to the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread and put into the nose after washing away the blood lint that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days or more until all of the remaining flesh is eaten away by the ointment.
Then place a lead pipe in the end of the thing into the nose for several days until it heals.
If however it requires treatment that dries it up, use this.
For making the nasal douche with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it out of silver or brass; let it be similar to a small lamp, open like a seashell; and its nozzle likewise.
And if you wish make the pipe closed, like a cane.
And the small seashell of the instrument for making a nasal douche should be full and open: it should have a handle at its end with which you hold it when heating oil or whatever liquid in it that you want.
Chapter 25.
Many times there appears at the end of the nose a polyp that grows larger with time until it disfigures its appearance.
And because of that it is necessary to attack it at the beginning of its appearance and eradicate all of it. Then put over the place either a caustic substance or a burning medicine that stays in its place.
If therefore you delay its excision until it grows larger, then consider whether it is of stony hardness and dark-coloured with little sensation; then do not touch it with the iron, for it is a cancerous ulcer.
Many times I have seen someone who cuts out such an ulcer and a great harm befalls the one who had it.
If the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient's fluids, and make the incision without fear or hesitation: treat the area with a drying and constricting agent until it heals.
Chapter 26.
On sewing together the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when there is a break of continuity in one of these cartilages, surgery will not be of much help, except in some people.
Therefore, when something like this happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture; afterwards treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of a break in continuity are already separated and each side has healed over, then you must scrape away all the tissue down to the bleeding flesh. Afterwards bring the two lips together with a suture and tighten them; sprinkle over it powdered dragon's blood and ground frankincense; put on top of these powders a piece of fine wax plaster, and another of the harder consolidating plasters; leave this bound up for two or three days. Then remove the bandage and change the dressing; leave it until the threads come away by themselves. Afterwards treat it with an ointment until it heals.
The method of sewing is that you bring together the break in continuity either with needles as we described for the abdomen; or bring it together using thread as I have made you aware.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of lip nodules.
Hard nodules resembling grains of grass often occur for many people on the inner parts of their lips; some are smaller while others are larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lip and cut above all of its nodules; suspend it with a hook and then slice through it on all sides.
Next fill the place after incision with ground gallic nettle until it stops bleeding.
Afterwards it should be washed with vinegar; the wound site is to be treated with an astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter 28.
On the excision of excess flesh over the gums.
Many times excess flesh grows over the gums; this is what the ancients call abuluss.
It is necessary to hook it or grab with forceps and cut at its base; allow blood (or pus) to run from the wound.
Afterwards apply ground gallic nettle or other drying astringent powders to the wound.
If this flesh returns following treatment, as it often does, then cut away a second time and cauterize; for it will not return after being burnt.
Chapter 29.
On tooth extraction with an iron tool.
Sometimes rough, ugly layers of enamel accumulate on the inner and outer surfaces of teeth and between the gums; sometimes they darken and become yellow and green until corruption reaches the gum from it, making teeth foul-looking.
Therefore, you should make the patient sit between your hands and rest his head on your lap; then scrape away the enamel as well as any part of it that looks like grit in a millstone; do this until no trace is left, and treat blackness, yellowness and greenness similarly until they are cleaned off.
If you remove the cause of these conditions early on, that's good; if not, repeat your treatment every day for two or three days until finally you reach where it is you want to get at.
Also be aware that you will need scalpels of different shapes and sizes for preparing the tooth before your treatment; since the scalpel used to scrape off its lower surface is not the same as the one used to clean between teeth, given their differing forms.
And have these various types of scalpel ready for use as you see fit.
Chapter 30.
On toothache extraction.
You must relieve the tooth of its pain with all your skill, but be slow to extract it. For once it is extracted, there can be no replacement; so significant a thing is lost; unless there's simply no other option but to extract it, then you should strengthen and reassure the patient about the offending tooth when forced to pull it out.
Frequently, pain deceives the sick person, and they think that the ache is in a healthy tooth and pull it out; then the pain does not subside until the sick tooth is pulled.
For we have already seen this from the practice of blood-letters.
So when the sick tooth is certified by you, then it is necessary that you sacrifice at the vicinity of the tooth with a spatula having some virtue, until the gum is loosened from every part; then move the tooth with your fingers or with thin forceps especially little by little, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in a good position there, and put the head of the sick person between your knees; which you have held well so that it does not move; then draw the tooth according to its straight line so as not to break it.
If it does not come out then take one of those instruments and insert it under the tooth from every part gently, and try its movement as you did before.
But if the tooth is perforated or corroded then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the end of a thin stick so that it does not break at the time of your tightening over it with forceps.
And you must take great care with the scarification around the gum from every part.
And guard your strength so as not to break it and leave a part of the tooth which will return to the sick person with an evil pain greater than at first.
And beware of doing what ignorant blood-letters do in their haste and boldness over pulling it out, besides administering what we have narrated.
They often inflict upon people serious injuries that are difficult to treat; either because some or all of the roots remain after it is broken, or when they remove it along with certain jaw bones - we have witnessed this many times.
It should then be boiled after its removal with wine or vinegar and salt.
If there is any bleeding from the wound (which often happens), grind some zegi and fill the wound with it; if this does not work, cauterise it instead - unless you do not have any zegi available.
The handles of the dental forceps should be long and short-handled, with thick heads so that they do not bend when pressed against a tooth; you can make these thicker at the handle to prevent them from crossing over each other as you close them: make the ends of the handles broad as well, such that even if you clamp down on them very hard, they will not cross over or damage one another. The ends should be made out of good iron and not cheap steel; the heads should have teeth which lock together tightly without slipping when clamped down with force.
And sometimes the heads of the handles are made in the form of a bird's beak, like that of a stork, with a strong, firm grip.
Chapter 31.
On the removal of teeth, and extraction of fractured jaw bones.
If any fragments remain after a broken tooth is removed, pack the site with butter for one or two days to soften it; then insert either an alum plug or forceps whose ends are shaped like a stork's beak into this softened area and extract. This is the form of the forceps.
Their ends should be made like a rasp inside; or like the claw of a cat.
If therefore it does not obey you at the exit with these forceps, then it is necessary for you to hollow out over the root, and remove all of the flesh, and insert the instrument which is named small Palanca, whose form is this.
Let the end be short, somewhat thick; and let it not be sharp so that it does not break.
If therefore the root exits through it, that is good; and if not, you should help with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular at its end, on which there is some thickness.
The form of the second is triangular at its end, but thin.
And with other instruments and tools that have been mentioned previously in relation to teeth.
There is also assistance with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, twisted triangular end, on which there is some thickness so that it does not break; and let it be blunt so that it does not sharpen.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth; and similarly the other instruments are not counted, and a wise craftsman, skilled in his art invents by himself an instrument according to what is indicated by the operation itself and the illnesses themselves.
Because for diseases to which the ancients did not say any instruments, due to the diversity of their species.
If a bone of the jaw or any other bones of the mouth breaks or becomes infected, then examine it carefully where it is located and use whatever tool suits its extraction from among the instruments and forceps that I mentioned in the section on tooth extraction; this one has the following form.
Place a thick material in it gradually so that the bone is clamped or held fast and does not fall out or slide until the bone comes out; then restore the site with suitable medicines for such an injury.
If there is some decay on the bone then remove it along with its decay; treat it until it heals.
Chapter 32.
On the removal of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth grow out of their normal path and direction, it becomes a deformity; especially when this occurs in the case of women and girls with fine features.
Therefore, one must take into account the following... If a tooth is born after another and does not have its own place, or if it cannot be extracted or filed; then remove it instead by cutting into it with this instrument...
It is like a small beak; its end is made of hard iron, very sharp.
And let your incision on it be over many days; this is due to the hardness of the tooth and so that others may not be shaken out by it.
If the prominent tooth is exposed to filing then file it with this iron file; its form is as follows...
The tooth shall be removed entirely with a tool of gold; its handle should have fine points like the file used to make needles - which is how it will gradually reduce the tooth over many days easily so that the tooth isn't shaken and falls out, then ease it gently and finally scrape it with small scalpels.
If it is a tooth from which part has broken and damages the tongue, then you must also file it until its roughness from that break is removed and becomes even; smoothen it, so that it will not hurt the tongue or corrupt speech.
Chapter 33.
On removing teeth with gold wire.
When the front teeth suffer from concussion and shaking due to a blow or fall; and when the patient is unable to chew on them for fear of losing their food; and if you have treated them with astringent medicines without success, then it's time to bind these teeth together using gold wire. Silver will turn green and rot within days, but gold never changes.
The wire should be of a thickness and thinness according to the size that fits between teeth.
The technique is to take the wire and insert half of it between two healthy teeth; then weave its two ends among the loose tooth or teeth until you reach with your thread up to a sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weave until you reach back to where you started; and tighten your grip with skill and wisdom so that it's not at all removable: your tightening of the wire should be at the roots of the teeth so as not to come out.
Then cut off the excessive gum with scissors: gather both together and turn them over with forceps; hide them between a healthy tooth and one that has been moved so as not to harm your tongue, then leave the thread tight while you do this.
If it loosens or is cut, tighten them with another thread and this will keep them in place during the whole process.
And this is the arrangement of teeth, showing the positioning of two healthy teeth and two moved teeth as you can see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return after falling out and are retained in their places as we have described; they remain like this unless the skilled and experienced dentist removes them.
And sometimes some bone splinters are fitted and made into the shape of a tooth; it is placed where the missing tooth was located and secured as we have said before; it remains like this for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament under the tongue is either natural, with which one is born, or accidental from an injury that has already healed.
The procedure for this is to open the mouth of the patient and place it in your lap; then lift their tongue; next cut through that sinewy ligament until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is any hardness or knotting within it and this comes from the healing of a wound; then thrust a hook into it and move it sideways until you cut through the ligament and free up that knot.
And take care that there is no fissure deep within the flesh, and you do not cut into an artery there, causing a flow of blood.
Then it should be washed around the track of the incision with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue lint from linen that the patient should hold through every night so it does not heal a second time.
If, however, there is a flow of blood, then place on the spot ground ginger. If blood prevails over you, then cauterise the spot with a lunar cautery suitable for this; afterwards treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes there happens under the tongue a boil resembling a small frog, hindering the tongue from its natural movement; and perhaps it grows so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation for this is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun, then examine the boil.
So if you see it is dark or black in colour, hard and without sensation to the patient's touch then do not touch it for it is a cancer.
And if it slopes towards whiteness with some moisture then thrust into it a curved hook and cut it out with a sharp spoon-shaped instrument and remove it whole. If blood prevails over you during your work then place on it ground ginger until the bleeding is checked; then return to your work until you remove it and all of its covering.
Then it should be rinsed with vinegar and salt: and treat it with any other suitable treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter 36.
Sometimes there occur between the throat and jaw, that is, glands which resemble cotton, which happen outside; and they are called two tonsils: when you treat them with what we have said in the section on division, and they do not heal, then consider this.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of a dark colour, with little feeling: then do not touch it with the iron.
And if it is of a red colour, and its root is thick: then do not touch it either with the iron, fearing an outflow of blood, rather leave it until it ripens. And then you will either pierce it through; or it should burst by itself.
And if it is of a white colour, round in shape, and its root is thin: then you must cut into it.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before its treatment, if the abscess has become quiet while warm and complete, or diminished to some extent: then make the patient sit facing the sun, and let their head be in your lap; and open their mouth, and have someone take this instrument between their hands and press the patient's tongue down with it, which is of this shape.
It shall be made of silver or brass, which should be thin like a knife.
When therefore you press with it on the tongue, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it into the tonsil, and draw it out as far as you can towards the exterior, except that you do not pull anything of the tunic with it.
Then cut it with an instrument that has this shape which follows. It resembles scissors; however, its ends are curved so that the opening of each is at a right angle to the other and is sharp; hence ensure it is made from iron or hardened bronze.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatuli whose form is thus. It should be sharp on one side but not sharp on the other.
And when you have cut one tonsil, cut the other in exactly the same way.
Then after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
But if there is bleeding have the patient gargle with water in which boiled gallnut bark; or myrtle leaves and similar astringents until the blood flow ceases; then treat it until it heals.
And sometimes there appear other abscesses in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them then as I have said for cutting of the two tonsils equally.
For my part I have treated a woman for an abscess that had formed in her throat which was causing difficulty breathing and loss of sensation to the point that her throat was almost closed; and she could only breathe through a narrow opening, and was already unable to eat or drink water: and by now would have died if left another day or two.
The abscess had risen so high that it had sent out two branches until they came out at the top of her nostrils.
I therefore hurriedly made a stitch with one of those hooks. Then I pulled it out, and a large portion came away from it.
I then cut that off where I found the hole of the nose.
Then I did to it what came out from the other nostril.
I then opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess, then cut off part of it, and no more came from it except for some blood; so the woman's throat was healed, she immediately drank water and then took food.
Therefore I did not cease cutting out from the abscess many times over a long period; and an abscess would form in place of that which I had cut away, until it was extended too far and along with her.
I therefore devised something, and I cauterized the abscess between the throat.
I therefore refrained from adding anything further; then I went away from her side, and did not know what God would do with her after that.
On cutting out an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When a catarrh descends to the uvula and becomes abscessed and elongated, then it is called "perpendicular"; and if it forms on the lower part of the uvula as a thick, round mass, then it is called a "tuba".
When you treat it with what we have said above and the treatment does not take effect; and you see that there is an abscess which has gone away from her heat but remains soft; then you ought to cut this out.
And if it has formed a round mass and there is no length to it, and its colour is red or dark brown or black, and it lacks feeling; then you should make an incision in it. For there is fear over the sick person.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner that I have described regarding its colour and length, you should make the sick person sit facing the sun; then press their tongue with an instrument as previously mentioned; next, fix a hook into one ear and pull it downwards; finally, cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for incising two tonsils.
And you should not cut anything from it unless what is added beyond the natural end without being included.
For if you cut more from it, you will damage their voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we described for incising two tonsils; and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the sick person is afraid of the incision, then you should use skill in cauterizing it without fear or anxiety.
The method of cauterizing it does not involve anything but cold water; you should place the sick person's head in your lap. Then press their tongue with the instrument that we mentioned earlier. Next, take some of the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterizing, and make a paste with it using unquenched lime; ensure it is neither thick nor thin, and fill the hollow of this instrument with it, which has this shape:" (at this point the original text includes an illustration).
The end of it should have a concave shape, like the concavity of the handle of a spoon.
Place the instrument with the medicine on top of that wound; and let the patient lie on their side so saliva flows out from their mouth away from the medicine inside it so nothing runs down into their throat to hurt them.
Then hold your hand with medicine in it. And keep it there for half an hour until you see the wound turning black and the medicine calming down from its initial agitation.
And if you wish to use a plug then wrap it with lint around the end of the radius and dip this lint-plug into medicine before inserting the radius (with lint-plug attached) into its tube until the lint plug adheres well onto that wound.
Do this many times over until you achieve the desired degree of cauterization for that wound. Then let it be; for within three to four days it will dry up and fall off by itself.
If you need to apply more medicine then do so; and after the cauterization has been done clean around that wound with a lint-plug soaked in butter and wipe off any remaining medicine from its surroundings, then wash it all down with cold water.
It should be treated from outside by means of poultices as well as internally with mouthwashes until it has completely healed up.
This kind of treatment is healthier than cutting (i.e., the lancet) as well as less frightening to be around.
And sometimes a wound is even healed by means of something finer than either cautery or incision; it is healed with the aid of fumigations in this way. Take calamentum, hyssop, oregano, rue, seah, chamomile, abrotanum, and similar herbs; gather all of them or some of them in a pot, submerge them with vinegar, and boil; the pot must be glazed with decent clay; there should be a hole in the middle of the pot on top of which is placed an instrument shaped like this.
It shall be made of silver or bronze; the end with the pomegranate design is to be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the small bowl above the tube until the bowl becomes very cloudy.
Then repeat over it many times until it dries.
And beware not to make this cure at the beginning of an abscess's appearance. For it often adds to the abscess; one should not do this unless at the decline of an abscess's warmth.
If this tool is not available to you, then take a tube and place goat skin at its end so the patient's mouth will not get burnt as the goat skin prevents the vapor from burning; this is an excellent cure with success.
On extracting splinters and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Often there gets stuck in the throat bone, or fish spine, or another object.
One should therefore extract what is visible of it above that which falls on sight after you press the tongue with an instrument towards the sun and identify to yourself what is in the throat.
And if it is not apparent to you and is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps it will come out through vomiting.
Or the patient should swallow a piece of turnip, or a lettuce root, or they should swallow a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of dry, soft sponge seaweed and tie it up with thread before swallowing. Once it reaches the place where there is a thorn or bone, and it comes out, then it will be removed.
If therefore, it does not come out with what we have said, then use a lead instrument in this form.
It should be thicker than a spoke by a little bit and at its end it should have a curve that can easily be inserted into the throat when their head is raised upwards.
And be careful not to touch their epiglottis so that it does not cause them to cough and push the bone or thorn with it, or let the doctor insert it by hand.
However, it is better for the patient to insert it themselves because of their knowledge about where the object is located; and they should push downwards or pull upwards with their hand while holding the instrument until it comes out.
On extracting leeches.
Chapter 39.
When a leech is treated with what I have mentioned in the section on treatment using medicine and it does not improve then look at the patient's throat in front of the sun or towards the sun after you press their tongue down with the instrument that I described to you.
If your sight falls upon a leech, remove it with a small hook or with thin tweezers deftly.
If it is not possible with these [tools]: then take a hollow tube, and insert it into the throat of the patient close to the esophagus; next, insert into the cavity of the tube a red-hot iron with fire. Do this several times, and let the patient refrain from drinking water all day.
