Then he should take a cup filled with cold water and open his mouth into it; let him gargle his mouth with it but not swallow any of it; then move the water around in his mouth once an hour for another hour with his hand. For the leech will immediately drop off when it senses water.
But if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the patient with bedbugs or leeches with the instrument I mentioned for fumigating a cold.
Do this several times, since it will fall off.
And the method of operating with fumigation is such that you take a pot containing live plums over fire; and let this pot be covered by a lid where there is a hole in its middle, and let an end of the instrument be fixed into it.
Then direct the fumigation and let the patient put his mouth on an end of the tube and close his mouth so that no smoke escapes until you know that the smoke has reached the leech. For the leech will immediately drop off.
If it does not fall out, then repeat fumigation many times.
And let the patient bear thirst, eat salty things, garlic, and onions, but not drink water.
It is necessary that he depart with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is removed from the throat, when it falls upon it with its eye.
It is like a pair of forceps, as you see; except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. And its end is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file; when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
### Concerning the opening of abscesses, their incision, and care in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many types of abscesses, of many forms, according to the manner in which they come about; this will be discussed further when we treat of one kind herein; and there are differences as regards the manner in which they should be lanced or opened with two methods.
One of these is of the kind of abscess within itself which contains moisture.
The second method concerns the places on the body where they occur.
Since an abscess occurring on the head is different from one that occurs in the anal region; and since an abscess in soft tissue is different from one which arises at a joint, each of these requires its own judgment as to treatment.
And from inflammations are those which should not be punctured unless after the maturation of pus in them and its completion; and among them are those that must be punctured while they are raw and do not mature according to their completion.
Such as inflammations that are near joints.
Because an inflammation when it occurs near a joint, and its duration is prolonged until what is inside it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; this is the cause for the closer proximity of the contraction of that limb.
Or it is an inflammation in a principal limb; because if you delay its puncturing until it matures, it will harm either that limb or the principal member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore puncture it while it is immature; and you should not puncture a raw inflammation that has not completed digestion unless it is near the anus so as to prevent the putrefaction of its depth and prolonging towards the interior of the anus, and for there not to be a fistula or non-healing in its termination.
You must know the time of puncturing an inflammation that has matured according to its completion; and this is at the relief of pain, reduction of fever, and decrease in redness, throbbing, and sharpness of the head of the inflammation; as well as other signs.
And you must make the same puncture at the lowest point of the inflammation and its most prominent peak; and let the puncturing go along the length of the body if inflammations are in parts of hands or feet, and places of forearms, cords, nerves, arteries, and finally all equal locations that do not have a reflex.
Go with a suture along that member according to its perforation.
When however the abscess is in fleshy parts: then it is better that you leave its opening alone until its discharge has been completed as we have said according to what follows.
For if you open it before this, the course of the pus from it is prolonged and there is much dirt and nastiness, and perhaps its lips will harden and its depth increase.
And some abscesses are opened according to the width of its body for what is needed or according to that part which requires it.
And it is necessary that you make a small opening for small abscesses and a large one for large ones or several cuts according to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which it is necessary to cut away the skin and incise when already formed like a cloth and has reached its limit because now it is dead such as happens with many ulcers and swellings.
And of these some are incised by an incision having three angles. And there is one of them which is incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess of the groin.
And among them there are those in which you make a round incision or a lunar one and incisions similar to these.
That which has no head like soft superficial abscesses must be opened by a superficial or flat incision only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected a lot of pus, and you puncture it, do not rush to extract all the pus at that moment; instead, remove only a portion from within. Then neglect the abscess until another day. Then extract something from the pus again.
Do this many times in stages until it is gone; especially if the patient is weak and feeble, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old decrepit man: for frequently the animal spirit of one animal dissipates along with the discharge of pus; and perhaps the patient may die without you realizing.
Therefore, exercise great caution in this chapter.
And after your puncture of the abscess with this instrument, you must clean the wound; and take note. If the wound of the abscess is small or it is a flat cut, then apply lint made from linen, or old cotton. But if the abscess is large and there are many punctures, then you must insert lint into each puncture until they meet one another.
And if in the abscess you have cut away a piece of skin or you have scooped it out around its edges, then you must fill this with old cotton, or like when you weave cloth, or scrape with linen wool without moisture; and bind it tight until the third day.
Then wash it clean and take care of it with whatever ointment is required until it heals.
If there is a flow of blood during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after soaking a cloth in them; place it on the bleeding site multiple times.
If the bleeding persists, you must apply a diet, treatment, and powders that I have described in many places throughout this book and its sections.
If it is winter time and the site of abscess involves many nerves; then you must apply small poultices made with warm wine and oil on these areas.
And if it is summer and the areas are fleshy; then you must apply poultices soaked in water and oil or wine and oil, making sure everything is cold. Keep doing this until the third day, as mentioned before, then you must open the abscess, clean it and apply treatment suitable for it until it is healed.
This, therefore, is what is necessary for the general knowledge of abscess treatment.
Following the path of this book's structure, I have already described every type of abscess and how to treat each one in detail.
Chapter 41.
## On the treatment of abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
Abscesses that occur on the skin of the head are small and similar to nodules; they contain cysts which resemble chicken pouches.
And there are many types of these abscesses.
Among these are the fatty tumors; and among them are those which contain moisture resembling mud; and among them are those containing moisture like mincemeat and poultices; also such as resemble these; and there are among them hard stony ones.
And in the operation on all these growths, and their removal there is no danger provided that during the operation upon them the artery does not present an obstacle.
And the operation in their excision consists of exploring them with an instrument whose form is given below and which is called a trocar until you know what they contain.
For if it contains moisture then cut it open by an incision lengthwise along the surface in this manner.
And begin the incision from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture is evacuated then scrape out the cyst which contains that moisture and do not leave any of it behind.
For it frequently returns if any part of it is left behind.
Then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment if it is available; and if not then in water and salt; and fill with it the wound, and leave it till the next day. For it eats away what remains of the cyst.
Then repeat over it the Egyptian ointment a second and third time; and if you require it then continue till you are confident that there is no part of the cyst remaining: then treat the wound with any sort of ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty tumour then cut it open in this form with an incision having the shape of a cross; and throw hooks between the lips of the incision, and scrape it out on all sides: and endeavour your strength at extracting the capsule which contains it.
If however the artery presents an obstruction to you, then do what I shall tell you after this.
And do likewise for an abscess if it is of the stony type, by incision and cure with the same treatment as we have said.
And the incision above a stony abscess is easier because there is little blood or moisture. And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses which occur on the head in some people where moisture is completely absent.
This is because I incised an abscess on the head of a woman, an old lady, and found that the abscess was like a solid hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; indeed if anyone struck it with their finger they would wound themselves.
However, with other types of abscesses occurring on the head except these types that occur on children's heads and at the roots of their ears, the incision should be a shallow one for all such cases.
Or make the perforation of them always from their lower part so that you may ease the course of the pus downwards.
Then cure it with whatever is suitable to their treatment.
On the incision of goitres on the neck.
Chapter 42.
These abscesses often occur on the neck, under the shoulders and hips, but sometimes they are found elsewhere in the body too.
Those of this type which occur on the neck can be a single growth or several; they breed from one another and each goitre among them has its own membrane like a skin just as is seen with knots and abscesses in the head, as we described earlier.
There are many types of these tourniquets. And some of them are made of stone; others contain moisture; yet others are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, for that one among them which you see to be of a good disposition on touch, whose colour is close to that of the skin, and it moves with every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck nor to the muscles nor to the artery, nor does it lie deep: it ought to be cut by a superficial incision from the upper body to the lower body along the line b. to g., and scrape it all over; and stretch out two lips of skin with a hook or many hooks if you need them, as we said in head abscesses, and extract it gradually.
And caution must be taken not to cut into any veins or nerves.
And let the knife be not too sharp, so that it does not give you difficulty in cutting and disturb the patient and cut into what is unnecessary.
But if you cut into a vein or artery and it prevents you from operating then apply to the wound some caustic rubbed down to powder, or certain powders which check blood, and bind up the wound and let it alone until the severity of the abscess has subsided, and the wound softens; otherwise it begins to decay.
For blood is checked by caustic substances; so return to your operation until you have finished it.
Then feel with your index finger whether there remains anything of the other small tourniquets; therefore cut them and clean them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the tourniquet then you ought not to cut that tourniquet from its root but rather you should tie it with strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without causing harm.
Then fill the wound with cotton that you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat yourself.
If the entire strophulus is cut off, then you should bring together the two lips of the wound and sew them immediately once you see that there is no more superfluity remaining.
But if you see that there is superfluity remaining because of the size of the strophulus, then it's necessary to aim at cutting its root and extracting it, and then administer the suture we described.
And for those strophuli that contain moisture, pierce it with a shallow puncture so its maturity site becomes apparent to you; and place the puncture below what follows from the body, as we described.
Then administer lint dressed with ointment and similar to it after the puncture, so that what remains of the corruption is corroded away until you see the wound now cleansed; then treat yourself with generative flesh-forming ointment until it's healed.
Chapter 43.
On the excision of the epiglottis from an abscess inside the throat that occurs.
The ancients called this excision of the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it; and here is their description of the operation.
In those with synanche, the excision of the epiglottis should be avoided as it is of no benefit due to all the veins and lung being compromised.
However, for those with a hot abscess in the mouth or throat or on both tonsils but without illness of the gullet, the excision of the epiglottis should be performed to escape destruction from suffocation that may result.
We should therefore, when we wish to do so, cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across its width, at the point between two circles in length, which is an incision into the soft tissue and not into the cartilage; and this indeed is a suitable place for the incision, since it lacks flesh, and blood vessels from there are distant.
If the physician is timid, he should then stretch out the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should slit the skin until he reaches the canal; let him avoid blood vessels if he sees any of them there.
Then he should cut the soft tissue as described; and the incision of the canal is indicated by the flow of phlegm that comes out from it along with what has been ruptured, and the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When therefore the hour is removed at which suffocation is feared, bring together the two lips of the skin wound and stitch it solely by itself without the cartilage.
Then apply medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
Albucasis says so. The sum of this speech we have related is not an explanation unless they see that one of these abscesses has already filled the throat of the sick person; and the patient is raised up to death, and wishes to be cut through in his panting: they proceed to cutting the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe on the place of the wound with a certain breath, and may be saved from death. And for this reason they have ordered the leaving open of the wound until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy is three days, or almost itself: then therefore they have ordered to stitch the wound, and to cure it until it is healed.
And what I witnessed was that one female servant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, thereby cutting through a part of the pulmonary canal.
I was called to treat her, and I found that she was moaning like the moan of a dying person.
I therefore examined the wound and found that very little blood had come out of it. I was therefore confused because she had not severed the vein or artery: yet air was coming out of the wound.
I therefore prepared some warm water and cleaned the wound until it was healthy; and nothing happened to the female servant except that she had a hoarseness in her voice, and after several days she returned with an improved condition.
From this I say therefore: there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
On cutting an oesophageal hernia.
Chapter 44.
This abscess named Oesophageal hernia is a large one, according to the colour of the body, and it occurs much more frequently in women.
It occurs in two ways: either naturally or accidentally.
Certainly it is not natural genius.
It is accidental, however, in two ways: one of which resembles fatty lumps. And the other way is similar to an abscess that forms from arterial pulsation. And it carries risk, and one should not presume to operate on it with an iron instrument, unless for a minor procedure; and you must first try it out, and investigate its interior using the introducer. If you find it similar to a fatty tumor and not suspended by any of the veins, then cut it out as tumors are cut; and remove it together with whatever contents there are from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; or otherwise, then explore its entirety. Then treat the site with what is required for healing, until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On excision of superficial tumors:
There are many types of tumors and I have already said all their types in the classification; it is necessary for me to explain here the difference between a tumor and an excrescence since this causes doubt.
I say therefore that when there is an excrescence, there is heat and fever, with violent pains until the boiling overflow of corruption subsides and is completed; then indeed do the fevers and heat subside.
But with a tumor there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains; and they are contained by a cyst or sac which is their own case, and is of the color of the body; and its inception is like a chickpea, and it becomes like batecha, and is both larger and smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty; or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, as I said in the classification.
You must therefore, when you come to treat a tumor, examine it and probe it first with the instrument called an introiterator, according to which its form will come in the chapter that follows this one.
And the method of probing abscesses and all tumors is this: take up that instrument and insert it into the moistest spot, which you find in the abscess; then turn your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has now penetrated through the skin. Then measure out your hand according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the introiterator and consider what comes out in its track.
If, therefore, moisture flows out of it, whatever its colour may be; then dry it by a shallow incision, as I have said about other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the track of the introiterator, then know that it is a fatty tumor; therefore dry it by an incision having the form of a cross, like this one, as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and scrape off the skin all round gently: and keep a poultice over it if you can to draw out the disease with the tumour.
But if the poultice is burst at the operation and it is not possible to draw out the whole of the disease, which often happens, then cut away every bit of it till there remains nothing more of it.
For if any part, small or great, remains, the tumour returns for the most part.
If it gets the better of you and there still remains a bit of it; then fill up the wound at its close with some sharp corrosive powders: bind up the wound, and put on what sits over the abscess warm, and treat it by the rest of the cure till it is healed.
If it is a large cyst, then its two lips, and treat it with that which causes flesh to grow into it.
But if a pulsating or non-pulsating vein opposes you, then hurry and fill the place with pitch that has been pounded; leave it tight for two or three days until the wound becomes rotten and the boiling of blood subsides; then return to cut what remains of the cyst.
Chapter 46.
The instruments necessary for cutting and piercing.
Among them are probes, which are of three types.
Of these, there is one large, another medium-sized, and the third small.
They should be made of iron wires with square tips to facilitate their entry into the abscess.
These are the probes, that is, piercing instruments; and one of them is called beris, that is, the piercer, which is also of three types.
Of these, there is one large, another medium-sized, and the third small.
They agree to be used to investigate abscesses, ulcers, fistulas, and buboes by that which is made of bone among them.
Others are made round, smooth, and soft like large needles with which cushions are stuffed: that is, from leather or brass or iron or silver.
And the best of them are those made from leather.
Probes are also made of black lead for use in exploring fistulas with rough surfaces to gently rub against those surfaces.
And there are again three types. Because from them there are long ones, from them medium ones, and from them short ones, according to the quantity of the length of the fistula and its stricture.
The form of splints, and they are of many types.
Because from them there are simple ones, that is to say those which have one curve; and they are of three types as you see, large, medium, and small.
And from them there are blind splints; and they are of three types.
And from them there are hooks having two curves, and they are of three types; and from them there are hooks having three curves, and they also are of three types; and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels with which nodes and abscesses are cut open and skinned; and they are of three types, because from them there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
Their cutting ends are sharp and their other ends are not sharp; and they are not applied like this unless with them help is provided in the skinning of nodes for fear of incision into a vein or nerve; and so that the sick person may be healed by means of them, and find some relief from the burning compared to what he finds when the abscess is skinned.
They are made of copper, and they are like a tube with which spirits are made; and at the wide end there is hidden a needle's point; and in it runs within to without as you wish, as you see.
The shape of splinters hidden between the toes due to the ulcerated perforations: and they are not perceived by the patient; these come in three types, large, medium, and small.
Shapes of tourniquets with which the flow of blood is cut off; they are three types: large, medium and small.
They shall be made of brass or bronze, rounded, slightly elongated as you see; they should be thin.
And you should have these types of tourniquets with you in large and small sizes; so that you can quickly stop the blood flow when needed, especially when no medicine is available.
However, do not use them to stop blood flow from any part of the body; only in fleshy parts such as the calf of the leg, hip, forearm, breast, abdomen, groin and similar fleshy, moist areas.
Sometimes small instruments are made in the same shape from thin strips of wood resembling almond shells; with which blood is stopped when it flows out of a phlebotomy site or after an incision into a vein or artery. Sometimes they're also made rounded like this.
Chapter 47.
On the treatment of male breasts resembling those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men swell at the completion of sleep (that is when they begin to ejaculate during sleep) until they resemble those of women; and they remain ulcerated, ugly and sore.
Therefore he who is disgusted by it should cut above the nipple with a crescent shaped incision in this form from b to g; then scrape off all the fat. Then fill the wound with incarnative medicine. Next, bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and take care of it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it must be cut in two on its upper sides with two similar cuts in a moon-like shape, each one containing the other at either end, so that there is a larger line enclosing a smaller one, like this form from line b. to line g. Then scrape away the skin between the two lips and remove the fat, and apply what we have said about sutures and powders which are necessary for healing until it is healed.
But if you cannot complete the incision of what needs to be cut because of the patient's distress or bleeding: then you must fill the wound with a piece of corrosive ointment and leave it until it has eaten away any remaining fat. Then take care of it until it is healed.
On the perforation of an abscess which happens under the armpit.
Chapter 48.
These abscesses which happen under the armpit are hard in nature and contain a collection of pimples or contain moist humours.
Those which are of the moist type must be cut open with a moon-shaped incision like this form from line b to line g. When all the pus has been drained from the abscess then fill it with an old piece of cloth and leave it until the next day.
The wound should then be cleaned and treated with an appropriate ointment until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the type known as strophulus, then it should be lanced over its surface, as we have said in the chapter on strophuli.
But if the wound discharges pus, a caustic should be applied to it, as we said above.
Chapter 49.
On the excision of an abscess which occurs between an artery and a vein.
When an artery is torn and the skin above it heals over, an abscess often forms; likewise with a vein, so that an abscess and swelling may occur therein.
The signs by which it can be recognized that the abscess and swelling are due to an artery and a vein are these: when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and deep in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, there is almost a crepitation.
And that which is due to the vein is round on the surface of the body.
The incision of this abscess is a mistake, especially that which occurs under the armpits, groins, neck and in many places on the body; it is extremely dangerous. It should therefore be treated without the use of the knife, and even those which are on extremities or the head ought to be avoided.
In that which is due to the swelling of an artery's orifice, make an incision lengthwise on the skin. Then open the incision with hooks, afterward the artery: and free it from its sheaths until it is uncovered.
Then introduce beneath it a needle and make it penetrate to the other side; then ligature the artery with double thread in two places as I taught you for extracting two arteries that are in the temples.
Then prick the place between the two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out and the abscess is discharged.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off; afterward treat it with suitable ointments until it is healed.
If the abscess is due to a vein having been cut then you must hold with your hand as much of it as possible together with the skin. Then introduce the needle under that place which you held with your hand and make it penetrate; and let there be on it double thread until you withdraw it from the other side. Then ligature the abscess with it by means of a good ligature; as I told you for single ligaturing, from two sides with the two ends of the thread.
But if you are afraid that the thread will be withdrawn then introduce another needle with another thread beneath the whole abscess near the wound of the first needle; press and ligature your threads at four places.
Then slit the abscess down the middle until all that is inside comes out; then cut off the superfluous skin and leave what has been ligatured from it as it is.
Then put over it a poultice which you have steeped in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with bandages and ointments, until it heals.
Chapter 50.
The abscess that occurs from nerve torsion.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, similarly, an abscess can occur in nerves; this happens when they are struck or due to excessive effort, and similar things; it most often occurs in the regions of the assistants' [muscles] or calves, and in every place where joints move: and it is a severe abscess.
Its colour is similar to the rest of the body, and it appears in multiple shapes without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, one feels in it a numbness like giddiness.
And it is not an abscess collected deep within the body, but under the skin; and it moves to every side, and does not go forward or back towards the front, nor backwards towards the rear.
What then arises from them in joints should not be invaded with iron [a knife or blade]. Perhaps contraction will occur.
However, for that which is from them in the head or forehead, lift up the skin with a spatula. So if it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps, and cut it away from its root.
And if it is large, then lift it up with a hook and scrape it; afterwards remove it, and collect the wound with a suture; then treat it until it heals.
Chapter 51.
On the incision of an abscess that occurs on the abdomen.
There occur to some people frequently in their bellies and other parts of their bodies swellings which are called 'fungus', on account of their likeness to fungi.
The root of which is fine and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And there is some that are small; and there is some which grow to an extraordinary size.
And indeed I have seen a man in whose belly were two swellings which more resembled fungi than other things: there was no difference between them except that the one had a white root and was smaller; and their lips were inverted and they discharged pus, and moisture flowed from them continually.
So I cut them out and found that one weighed about ten ounces and the other six. And when operating on them you should note that if the patient is moist of constitution and the colour of the swelling is white and it has a moist fine root, then you should cut it with a spatulate knife an inch wide; and have ready some cauteries in the fire.
For frequently much blood is discharged when it is cut into; so if the bleeding gets the better of you, then cauterize it at once.
But if you see the patient fearful and timid about being cut with an iron knife, then take a thread well made of lead and bind it around the swelling in the manner which I have described; and leave it on for two days.
Then add some caustic powder to the binding of lead. So do not cease binding with lead whenever it bites into the swelling until it falls off by itself without further trouble.
If, however, the root of the tumor is large, then there is some fear and dread associated with making an incision into it; this is especially true for tumors located in the abdomen.
However, it is possible to cut it in half. Then cauterize it so that it does not return.
Treat the wound until it heals.
Also be careful not to rush into cutting a tumor that is dark in color, has little feeling, and looks ugly; for it is indeed a cancerous abscess, and I shall come back with a reminder of Cancer later on.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia forms for many reasons. It occurs either because of a tear in the fascia above the abdomen through which protrudes an omentum or intestine, depending on what happens in other hernias. Or from blood that flows out of a vein or an artery, as has been mentioned before. Or from air trapped within it.
If it is due to a tear in the fascia and has an exit for feces, then it is the colour of an abscess-like body and is soft, pain-free, and appears at various sites. And if it is due to the exit of an intestine, then its location will be of a more violent variety, as we have described above. When you therefore press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns and is perhaps accompanied by a sound; and it grows greatly upon entering the bath and with violent effort.
If it is due to moisture, it is soft; then it is not concealed when you press it with your hand, neither constricted nor added.
If it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears blackening the skin.
If however it is due to born flesh: then it is a grave abscess, large, hard, and swelling according to its quantity.
If it is due to moisture, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is, as you should then consider, if the navel's swelling is due to arterial blood, venous blood or moisture: then you ought to be restrained from its cure. For in it is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter where I spoke of abscesses due to arteries and veins.
If the navel's swelling is due to intestine or stomach: then you ought to advise the patient to hold their breath and stand stretched out. Then mark with caustic around the navel's whole circumference. Afterwards instruct them to lie on their back between your hands on their back. Then surround with a broad spatula around the navel on the place you marked with caustic. Afterwards extend towards higher parts from the middle of the abscess with a large hook. Then bind him around firmly with strong thread or a silk cord: and let the knot of the binding be slack.
Then open the middle of the abscess, extending above the ligament. If you therefore find it already grasped by the ligament: then extract the ilex hook and insert your index finger into it, pushing the intestine towards the interior of the belly; and if you find a cecum, then take hold of it with a hook, and cut off its superfluity.
But if the artery or vein is facing away from you: then grasp it firmly. Then return to your operation. Take two needles and thread a strong thread through each; then insert the two needles into the circumference you made around the abscess, arranged like a cross, ensuring they penetrate [the skin].
Then draw the abscess together at four points above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, leaving an open area until the extended flesh becomes necrotic, and it falls away of its own accord; or cut it away when it is softening and becoming necrotic.
Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the umbilical hernia is made of flesh with an opening into it, or if it's fluid-filled; then you must make the abscess concave as we described above. And extract any blood or fluid that you find inside it.
Then treat the hernia with what is appropriate to solidify the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed the different types of cancer and how to treat them with medications; and we advised caution when treating it with iron instruments so as not to cause an ulceration; and we spoke about cancers that originate in the uterus and precautions against treating them.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is located in a place where its eradication is possible; such as cancer found in the breasts or hip, and similar locations on parts of the body which are easier to remove entirely; especially when it is small and just beginning.
When however it has become old, and is large, you should not approach it.
I myself could not heal anyone of it; nor have I seen any other before me who has reached it.
And the treatment for it, when located as we have said, is to proceed with purging the patient of black bile several times. Then perform bloodletting if you find it with clear engorgement.
Then prepare the patient for treatment, which should be done with care.
Then apply hooks to pull out cancerous tissue. Next, scoop it out from all sides with the skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and allow blood to flow without cutting quickly but by squeezing the areas and wiping off thick blood with your hand or any other suitable tool available.
If however the flow of thick blood from an artery or vein presents a problem, then cauterize the vein until the blood stops flowing. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until cured.
On the Treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter 54. Now we have described in the division of types of dropsy, and how the accumulation of water occurs: and all signs of each type, and its cure with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the type of ascites; and it does not approach with iron for types tympanitis or hyposarcum, for this is mortal.
When you have been already treated this type of dropsy tympanitis with medicines, and it has not yielded to your treatment, then consider. For if the patient has already developed weakness, or there is another illness besides dropsy, such as a cough, stomach problems, or similar; then beware of treating them with iron, for it is to be feared.
But if you see a patient with abundant strength, no other illness besides dropsy alone; and they are neither young nor old: then the method of treatment is for you to lift them up while standing between your hands, while an assistant behind them presses on their stomach with their hand, forcing water downwards.
Then take a sharp spine, shaped like this. It should have two pointed parts, be long, with the point like that of a spine. Yet let there be some shortening along it gradually so you do not pierce through to the intestine during treatment and injure it.
Then consider... For if the dropsy has originated from the intestines, then it is necessary that you make an incision three fingers long below the navel, leading downwards towards the pubic bone.
If the cause of hydropsy is disease of the liver then your cut should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its cause is the spleen then your cut should be on the right side and it must also be on whichever side the patient wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow towards a weak place.
Then pierce the skin entirely with an instrument. Then insert into that incision and raise your hand with a spatula between the skin, and scrape as if you are flaying it; and let what is scraped off be the size of a fingernail or thereabouts.
Then pierce that which has been scraped until the spatula reaches an empty space; this is a place of water. Then extract it and insert into the hole an instrument that is shaped like this: [diagram of an instrument]. And it is like a cannula; it is made of silver or brass or gilded bronze, soft and clean; there is a small hole at its lower end, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side and one on the other: and its end is cut in such a way as to resemble the incision of a reed, as you see; at its upper end is an annular ring.
For when the instrument reaches water then it immediately sinks on top of the instrument; so evacuate from the water at once half a measure [of liquid].
Because if you evacuate more than is necessary then perhaps at that moment the patient will die due to loss of his animal spirit; or he will suffer a swoon which approaches death.
Indeed, evacuate from it according to the quantity of its strength, and according to what the dispositions of the patient indicate to you regarding the strength of their pulse and their good health.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water.
This is because it is kept immediately next to the skin which retains the hole that is over the fistula: of which I have spoken, so that you may bore it according to that manner.
Then repeat the use of the instrument on the second day, if you see that the patient can tolerate it: and also remove a small quantity from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself against error: until there remains of the water only a small amount.
But if you fear for the patient, and let out a large amount of water: then treat it with burial in hot sand, and much sweating in the bath and the sun, and allow them to suffer thirst: and treat it with drying medications until they are healed: and cauterize over the stomach, flanks, and spleen after the extraction of water: according to what I have spoken about previously.
On the treatment of children who are born with their urine passageways unperforated.
Chapter 55.
When any child emerges from their mother's womb, and the head of their penis is unperforated, or has a narrow hole, or is in the wrong place...

But those in whom the hole is not in its proper place suffer harm. This is because in those who are born the hole is at the end of the shaft; therefore they cannot urinate forwards until they raise the penis upwards with their hand. Nor is he able to generate offspring because the semen cannot reach the womb by going straight; and this is a very nasty disease.

And take care during your procedure not to suffer from a bleeding. It often happens indeed. Object therefore to him with that which cuts off the blood, and take care of the wound until it is healed.
 Of phimosis that occur in the foreskin and glans penis, and of their corruption, blackening, and adherence.
Chapter 56.
These phimoses often occur on the penis and there is an ugly flesh bump.
And from this some are malignant, and others not malignant.

In the case of an adhesion of the foreskin with the head of the rod, and this kind of adhesion only occurs in someone whose foreskin is healthy and does not need circumcision; and when there is an adhesion due to a wound or swelling: you must therefore scrape it away using a blunt spatulum until the ligament is loosened and the head of the rod is freed on all sides.

Then it should be treated with styptic wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
On cleansing children and curing them from error occurring to them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like the rest of wounds; but since it is from our action, by our will, and its use is proper for boys, it is necessary that we mark this operation with an improvement and lead to health via an easier path.
I say therefore that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because they do not use it in their laws and it is only something we acquired through experience.
This is because I found that most craftsmen and bloodletters use cleansing by cutting with a knife or scissors; they apply alfechi (a type of medicine) and ligature with thread, and incision with an unguis (nail tool).
I myself have now tested all these methods, and I did not find a better cleansing method than using scissors and ligature with thread.
Because in cleansing with a knife the skin often escapes (i.e., is not properly treated). Because the foreskin's skin has two layers; therefore, perhaps the upper layer is incised and the lower layer escapes (i.e., is not treated), necessitating another incision and causing pain. And with cleansing that is done with alfechi, i.e., a finger-like tool which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the male member is not secured because it may enter its opening (and cause damage).
In cleansing with an unguis (nail tool), perhaps the iron slips and spoils your operation; moreover, a boy's skin is naturally short (i.e., small) and they are often born in such a way that they do not need circumcision - I have seen this myself.
The process of excision with scissors and tying the wound with thread proved to be a good experience for me because the scissors are proportionate to the incisions; therefore, as their sharpness above is also sharp below. When you place your hand on the above-mentioned premises, the two sharpnesses are proportionate and make incisions according to one operation at a given time; thus creating compression of similar form containing skin on every part of the rod with which there is no possible mistake.
The way to perform this operation is that you make the boy, especially one with some intelligence, understand that you are doing nothing other than tying his thread around the rod and will let him go until another day.
Then make him enjoy and rejoice in every possible way from the fact that he understands it. Then make him stand between your prepared hands and not be seated; hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot so that the boy does not see them nor any other instruments at all.
Then insert your hand into his rod and blow on the skin; pull it upwards until the head of the rod comes out. Then clean that part from any dirt that has gathered on it.
Then tie the marked place with double thread. Afterwards, tie below that another slightly looser one according to what is needed. Then hold between your thumb and index finger the lower place with a good grip; make an incision in between both threads. Then lift the skin upward quickly and extract the head of the rod. Then let the blood run out gradually (for this is better), and put powder on it; then upon the powder a cloth spread with reduced veal broth in which has been stirred rose oil of good quality, bruised with fresh roses; leave him thereon until another day, and treat with remaining treatment until he is healed.
The shape of the scissors used for cleansing (wounds) is as follows:...
Let there be two sharp points at the ends and let them intersect; lest an arm of the nail jut out on these; and let the length of both cutting edges be equal to the length of the two handles.
However an error occurring in cleansing is this: for perhaps the whole of the inner skin has escaped, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore you must stretch it back from your hour with your nail before the application of the styptic; and cut it equally.
But if you cannot hold it with your nail then extract it with a hook and cut it.
But if three days have passed and that which is under the swollen rod remains an abscess; then let it be, until the abscess sits warm, and scrape it with ease, and cut it as prepared for you: and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm him.
Treat it therefore with that which consolidates wounds from the powders we described in the treatise on powders; and if it is cut out of the skin beyond measure and escapes upward, neither will this harm him either.
Take care of it with what we have said until it heals.
Chapter 58.
On the extraction of retained urine from the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an occlusion caused by a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by flesh and things similar to those that we have described among the ways of treatment in this division.
And if the urine does not loosen and you see that its retention is due to a stone which has already reached the neck of the bladder; then it's necessary for the patient to be positioned bent over his knees. Then his feet should be arranged on his back and he should stretch out his belly from the upper part of his spine; and the patient should draw in his breath as far as is possible for him. Thus the neck of the bladder is forced towards the back for the ejection of the stone; therefore, urine is evacuated.
But if this does not resolve and the matter is severe for the patient; then it's necessary that you perform its extraction with an instrument called a syringe, of which this is the form Let it be made of silver, and let it be fine, soft, hollow like the quill of a feather in fineness equal to a ray, long about the length of one palm: let there be a fine nozzle at its head.
And the way of urine's exit through it is, take a doubled string and tie at its end wool or cotton, a good ligature: and insert the end of the string into the lower part of the syringe; and cut with scissors if anything superfluous remains from the wool, but insert [the filament] into the nozzle gently like a plug.
First grease the syringe with oil or butter and the white of an egg; then make the patient sit on a seat, and swab the fistula, and anoint his member with moistened lint and olive-oil and tepid water.
Then introduce the syringe into the member gently until it reaches the root of the member.
Then tie it with two threads above, on the part of the belly.
Then push the syringe inside it until you reach the bladder and feel the patient has now reached emptiness; and this does not happen according to this order unless because the passage, where he enters, is one of twists and turns.
Then draw out the thread with wool gradually. For what you seek will follow the wool.
Then extract it and make what you seek run through the urine passage; then repeat with the syringe and do not cease to do this until the fistula is emptied and the patient finds relief.
On the quality of bladder irrigation with a syringe.
Chapter 59.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder or blood is congealed in it, or pus is confined there and you wish to pour water and medicines into it; then use this instrument which is called a syringe, and this is its form.
It is closed, that is to say deaf. And it has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
The hollow place where the piston is located should be of a size to accommodate it without any additional space; as long as when drawn back, some fluid adheres to it and when pushed forward, that same fluid is expelled outward like a force pump discharging Greek fire (naphtha), i.e., during the battles of Mars.
When you wish to discharge fluids into the bladder, immerse the end of the pump into the fluid and draw upwards on the piston; for the liquid will be drawn up into the hollow of the pump.
Then immerse the end of it into the rod, as we have described for the syringe. Then push the fluid with the piston; that very fluid reaches the bladder immediately so that the patient feels it.
Design an enema apparatus by which to give an enema to the bladder.
It should be made of silver or latten; its head to be higher than the small alcam and beneath this a hollow into which falls the ligature.
Take the bladder of a ram and put into it whatever fluid you wish to administer as an enema to the bladder. Then tie it between two hollows with a strong ligature of double thread, and warm that fluid somewhat over the fire.
Then immerse the end of the enema apparatus into the rod; afterwards press your hand over the bladder containing the fluid with a gentle pressure until the patient feels that the liquid has reached his bladder.
If the bladder is not available to you, then take a piece of parchment and make from it a circle; then perforate it throughout its roundness. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes as they revolve. Then assemble the circle as you would a wheel: after placing on it what you want of moistures and oils and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument; and do with it as you did with the bladder from an expression by hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
 On the extraction of stones.
We have already spoken about the types of stones and their treatment with medicine; I have explained the difference between a stone formed in the kidneys, bladder, and its symptoms overall; I have described a stone that requires surgery using the hand or cutting; there is also a stone formed specifically in the bladder; and one fixed to a rod. I am narrating all this with clear exposition and abbreviation.
I say therefore that a stone formed in the bladder occurs most often in boys.
And from its symptoms are that the urine comes out of the bladder like water due to its thinness; and there appears white sand in it, and the patient rubs his penis, labours with it, and hangs down frequently. Then he rises up, and the anus comes out with him in many of them.
And healing is easy for boys until such time as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. Among young men it is moderate; for those whose stone is larger, cure is easier; if it's smaller, the patient's condition worsens.
When we reach the stage of treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the patient with an enema, which will extract all the feces that are in their intestines; for this very thing sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone during examination.
Next, the patient should be taken by their feet and shaken, and moved downwards; so that the stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or jump out from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands, prepared; and let his hands be under his hips, so that the bladder becomes completely sloped downwards.
Then examine him, and feel him externally.
If therefore you perceive the stone within an area, then hasten immediately with an incision over it.
If it does not fall under your touch at all, then you must cleanse your index finger with oil of the left hand if the patient is a child; or the middle finger if they are an adult; and insert it into their anus, and feel for the stone until it lies steady beneath your finger: and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and push it to the outside towards the place of incision which you wish to make; and instruct an assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and direct another assistant to lift the testicles with his right hand upwards: and with his other hand, to remove the skin that is beneath the testicles on the side of the place where the incision will be made.
Then insert the scalpel, cutting with one which is shaped like this: And make the incision on it which lies between the anus and the testicles, but not in the middle, towards the left buttock: and let the incision be made over the stone itself; and your finger should be inside the anus: and make a transverse cut, so that the exterior cut is wide, while the interior one is narrow, according to how large it can be for the exit of the stone from there not to be larger.
Perhaps the finger compresses the stone which is at the anus during the operation, and it comes out without difficulty.
And you should know that because of its corners and edges, a stone is something from which difficulty arises during its removal.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round: its exit becomes easy.
Therefore for those with corners and edges, add it to the wound.
If it does not come out like this, then you should use a lever on top of the stone; or squeeze above it with an implement whose end is curved like the moon, that squeezes the stone and does not slip from it; or insert beneath it a slender instrument with a curved end.
If you cannot do so from above, then widen the hole slightly. If anything of blood overcomes you, cut it away with the knife.
But if there is more than one stone, then push the larger towards the bladder's mouth first. Then cut above it. Afterwards push the smaller one after that; and do likewise if there are several, in twos.
If the large one is very big, it is ignorance to cut above it with a large incision. For the patient either dies, or is troubled by constant dribbling of urine; this happens because the place does not heal at all.
But give him the instrument of discharge until it comes out; or insert levers into its fracture with forceps, until you extract it in pieces.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with incense, aloe, and dragon's blood; compress it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: to settle the hot abscess.
Then turn the patient onto their front over their neck, and do not remove the bandage until the third day. When you remove it therefore, bathe the place with water and lots of oil.
Then treat it with daphne ointment and royal ointment until it is healed.
If however, a hot abscess develops on the wound and it becomes ulcerous, and other similar things happen; such as if blood freezes in the bladder and prevents urine from coming out: this is known by the passage of blood with the urine. Insert therefore your finger into the wound and draw out that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and apply to it everything that is suitable for its cure until it is healed.
And it is necessary throughout the whole time of healing that you bind together the two thighs and attach them; for the sake of medicines which are placed on the place.
If however the stone is small and it is in the channel of the penis and becomes fixed there, and prevents urine from coming out; then treat it with what I have told you before you resort to cutting. For many times will you be excused from the operation by this cure; for I have already proved it.
And it is necessary to take a perforator made from iron or steel, which has this form, with a triangular tip that is sharp and fixed into wood.
Then take some thread and tie it to the rod so that it doesn't return to the vesicle with a stone underneath.
Insert the piercing iron into the rod with ease until it reaches the stone; then turn the piercer with your hand on top of the stone gently and gradually, attempting to make that hole deeper until you break through from the other side.
For urine is immediately released.
Then push your hand down on what's left of the stone from the outside part of the rod. For it will break apart and leave with the urine, and the patient will be healed.
But if this cure is not prepared for you because of something that prevents it from happening, then tie a thread underneath the stone and another thread over the stone. Then cut above the stone on the rod between the two bindings. Then extract it. Afterwards, untie the binding and clean the frozen blood that forms on the wound.
And it is not necessary for the thread underneath the stone unless to prevent its return to the vesicle; and another binding over the stone unless so that when you untie the thread after the stone has left, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound. And that's why you need to raise the skin upwards when tying the upper thread so that it returns upon completion and covers the wound as we've said.
Chapter 61.
The removal of bladder stones in women.
Bladder stones occur less often in women.
If bladder stones occur in any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is prohibited due to the many complications.
One of these is when the woman in question is perhaps still a virgin.
And the second is that you do not find a woman who will expose herself to a doctor when she is chaste or among those having husbands.
The third is that you do not find a woman who has sufficient knowledge of this art, especially the surgical procedure with their hand.
And the fourth is that it is far away from the stone's location, requiring deep incisions; this poses some concerns.
If necessity requires you to proceed with it, then you should take a female physician who is well-versed; such people are rarely found.
If you decide to proceed with the procedure, then seek out a chaste and skilled male physician; also find an experienced female midwife or a woman who has some skill in this art: make her present too and instruct her to carry out everything that you prescribe regarding examination of the stone.
Firstly, it is necessary to examine whether the woman is a virgin; then you must insert your finger into her anus and search for the stone.
If you find it, grasp the stone beneath your finger and then instruct the midwife to insert their finger into the patient's vagina; they should examine for the stone after placing their left hand on her bladder and applying good compression.
If he finds a stone, then it is necessary that he gradually move it from the urethral opening to the lower part with all his strength until he reaches the hip joint with it.
Then he cuts over it at the opposition of the midpoint of the pubic bone near the hip joint, from whichever side is prepared for him and where he feels the stone; his finger should not be removed from the stone, pressing underneath it; and let the first cut be small. Then he should insert his finger over that cut.
Then he should insert his finger over that cut. If he feels the stone, then he should enlarge the cut according to how much of it is coming out from it.
And remember that stones come in many forms. Among these there is one that is small or large; smooth or rough; it may be long or round with branches.
Therefore, you should recognise its type so that it may be signified to you by this over what you wish.
If blood overcomes you, then add some crushed ginger to the place for an hour until it stops bleeding.
Then return to your procedure until the stone comes out; and prepare what I have mentioned for extracting stones from men so that you can be helped by them with your procedure.
If you are overcome by bleeding and know that the blood is coming from an artery that has been cut; then put some powder on it and bind it with suitable bandages; do not change them; leave the stone alone and do not remove it. Perhaps the sick woman will die. Then treat the wounds.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood is stilled after a few days, and the place becomes suppurated, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
 On cutting for watery hernia.
Watery rupture is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the sac which is below the skin covering the egg-containing testicle; and it is called the scrotum.
And sometimes it is in its own little sac which nature has prepared for it on the part of the egg; and this is another egg, and it is the skin of the testicle between the scrotum that we mentioned; and this happens only rarely.
And the cause of this type of rupture is indeed weakness that happens to the testicles; because this moisture-like material pours out towards them. And sometimes it happens due to an impact on the testicles.
And this moisture-like material has many colours. Or else its colour is inclined towards yellow, or it is bloody red, or dirty black, or watery white; and this last one is more frequent.
The signs by which you may learn where the collection of water is, are these: For if it is in the scrotal sac which we have mentioned, then the abscess forms a round swelling of even length like an egg-shape; and the testicle does not appear because moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if there is moisture in its own bandage, then it becomes a round abscess with an ear of barley, and because of this the man mistakes it for something else.
And if moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white part of the scrotum, then it falls under this category.
When therefore you wish to know the colour of the moisture, then try the colour of the moisture in the abscess with a square introduced, whose shape preceded it. According to that which emerges in the track of the introduced object, judge about what is between it.
When therefore we come to treatment with the iron, then it is necessary that you instruct the patient to be bled if possible for him, and observe his body to be full.
Then he shall lie on his back on something somewhat high, and place beneath him many cloths.
Then sit to his left, and instruct the assistant to sit at his right, who shall stretch his rod to one of two sides of the skin of the scrotum, and towards the navel.
Then take a spatula broad and flat, and cut the skin of the scrotum from its middle lengthwise, near to the comb; and let the incision be made according to an equally distant line which divides the skin of the scrotum until it reaches the white part embracing [the testicle]: and scrape it off and observe it, or take care against this, lest you cut into it: and let your scraping, by means of which you scrape, be from that part which is suspended with the egg more: and carefully examine with your scraping according to the quantity as far as possible for you.
Then pierce the bladder full of water with a wide hole, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and stretch the bladder upwards, but do not touch the skin covering the testicles: and cut the bladder as best you can, either all at once or piecemeal, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully examine it, there is no safety that water will not return due to its incision.
But if an egg comes out of its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the bladder's incision, push it back inside. Then join the two lips of the skin of the testicles with stitches.
Then treat it like other wounds until they heal.
If you find the egg corrupted by other blackness, then you must tie up the vessels which are its support, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its support, and extract the egg; afterwards treat it as we have said.
But if water is gathered into two parts, then know that there are two branches; therefore cut off the other part as you did the first equally.
And if it is equal to be one operation, then do this: afterwards wipe away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rosewater; and put outside another piece of wool that you have steeped in wine and oil, and spread it over both testicles and groins; and place doubled cloths on top, which are called 'poultices'; and tie them on with a bandage having six according to this pattern.
Two cloths should be taken and soaked; then they are filled with red lead powder, like so: the ends that are pressed are made of cotton or wet wool; these will be tied together around the patient's arms; one end reaches from above the anus to the back; the other side goes across the chest to the front; two more go over each thigh and finally another two go round both groins, and all ends should then be securely tied together around the patient's armpits.
Sometimes this abscess may be artificially treated with the cautery instead of the knife; and you must take a fine lancet-shaped cautery and cut into the skin covering the testicles with it; and this should be hot, as we have described until you see white pus containing water coming out; then take the cautery in this shape.
Is similar to Greek letters.
Then pierce that white pus with it (the cautery), whilst hot, until all moisture comes out.
Then pull the white pus out with hooks, and scrape it with a fine hot cautery; then cut into it as much as you can until you get all of it out.
And be careful not to let the fire touch the testicles.
And you should know that this method is safer than the other one, it will stop bleeding for longer and it's better than cutting.
If however any difficulty arises during the operation, whether from a hot abscess or bleeding or anything else at the time of cutting or cauterizing, you must treat that problem with whatever treatment is appropriate for it as we have already said.
If, however, the patient is fearful and does not expect to survive this operation due to its horror: then perform the perforation as I have described it: and that is all.
When you have confirmed through what we have said about the symptoms that the patient is watery in constitution: then you must make the patient sit on an elevated chair.
Then perforate the abscess on its lower part with a broad spatula: and make the opening according to the length of the body, leaving it wide open until all water has drained out.
Then place a poultice on the site, bind it with a bandage, and let it stay in place until all remaining water has drained out; then treat the wound until it is healed.
If, however, there is resistance to the draining of water at the time when you perforate it: and this is not due to any other cause than that a white membrane creates an obstruction at the mouth of the wound and prevents the exit of water: then you must insert a feather into the wound, or the instrument used for drawing out dropsy fluid whose tip has been blunted: or introduce the latter into the operation on the wound in small amounts.
And you should be aware that water returns and accumulates after six months or thereabouts. When it has therefore accumulated, then perforate again in the same way, and the patient will pass his days.
Chapter 63.
On the treatment of fleshy rams' horns.
You should be aware that, in the incision over this horn, fear leads to death in many cases. Therefore, it seems to me his dismissal is a salvation from that.
And I am reminded of the procedure on it, and the appearance of an abscess.
I say, then, that many abscesses occur on bodies where the testes are located; and this happens for many reasons, to wit.
Or from an acute superfluity or sharpness which is poured out onto the testes. Or from a blow.
And the colour of the abscess is according to that of the body's complexion, and there is no pain; and the abscess is serious.
And perhaps it is a petrous abscess whose colour is dark and has no sensation.
And sometimes it happens from the hardening of arteries and their swelling; as has already been mentioned before; or from the swelling of veins.
What is then from these from the swelling of arteries is recognized by the arrangement of the abscess when you press it with your fingers. And one ought not to touch it at all.
That which happens from the swelling of veins does not discharge anything upon being pressed with fingers.
And now it is possible that an incision be made over this appearance, and over the remaining flesh; and you must excise the skin of the testes. Then extend the scrotum upwards and extract it with a white cloth; and free the spermatic cord from the testis, and tie off the testis; and cut the spermatic cord after you have freed it from every part of the scrotum.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those newly born flesh parts, then you must draw out the egg and cut it off.
And if there is an adherence between something from the siphon or in that which is between the vessels, then you must free up all of that adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then you must cut all of it and extract the egg as we said before.
Once your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool steeped in rose oil and wine; afterwards care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals up.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of fractures, which is done along with splints.
It is an abscess that has developed a sort of wrapping around itself like a swelling with softening of the testicles; and it becomes difficult for the patient to move or exercise and walk about.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear on account of what preceded its recollection.
But it behooves us to describe the treatment for this as was done by the ancients.
I say therefore that you must make the patient sit upon an elevated seat. Then push up the supporter of the testicles to the lower parts. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers while holding onto the vessels that are closest to the rod; and let another assistant grip this and stretch it with a strong pull.
Then make an incision with a sharp-edged spatula that is two digits wide across the straight path of the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
Then remove the skin from all sides as we have told you when extracting arteries that are located on prominent areas of bone.
After that, attach to them a needle in which is a thread twice as long and tie it on the first of those places where the veins are present; and tie it again at the last one. Then cut them in the middle of that straight incision according to the length of the body; and extract what is accumulated from dirty corrosive fluids.
Then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds where you want to generate pus so that the vessels which nourish one of the testicles may fall out. Indeed, it is not an evil thing.
But if it should happen to all the veins, then both testes with their vessels must be removed on account of this; so that no egg may lose its nourishment due to the incision and shrivel or dry up without being any use to them.
Chapter 65.
On treating branches of the intestine:
The cause of these branches is from an incision that occurred through the sifach extending over the belly into the region of the groin on account of a tear in the belly.
Why do the intestines from that rupture extend towards one of the testicles? This rupture is either on account of the sifach or its extension.
These two types of injuries occur due to multiple causes such as blows, jumps, shouting or lifting heavy objects and the like.
And the sign is that it comes from an extension of the scapula. It is a condition that happens gradually over time and not suddenly; it is an abscess with its deep aspect pointing inward because the scapula compresses the intestine.
And its sign is when it comes from the breaking of the scapula because at first it causes severe pain suddenly; and it is an abscess turned outward appearing just under the skin: this is due to the protrusion of the intestine and its course towards the outside of the scapula.
And sometimes it exits with the intestine's tube; this is called a 'Ramex intestinalis' or hernia, and sometimes it happens with wind.
And sometimes faeces pass into the intestine and get stuck there; this is deadly for the patient because it causes severe pain especially when pressure is applied to the area.
And there is fear concerning the use of iron in treating these types of illnesses; thus, one must be careful to avoid complications when using it.
The method of surgery is to instruct the patient to push the intestine back into their abdominal cavity with their own hand if they are able and willing to do so. Then have them lie down with their neck between your hands; then lift up their legs.
Then stretch the skin that follows it upwards and cut through all of the scrotal skin lengthwise. Then fix hooks into the two lips of the incision according to your need in opening up the wound with them; and let the incision be such that it is possible for an egg to exit through it.
Then scrape the spermatic cord and what is beneath the skin of the testicles until you uncover the white albuginea, which is hard on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white albuginea, which is under the scrotum's skin, and the second spermatic cord, and completely remove the adhesion that is in the scrotum's back part.
Then move with your right hand towards the interior of the scrotal skin and, as you do this, pull upwards on the white albuginea with your left hand; raise the testicle along with the spermatic cord toward the area to be cut, and tell your assistant to lift the testicle upward while you free the adhesion behind it upon completing the dissection.
And check with your fingers that nothing of the intestine is wrapped around there at the white hard albuginea. But if you find anything of it there, then push that part towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle with thick thread made of ten linen threads and insert it into the last spermatic cord, that is, the tunica vaginalis that follows the scrotum's incision; then cut off the protruding ends of the thread until you are left with four strands. Next, form some of these into a cross-shaped figure and bind them with the aforementioned 'alsifach' (a tough band) that lies beneath the testicle's skin; do this tightly around the spermatic cord so as to prevent any blood vessels from sending nourishment to it, lest an abscess forms. And place another band on top of the first one, two fingers' breadth away from it. After these two ligaments let there be left of the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, the amount of one finger's length; and cut off all that remains according to its roundness, and remove from it the egg.
Then cut through the lower part of the skin of the testicles with a slice from which blood and pus flow out; as we described above.
Next apply wool steeped in oil to the wound; and use the ligament as we described above. Sometimes the white sifach that we have mentioned is cauterized after its incision for fear of profuse bleeding. For this often happens. And leave the ligature in place until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then bathe it with warm water; and place over it the substance that we mentioned until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment of wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Chapter on the Ramice Leech.
In this chapter on the treatment of the Ramice Leech, I have not seen anyone who has dared to attempt its treatment with iron [instruments].
And those who were the first to comment on it said that its treatment is carried out as we described above for the ramice leech with the vine [leech]. That is, bind the cords around it after its incision with ease at the bottom; then cut in the middle and treat finally with what opens it until the cords fall off; and treat the wound as we said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
The disruption which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, as we have said, and the site protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it does descend, it is only for a short while, and then returns at all hours: but if this state persists for some time, there is added a hernia of the spermatic cord until eventually the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And this occurs due to an extension of the spermatic cord which is in the groins, as we have said. Which is to say, because the spermatic cord becomes extended; then it softens, the site hardens, and it protrudes.
And its treatment is with the caustic, as has been mentioned in the previous chapter.
And sometimes it is treated with the iron in this manner: that is, to have the patient lie on his back between your hands. Then cut the site of the rupture, which is hardened and protruding, with an incision across its width to a depth of three fingers' breadth.
Then grasp the spermatic cords and that which is beneath the skin until such time as you have identified the white spermatic cord which follows it under the skin.
Take an iron rod and place it over the protruding site of the spermatic cord, pressing it deep into the abdomen.
Sew the two protruding sites on the end of the iron rod from the spermatic cord together with a suture; then remove the end of the iron rod.
And do not cut into the alsifach at all, nor stretch the ovum, or anything else, as I have made you aware of during the treatment for an intestinal branch.
Then treat that wound with the treatment of wounds. Therefore, when you cut the filaments, clean it, and restore the wound until it hardens.
For sisach contracts and does not add to the prominence of the rupture.
And the cautery is better in this place, for it is closer to healing.
Chapter 68.
Concerning softening of the skin of the testicles.
The skin of the testicles softens many times in certain men due to which the flesh that is between them also softens, and its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary for him who is asked to treat it that he make the patient lie on his back and cut all of the skin which has softened onto the natural skin.
Then gather the two lips with a suture; and if you wish, sew the excess of the softened skin with three or four sutures, and secure the suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat with the treatment of remaining wounds until it is healed, and the filaments fall off.
Chapter 69.
On castration.
You should know that castration is forbidden by our laws; therefore, I could have omitted it from this book of mine, and there would be no need to mention it except for two reasons.
The first reason is so that it may be within the knowledge of the physician when he is asked about it; and also so that he may know how to treat a person who has undergone such a procedure.
