The second reason is that we often require the castration of certain animals for our own benefit, such as rams, goats, cats, and similar creatures.
I will therefore say that castration is performed in two ways. Either by scraping. Or by incision and cutting.
In the procedure that is done by scraping, the method of operation is as follows: you should make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles soften, relax, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer perceptible to touch.
In the procedure that is done by incision and cutting, you must hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut above each testicle with one incision until you see two testicles; then cut these after scraping both of them, but do not let anything from the scrotum remain except for a thin layer which is over the cord.
And this method of castration is better than the one done by scraping because scraping may leave some of the testicle behind, which will make the animal desire sex.
Then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphroditism, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is made from men in two ways. One of them is because it appears in him what follows space, or on the skin of the testicles, what is between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine runs out of what is on the skin of the testicles.
However, in women there is one type, and it is a woman's vulva over the pubic bone like small male testes entirely, protruding to the outside; of which one is like a man's penis, and two are like two testes.
And the treatment of these three types, two from men, and one type from women, is as follows: because you must cut away the added flesh until the impression of it escapes. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
However, in the second type of men from which urine runs out of what is on the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is it treatable at all.
Chapter 71.
On the cutting of warts, and protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
A wart perhaps is added on top of the natural thing until it becomes ugly and its appearance is unsightly; and sometimes it magnifies in some women so much that it expands like men's, and reaches to intercourse.
You should therefore hold the excess of the tumor with your hand or with a hook and cut it off; and do not stop cutting, especially at the deep root; so that there is no flow of blood.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
However, flesh arising is the flesh that grows from the opening of the womb until it fills itself; and perhaps it exits to the outside resembling a tail; and for this reason some ancient physicians call the illness 'tailed'. We should therefore cut it like we cut the tumor, and treat it until it is healed.
On the treatment of alratica, which is an unpenetrated wound of a woman.
Chapter 72.
Alratica is when there is an unpenetrated wound of a woman or a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental alratica arises from an earlier illness; and it occurs either due to added flesh born of the flesh or a thin membrane or thick membrane; and it can be in the depths of the womb or its sides or at its top or bottom; and it prevents intercourse, impregnation, childbirth, and perhaps menstruation.
Its diagnosis is made by the midwife's inspection, if the obstruction appears nearby.
If it is not apparent, then search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from a thin membrane and it's near the two lips, then hasten to tear it apart.
And it is necessary to place some gauze on the two lips.
Then put the index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two lips with force until they tear open delicately, revealing the orifice.
Then take some wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the wound; have her come every day so that the wound does not heal with any other matter.
If the orifice is thick and dense, then it must be cut out with a spatula that is wide like a myrtle leaf.
If the growth is fleshy in nature, then suspend it with hooks and cut it out; have ready some medicines which will stop any flow of blood without cauterization such as gum acacia and dragon's blood mixed with egg white.
Then insert a lead tube of adequate size so that the wound does not heal too quickly; keep it in place for some days and apply a linen plug afterwards, then treat any remainder with healing remedies until it is fully healed.
And when there is a birth of other flesh-like growths inside the womb, they must be cut out like this; for while an abscess or cancerous lump inside the womb are not treated with iron at all, it would be counterproductive to do so.
Chapter 73.
On the treatment of haemorrhoids, itchy bumps, red moles on women's bodies.
Haemorrhoids are indeed an opening of the veins which causes much blood to flow out from them regularly.
When hemorrhoids are burned, they become hardened; and I have already spoken about their types and signs in the division of them, and here I will speak about which ones receive a cure and which do not.
I therefore say that hemorrhoids which are inside the uterus opening and do not show externally have no cure with a knife.
Those hemorrhoids which are inside the uterus opening but also felt externally - these are the ones that can be cured.
Therefore, it is necessary to put the woman into a cold house. Then stretch out the hemorrhoids with tweezers or a rough cloth, and cut them off from their roots.
Then sprinkle on them at the time of blood flow from one powder of these cut ones without any mixing - acacia, dragon's blood, frankincense and similar things.
Then press her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterward, put her into cold water.
If the blood flow persists then have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate rinds and galls and similar things.
Then take barley flour and mix it with honey and vinegar to make a plaster from it on her back.
If the blood stops with this then it is good; if not, apply cupping glasses to her back and breasts without making any cuts.
Then take wool and dip it into the juice of thapsia or eelgrass juice or the juice of alant roots (that is, greater meadow-rue), Pontic wine and bind it on with linen.
When the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments until it heals.
Bartholomew is red like the heads of garlic, and he is of a rough appearance.
It is necessary then that you cut away what appears from it, as I said earlier equally on both sides; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 74.
On the perforation of the womb which happens in childbirth.
There are many kinds of abscesses which occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcer, and scabby sores, and lekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulae, and haemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and telar, and hot abscess.
And I have already spoken of all these diseases and their kinds, and their signs, and their treatments in different chapters; it is necessary that I speak in this treatise about the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is one of those abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening with an iron is performed.
It is necessary therefore that you pay attention. Then if the pain of the abscess in its onset is sharp with a throbbing sensation, and heat, and fever; and you see it turn red; if feeling fails over it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and treat it with that which assists towards maturation, until such time as the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
Then you should make the woman sit on a seat which is wet with two cushions, and recline, or be propped up over her back, and gather her feet towards the lower part of her belly; and let her hips be expanded, and place her arms under her knees, and bind each one with a suitable bandage to it.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side and use the tool to open the womb's opening.
And when you wish to use this tool, you must insert the rod deep into the woman's womb so that what enters from the tool does not exceed the depth of the womb and harm the patient.
But if the tool is longer than the depth, then you must place markers on the shaft to indicate the womb's depth so that the tool does not penetrate further and harm the patient. The assistant should turn the handle which runs along the tool from above, holding it steady, and turning until the womb opens.
When the head appears and is felt by hand, being soft and gentle, you must cut it at its top with a spatula an inch wide.
When pus is drained, you must place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil with some styptic quality and apply it deep inside the womb.
And place outside over the womb and over the pubic hair clean wool soaked in water boiled with apples.
Then you must treat it for two days with an ointment suitable to the wound until it is healed.
And the womb and wound are sometimes washed so that they are irrigated with water and honey; then with water boiled with lily root or long aristolochya; after this return to ointment.
But if the wound is hidden inside the womb, you must refrain from treating it with an iron tool, instead treat it as said above.
Chapter 70.
The teachings on midwifery, how living embryos are treated when born with an unnatural shape.
It is necessary for midwives to first understand the shape of a normal birth.
From these signs it can be recognised that when you see a woman pushing downwards and wishing to take in air, and what is above her relaxes during the birth, and there is urgency about the exit of the embryo: then you should know that this birth is taking place according to its natural shape, and its exit will be over its head, and the afterbirth with it either suspended or hanging from the umbilicus.
When you see these signs, it is necessary that her abdomen be pushed, so the embryo descends quickly.
For when it has descended on its head, the afterbirth descends with it, and is cleansed of those superfluities by a good cleansing.
All other forms of birth besides this one are unnatural and unworthy.
Sometimes a fetus exits on its feet, with its body coming out before its head and its feet or hands first, sometimes one foot; sometimes the baby's head comes out with its hands; sometimes it is the breech which emerges: perhaps the neck turns; all these unnatural shapes are unworthy.
Therefore it is necessary that the midwife be prudent and subtle with regard to all these matters, take care not to err or be deceived; for I am showing each shape and form, how they are composed of parts, and indicating what they mean, so they can stand on their own.
When the fetus exits headfirst in its natural delivery and it becomes intense for the woman and difficult for her to give birth, and you see her strength already debilitated: then have her sit on a chair, or women hold her; and vapor from a woolen cloth with fenugreek decoction, and with moist oils.
Then the midwife should insert between her fingers a small spatula and tear with it the perineum, cutting it with her nail until what is inside it runs out of moisture; then press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman must be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her to be pressed after the enema; make her sneeze with a powder puff and hold her mouth and nose at once. The fetus will exit immediately.
If two of the fetus's hands exit first, it is necessary that you pull them back with ease gradually. If it does not obey upon entry, then place the woman on a chair and lift her feet upwards; then shake the chair on the ground; and the woman should be held so as not to fall with the shaking.
If the hands do not exit and the fetus dies; then cut them and extract its remainder; tie its hand with a cloth and pull it out evenly; for he will exit.
The exit of the embryo feet-first. When the fetus exits feet-first, then it is necessary to push its feet upwards; afterwards turn it gradually and then level it out.
He should then take one of his feet and stretch it gradually.
When they [the splinters] therefore come out as far as the thighs, then tell him to press it and make him sneeze with pepper: it will come out if God wills it.
And if it does not come out with what we have described, then draw the splinters gradually until you put them in their natural position. For it will come out easily itself at that time.
But if it is not possible with all that we have described: then take from the mucilage of the Althaea plant, and oil of sesame, and dissolved gum: and pound everything in a mortar with good force: then anoint with it a woman's woolen cloth, and apply to his lower abdomen. Then make him sit in warm water until he sweats profusely from the armpits.
When therefore you see that his lower parts have become warm, then give him a drink of myrrh: and place it [the poultice] on top. When therefore he has held the drink for one hour, then make him sit on a seat. Then make him sneeze: and squeeze his mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the abdomen with gentle pressure. For the splinters will come out immediately.
The outcome of the splinters is on his knees and hands. When it comes out according to this form, then insert your hands gradually into his body. Then the woman should lie down on her neck, and let her feet hang towards the lower parts; she herself is to be lying on a bed with her hands folded. Then press gradually upon her abdomen. Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows until she is ready in the lower part. If the fetus does not exit, then take her feet and move them with a strong motion. Then press gradually upon what is above the hypocondrium until the fetus rises to the upper parts. Then he should insert his hands and guide the fetus gradually; tell the woman to push until the fetus exits.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. It is necessary to manipulate her hand for its reduction. If however he cannot reduce it then lift the woman and make her walk.
If she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed; shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then apply the ointment you have prepared from mucilages.
If she catches hold of it with her labour then make her sit on the seat. Then try to manipulate her hand for its reduction and guide the fetus according to its natural shape; make the woman sneeze and tell her to push until it exits.
The fetus emerges onto its neck, with hands spread apart, and its face is turned towards the mother's back. The midwife must hold the baby's hand, then gradually turn it over, and equalize it with ease.
When the baby is equalized, then place on its head what we have described, made of oil and mucus.
Then ask it to move gradually. When you have done that, then make her sit on a chair and ask her to lean to the right side. Then tell her to bear down, and make her sneeze with force.
The prepared baby emerges onto its side. When the baby descends into the womb in this way with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your nail until it is evacuated of moisture.
If the afterbirth hangs over the baby's face and neck, then cut it from the navel so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the baby into the womb until you place it as required; and tell the woman to bear down several times, for itself will come out of a gentle birth.
The emergence of twins or many babies. You should be aware that twins often occur; and sometimes three or four babies are born. However, this is unusual and rare.
There are five things that exist outside of nature and do not fully live: although they multiply, one amniotic sac contains them; they are separated by the sifach which distinguishes between each of their umbilical cords.
And sometimes they are born as a single fetus with an easy natural birth, or they are born with an unremarkable exit, as we have described.
If some of them are difficult during birth and reach that which we have mentioned regarding unnatural positions, then treat them as we have described; and let there be a skilled midwife, who acts with skill and slowness, and the child will be born according to every position with whatever help is given for their safe delivery.
A reminder of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarried.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and even more up to ten; and I have verified that a woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all fully formed.
This was determined by the measurement of the wise and glorious one.
Thus, all of this ought to be within the knowledge of the obstetrician. Perhaps they will come to resemble it; therefore, let them oppose this with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus as described in the section on medicinal treatment and it does not come out, and you are forced to use manual manipulation, then you must be mindful.
If the woman is of sound virtue and has not been afflicted by any conditions that may cause her harm, then she should lie on her back on the bed with her head inclined downward and legs raised; two assistants must hold her in place or strap her to the bed so that during delivery her body does not slip out.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils mixed with marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these oils and mixtures, then easily insert it into the womb. Then she should ask the woman for the place where to hook the hooks from the fetus and convenient places to do so.
Consider then if the fetus is ready to be delivered head first: hook the hooks into its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or beneath the chin, or in the fork of its neck, or near the ribs and under the hips.
If however its descent is by foot first then hook into the pubic bone and middle of its back.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand and place its curve between the fingers of her left hand; then insert this easily and hook into one of the places mentioned until reaching an empty space.
Then hook another or even third opposite to the first one if required so that it contracts evenly and does not tilt to one side.
Then it must be pulled with an equal pull and not a straight one but rather one that moves to all sides so as to ease its delivery just like in the case of tooth extraction.
And it is necessary for that which lies between them to soften and lengthen. If a part is retained from it, then the midwife must grease certain fingers with oil and insert them on that side so as to turn with them what was retained.
So if part of the fetus comes out, then change the hooks to another place that is higher: do this until the entire fetus comes out.
If one hand comes out before the other and it is not possible to draw it back due to its contraction, then a cloth must be rolled up over it. Then draw it out until it comes out completely; cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and so you must do with the other hand and both feet.
But if the fetus's head is large or it becomes contracted during exit, or there is a build-up of water in its head, then one must insert between the fingers a thorny spatulum and pierce the head with it to draw out the water; or break open its skull using an instrument called an almizdaac, whose shape appears along with those of other instruments in the chapter that comes after this.
Likewise, if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then one must cut with an algengemat; or draw it out with an almizdaac, as we have said. Then draw the bones out with forceps.
If the head comes out and becomes contracted at the fork, then it must be pierced until all the moisture is drained from its chest.
If it does not form a knot and does not obey attempts to extract it, then cut off the head of the worm with as much tissue as possible.
If there is an abscess or dropsy of the lower belly, you should pierce it until moisture flows out.
If however the fetus is lying on its feet, then traction will be easy and you can direct it to the mouth of the womb with ease.
If however it is trapped at the belly or chest, you should remove it with a cloth wrapped around your hand; tear open the belly or chest until their contents flow out.
If however the rest of its body is delivered but not its head, then a midwife should insert her left hand... [and complete the sentence] If the mouth of the womb is open, then you should put your hand inside to search for its head with your fingers and draw it out until you can see it at the mouth of the womb; after this insert a hook or two hooks and use them to pull it out.
If the mouth of the womb is blocked due to a hot abscess, you should not work on it; instead give an evacuation of moist and fatty substances, apply poultices and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the fetus is lying on its side, it may be turned; you should do as we have said for a living child.
If this cannot be done, then cut it in pieces and remove it.
You must not leave any part of the placenta inside at all.
Once you have completed your procedure, then apply what we said for hot abscesses that occur in the uterus.
If there is bleeding, then get into astringent waters, and undergo the rest of the treatment.
Indeed, I have seen a woman whose belly contained a dead fetus; then she became pregnant again with another child, but that one also died afterwards. So after some time she developed an abscess at her navel, which swelled until it burst open and started discharging pus.
I was called to treat her, and I administered long-term medication; but the wound did not heal.
So I applied on it some of the strongest drawing plasters, and a bone came out from its place. Then after several days another bone emerged. I was amazed by this because there is no bone inside the belly.
So I thought that these were from the bones of the dead fetus.
I examined the wound and extracted many bones while the woman was in better condition; she has now lived like this for a long time and discharges hardly any pus from that place anymore.
And I do not assert this as something strange, except that there is knowledge and help in it for the skilled physician who treats with his own hand.
Chapter 77.
On the shapes of instruments required for extracting a fetus.
The shape of the pivot which opens the womb.
Here below is the form of the forceps, which has two handles that are equal in length and each have a pivot at both ends of two pieces of wood. However, this pivot should be thinner than the forceps and it can be made either from ebony or boxwood.
The width of each piece of wood should be approximately two fingers wide and the edge of both is about one finger thick; the length of each should be a palm and a half long. At the centre of these two pieces of wood are attached two extensions made from the same type of wood, which have already been fixed into place on either side. These extensions are each a palm and a little more in length with a width that is around two fingers or slightly wider.
These two extensions are inserted into the womb's opening so that it opens with them when you turn both pivots.
Another similar shape, but thinner and lighter.
It should be made from ebony or boxwood following the pattern of forceps. However at the end of it there are two extensions as you can see above and each extension's length is a palm; its width is two fingers.
So when you wish to open the womb with this device then make the woman sit on a bed with her feet hanging down and her legs parted. Then insert these two joined additions into the womb: and you holding the end of the lower instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, evenly according to the amount of opening of the womb that you wish, until the Midwife does what she wants.
The other form of vertigo that the ancients called the Form of the Pushing Force by which the fetus is pushed.
The Form of Almisdac, by which the large head is crushed: and it resembles forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is made oblong, like forceps in this form, as you see, which have teeth like a saw's, by which it is cut and crushed.
The Form of Vuncini having one spine.
The Form of Vuncini having two spines.
The Form of Spatumilis having two ends for cutting off the head.
The Form of the two broad Spatumilia.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types multiply, and the more they are prepared by the artisan, the quicker he is at his operation, and the higher in esteem with people because of their number.
Therefore do not despise any of these instruments lest it be unprepared for you. Their need for it is indeed inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
Regarding the extraction of the Secondina nerve.
When the Secondina nerve is retained during childbirth, you should instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with powder; hold your hand over her mouth and nose. If it therefore comes out by this method, that is good.
If not, take a pot and make a hole in the lid; put into it herbs that open the womb, such as galbanum, rue, dill, chamomile, sea-wormwood, wood aloe, and centaury: Take all these herbs or some of them and soak them with water, then place them over fire.
Then place a reed pip over the hole of the pot lid and another end into the womb; let her hold it until the steam reaches the depth of the womb.
Then make her sneeze as we have described. The Secondina nerve will come out with ease.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in oil of turpentine or marshmallow mucilage. Then she should insert it into the vulva and search for the Secondina nerve with it. Once she has found it, she should grasp it and gradually draw it out.
If it is attached deep within the womb, then insert your hand as we have described until you find the second membrane and extract it gradually following its direction so that the womb does not fall due to a strong pull; rather, it should be moved with ease to the right and left sides; then apply the amount of traction required. It then obeys (your instructions), is preserved, and comes out from any adhesions.
If the os of the womb is contracted, as we have already described, treat it with sneezing and with an ointment made from herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself by repeating the extraction process.
But whatever comes out at the os of the womb from it, you should tie to the woman's thigh. Then enema her with the four-fold ointment. For it will indeed cause her to rot after days, and be dissolved, and come out.
However, when it rots, a bad smell is raised from it to the stomach and head, and harms the patient. Therefore, appropriate fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced the fumigation of watercress and dried figs.
Make an instrument with which to fumigate the woman in cases of retained secundine and menses.
Make it from a glass similar to an alcama or from zere: and place the wide end over the fire: and place the fumigation on top of coals: hold it until that fumigation is consumed: and do another one.
Chapter 79.
On the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which has already been blocked by a very fine membrane. It is necessary therefore for the midwife to pierce this membrane with her finger; and if she cannot, let her pierce it with a sharp probe, being careful not to wound the muscles underneath.
Then place over it wool dipped in wine and oil. Treat it afterwards with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will become blocked again, then place a leaden tube in the orifice for many days and remove it when the child is able to void urine.
And sometimes obstruction of the anus occurs due to healing of wounds or abscesses. It is necessary therefore to cut that healing, then treat it with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter 80.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur on the lower part (of the body).
The fistulas which occur on the lower part are nodules and lumps that appear outside the anus or on one side of it; there may be a single fistula or several.
When that nodule becomes old, it opens up and runs out of it a white watery fluid or thin pus.
And sometimes there are penetrating fistulas which come from these (nodules). Thus any discharge that comes from a wound is known by its penetration, and also by the presence of wind, when the patient uses an emetic; and perhaps worms will come out with it.
And there are fistulas among them when they are in the space penetrating up to the bladder and up to the urethra. And some penetrate as far as the hip joint and as far as the tailbone.
But from that by which a penetrating fistula as far as the anus is known, there is also one which does not penetrate; you should insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when it has no twists.
If there is a twist in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe or a hair from a horse's tail until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it at the bottom and nothing comes out of the orifice by way of discharge, nor is there any wind, nor worms as I said, then you know that it does not penetrate; so proceed to its cure.
And when a fistula is penetrating up to the bladder or up to the urethra, its indication is the discharge of urine from it and the prevention of that place being consolidated with medicines.
If, however, it is penetrating up to the hip joint or up to the bone of the hip, then its sign is that the probe reaches as far as there if there is no twist in it down to the bone; and the finding of pain around the part, and that pus always comes out of it: and that medication does not take effect in it nor is it consolidated with ointment.
There is no cure whatsoever for all penetrating fistulas; and their treatment is a waste of effort for the foolish doctor on whom it is performed.
In cases where the fistula is not penetrating and not old, healing can be expected with the knife; as I tell you, and I have proven it myself: and it requires that the patient lie on their back between your hands; and raise their legs above their hips, and let their hips be inclined towards their abdomen. Then insert a lead or brass probe into it until you know where the probe has reached.
However, if the patient feels it near their anus, then you must insert your index finger into their anus.
If therefore you feel that the probe has already penetrated what you have detected with your finger, beyond which you feel neither sinew nor flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; do not labour over it because there is no cure for it as we have said.
Some say that flesh can be healed and from the medication by which benefit is expected, it requires a fine cauterium fire to be made according to the width of the fistula as mentioned before, and insert it burning into the fistula until it reaches near the anus. Then repeat this twice or three times until you know that all the added flesh near the fistula has been cauterised which is like a bird's cane.
Then treat it with linen bandages smeared in butter until the cauterised flesh comes out; then dress it with ointments that produce and strengthen flesh.
If it heals, then it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with any other treatment.
And if the abscess breaks out and does not reach your finger which is inside the anus, but there remains between it and the abscess a thick layer of flesh, or cauterize: and you see that there is a fistula in what follows along the surface of the skin, then cut away the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and as you go on cutting with the knife inside the fistula until you reach where the end of the abscess comes out, and the abscess will be released and fall off.
Then cleanse those flesh fragments which resemble the tubes of a feather so that nothing remains from them.
But if blood overpowers you and prevents you from performing the cut due to your own actions or those of another person, then it is better for you and more helpful in achieving a quicker recovery that you cauterize it with fire, or an acrid medicine.
For the fire-cauterization joins good properties: the charring of those additional flesh fragments and the cutting off of blood supply, as well as drying up moisture.
Then treat it with bandages wrapped in butter, or chopped-up cabbage leaves until the place emits pus and those charred flesh fragments come out into the pus.
Then treat the wound with healing ointments that form hard flesh; and these are medicines which have styptic as well as drying properties.
For once they are restored, then it is already healed; and you need not fear repetition [of the treatment].
But if the fistula has already reached down deep into the anus and stretches out from there due to its excessive length: then put your finger inside the anus, and feel around with it for [the fistula].
If you therefore feel a tumor and it is between you and the incision, and it consists of skin or flesh, and it is close to being penetrated; then there is no operation on it except by way of confidence and hope.
This is because one of three methods is administered on it: either you cauterize it, as we said; or you cut it away until you reach its concavity so that it is possible for you to introduce licinia and nearby treatment. And do not proceed too far with the cutting so as not to cut into the sphincter containing the anus, and it happens on the weak excretion outlet without volition. Then treat it with what we have said.
Perhaps it will indeed heal; or you will remove the tumor and clean it after cutting it close to the anus as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse the filtered flesh which is in the incision as far as you are able with scrutiny.
Then attempt the restoration of the whole wound with its incision and upper orifice where it has healed; and a fistula remains open on the interior part of the anus, and will be lighter for the weak person.
And sometimes a fistula follows in this way: that is, when you introduce a probe into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and margin of the anus; then take a pointed probe which is like a needle for removing splinters, that is, preparing coria, in this shape. And introduce into it a thread wound from five threads, and about that amount. Then insert the probe with a thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it penetrates inside the margin of the anus near to it, then pull out the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus and extract the end of the thread, and unite both ends together: and tie up each one, and leave it for one or two days. The more the thread cuts into the flesh, bind it well until those parts which are between the two ends of the thread drop off; then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate by any means that presents itself to you, unless it is very deep. For it does not behove you to make it for the sake of the arm in order that you may avoid cutting into it.
Then do with it as we have said until it is healed.
Make a spatula with spines from what the fistula is split in two; its curve being very acute and one part not acute so that it does not cut into that which is unnecessary, as you see.
Head 81.
Concerning the stitching of hemorrhoids from which blood is discharged when they are cut open: and treatment for fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types. Or they exist inside the anus either like bladders of coals or plums; or as if they were grains themselves. And among them some are small and others large; and blood is discharged from them always either singly or many at a time. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are mostly of little moisture, from which runs yellow water or a steady flow of blood; and they are according to the colour of the body.
And the cure for that which is inside the anus is to advise the patient to empty their bowels; and it should be expressed until it exits the anus, and you see the hemorrhoids appear. Therefore, also lift them with hooks; or hold them with your nail. Then cut them at their roots.
But if they are not retained by the hooks, because of their moisture and softening; then take them with a rough cloth, or with a cord made from double-twisted wool, and extract them with your fingers. Then cut them, and sprinkle over them after the cutting some hot powders, so that they stay in place like a caustic. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then care for them with the remaining treatment until they heal.
But if the anus does not obey by exiting, then give the patient an enema with a small bite, so that it washes what is inside the anus, and obeys quickly to exit when the patient empties themselves.
However, if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their condition is light and easy; and it is such that you should take them with your nail, or take them with a hook, and cut them. Treat them with what we have discussed until they are healed.
And for those who fear the incision of iron, you must administer their suture in this way. Take the rolled thread, and insert it into the needle. Then draw the needle to the upper part and make it penetrate with the needle into the root from its other side; and wrap the two ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle so they are perpendicular to each other, and tighten the needle strongly with a stitch. Then tie off the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the sutures, leaving one out of each; do not draw them tight so that excess blood may flow from it.
Then place over the wound a cloth soaked in rose oil or caustic oil, and put it over the wound; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When the sutures fall off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the cure until they are healed.
On the treatment of fissures. They often happen from drying out of faeces and natural constriction.
When they are dried out and medicine does not help them; then you must scrape with the point of a spatula or your nail until they become moist, and remove from them the upper layer which prevents them from healing. Then treat them until they heal as is appropriate.
If they do not become firm, repeat the procedure with more force than before until they bleed profusely and then treat them until they heal.
On the treatment of Almirmar for inverted and non-inverted conditions, and on Athelil for dry and moist wounds.
Chapter 82.
You should know that Almirmar is nothing other than a round knot of the same colour as the body, similar to the head of a nail; it occurs all over the body but particularly on the lower parts of the feet and toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is therefore necessary to cut what is around the nail-head and hold it with forceps or a hook, then pull it out by its root.
Then treat it, and if you wish to cauterise it as explained previously in its own chapter.
One type of Athelil in dry wounds is a small, rough, hardened, rounded bump on the surface of the body. And it often occurs on the hands of children.
To treat it, you should stretch out the Athelil and cut into it; or bind it with silk thread, or with a hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterise it with fire or with a caustic medicine as mentioned previously; and then remove the inverted and non-inverted Athelil with the instrument I will describe.
A Formica is also a small hardened bump on the surface of the body that goes very deep; and when it encounters cold, there is a sensation like an ant bite in it, and they appear all over the body but especially on the hands.
And its treatment is to take a small tube made from a goose quill, or the strong feather of a vulture; and place that tube over an ant until it fills the circle of the tube with its parts. Then turn your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the ant; and place it deep into the flesh, thus uprooting the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do this with an air tube or iron tube, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the tube be sharpened to a point, which will draw out the deaf person's earwax; easing its grip on their finger and its own turning.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant as has been mentioned previously regarding cauterization of a boil; see Chapter on Cauterization.
Chapter 83.
On the shapes of instruments which are administered for enemas in illnesses of the anus, and for relieving the belly, and colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or brass, or cast bronze, or sheet copper.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small or large, depending on those to whom they are administered.
That which is administered for the treatment of small children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak or painful, their enemas likewise.
Enema, large.
Its tube - that is the upper end to which is tied a bladder - should be wide according to this pattern; and it should have a mark showing how to tie the bladder as you see here; its lower end which is inserted into the anus should be tapered, that is not open but smooth, very soft; on one side of it are two holes, and on the other side there is just one hole.
The size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the spokes, slightly larger and into it place the medicines from an animal's bladder or from a sheep's paper which is shaped like a small asofti whose base is one palm in diameter.
And it is pierced with many holes as it revolves, and there should be the space between each hole equal to the thickness of a finger. Then introduce into those holes a strong raw thread, which is made of ten threads or thereabouts, and attach to it the bag like an asofti's. When therefore the bag is placed on it, bind it at the head of the enema tube between the two distinguishing marks with a strong tightening. Then administer an enema with it.
And this is the circular form of the bag depicted here, along with its holes.
The quality of administering the enema with it is that you fill the bag or bladder with medicine which has been heated according to the tolerance of the patient's condition; and be careful not to use very hot nor very cold [medicine].
Then let the patient lie on his back and raise his legs upwards; and place under him an inthan, that is, a leather hide upon which people sit, or a cloth.
Then anoint the end of the enema tube with oil or sheep's tallow, or with linseed mucilage or honey. Then introduce it into the anus gently. Then the attendant should take up the bag with both hands together and squeeze it forcefully until all that is in the enema tube has been evacuated into the intestine.
Then extract the enema and raise the patient's legs to the wall.
If medicine makes him feel deathly ill then he should hold on as much as possible. And if they sleep through the whole night with it then it is even more helpful.
But if you enema them due to abdominal pain, and their death is hastened as a result, then repeat the enema until they are cured.
If there are haemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus then a very gentle and mild enema should be given, using those types of enemas with which children are given enemas.
And let this be the form of a gentle enema for when there is tight skin.
Chapter 84.
I have already spoken about the division of wound treatment in a previous chapter and I will now explain here their treatment by hand and with medicine according to the completion.
I therefore say that wounds are divided according to the means by which they are inflicted and also according to the place on which the wound is received.
The means by which wounds are inflicted are therefore many and various as for instance a blow from a stone or the stroke of a sword or knife or a thrust with a lance or wooden staff or arrow; there are numerous similar cases.
The places on the body where wounds are received also differ; for instance a wound to the head or neck chest abdomen flank and similar parts of other limbs.
And I am describing the cures for certain wounds, so that I may set out this reasoning and rule for other wounds.
And I shall start with simple head wounds, since the description of compound fractures will come at the beginning of Chapter Three.
I say therefore. When there is a simple wound to the head and no bone fracture occurs: consider then if it was caused by a blow from a stone or similar object, and has already broken the skin only; and it is a large wound, and you fear an adverse outcome of warm abscesses: then proceed quickly, and perform an immediate cephalic phlebotomy: and do not delay it: and draw off some blood according to the extent of his strength: if there is nothing preventing him from undergoing phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing out of the wound is small in amount. And let the phlebotomy be performed on the opposite side from the wounded area.
And let the phlebotomy be performed on the opposite side from the wounded area. And let the patient beware of over-eating, wine, and heavy foods.
If anything prevents him from having reduced amounts of food, then let his diet be further diminished, and let him avoid meats or wines.
And if an abscess occurs on the wound, place upon it a poultice soaked in rose oil alone or with wine that has some astringency to it.
If you are confident there will be no warm abscess, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not yet changed it, some powder which I shall describe shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then apply certain ointments until it heals.
If the wound is large, or it is caused by an incision of a sword and similar weapons: and its lips do not join together with the help of poultices, then you must bring them together with stitches, as I will explain in relation to abdominal wounds regarding their suturing.
If a wound has been detected near the bone and it is hanging loose with no retention except for a small bandage: then cut it open. After that treat the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh in place of skin there; and tell the patient to eat foods which have some weight/substance such as heads (of animals), cooked wheat until it is soft like porridge and similar things.
If you find putrefaction in the skin and it is not attached to bone: then cut away all that is putrefied and throw it out; afterwards treat it.
If there is an artery or a vein from which blood continues to flow in these wounds and it is not stopped by medicines; then make an incision above the artery. If you find that it is not cut then cut it with a sharp knife or ligate (tie off) it. If the need arises such that what I have said is not applicable then cauterize it until the blood stops flowing.
If these wounds are small and simple then their treatment is easy; so that you sprinkle this powder on them: since it hardens them before the air changes/alters them.
Recipe of Flesh Powder (pulveris incarnati) Take one part frankincense (olibanum), two parts dragon's blood and three parts unslaked lime; grind all of this very well and sift it: fill the wound with it and bind it tightly so that it adheres to it well and coagulates together with the blood.
Then compress it from above with swabs and leave it be, without removing until such time as it shall not fall off. See that the medication is not removed from him before he is healed.
And sometimes lime alone will do this, when you have no frankincense on hand, and even dragon's blood performs the same operation in many large wounds, where it is appropriate to place them on top of these and compress them; they are fresh wounds with their own blood that has not been altered by air.
But if it has already been altered by air in some way, especially when this is at a time of year that is summery, then the intention of this powder is not in place: rather take care of it with what we have said, so as to put some digesting ointments on top; or lay poultices made from barley flour mixed with water and honey until they discharge pus. Then take care of the remainder with healing until it is healed.
If however a fracture occurs with the wound in bone and is small, then extract it with gesti: and I shall now speak of the healing of fractures to the head at some point in the future.
On wounds of the neck:
If however a wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference between it and the treatment of a simple head wound.
If however the neck nerve has been cut through or an artery: then if it is a nerve, there lies no skill in its healing apart from to be deprived of that which causes violent constriction, such as Zinnia and Zea mays, and their like. Since they harm nerves and contract them.
And do not place anything cold whatsoever on the wound: for the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is superior to other body parts; let it be that by which it is healed from among medicines, gentle, such as lime washed with sweet water several times infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tutia, when it is also washed with sweet water: and the remaining mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then apply a suture; or contract its lips with threads. Then treat it until it heals.
But if there is depth to the wound, or if a cavity has formed within its lower part, where pus has already gathered, then pierce it at a more depressed place on it.
If an artery has been cut within the wound and its flow of blood has been lost; then separate it or tie it off, and bind it; or cauterize it if necessity provokes you to do so.
But if now the windpipe has been split or entirely severed and the larynx saved; then bring together the lips of the skin with a suture over the windpipe, and do not touch the windpipe itself, but rather set it equal to its former state, and return it to its natural shape. Then bind it with an appropriate bandage and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments that have an astringent quality or those which dry out the wound, until it is healed.
If the wound has already made an impression in the bone, then probe it; if there are fragments of bone inside, extract all of them or as many as you can; and what cannot be removed, leave it until the wound becomes gangrenous, separates, and its discharge is alleviated.
If the wound is fresh, then sprinkle over it the powder I described. If however you see bone in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with what was mentioned in its own chapter.
On wounds in spatulates.
If there is a blow from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider it. If breath escapes from the wound when the patient gasps, then know that the wound is mortal.
If it is not deep and is fresh with its blood, then do not apply any powder to it at first, nor bind it tight so that the blood is not retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient; but put on it an attracting ointment, and loosen it twice a day.
If however the ointment is not available to you, then put on the wound's orifice an old cloth so that it sucks out what comes from it of humours; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what gathers in it runs off.
If however three days or more have passed since the wound and no spasm, nor evil trembling of the heart, nor tightness in gasping has occurred to the patient, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is saved. Take care of it yourself with lint dressings and other treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is neglected, and it keeps opening up, then know that a fistula has formed. Therefore treat her according to the chapter on female disorders.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back then treat it with what has been described before regarding stitching if it is large, or with dust if it is small.
But if it has already made an imprint on the bone and cut off a piece from it then probe the wound and hasten to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated then leave them until the wound becomes gangrenous because their extraction would be eased.
The judgement on the treatment of other accidental wounds in the remaining body parts is the same as what we have said before.
On abdominal wounds, and intestinal protrusions, and their treatment:
A rupture of the abdomen is great, medium or small.
Therefore sometimes from the great [rupture] an intestine emerges or several intestines emerge.
Why does this happen? Their introduction is difficult as is their stitching.
And the small rupture also occurs in another manner. It follows therefore that their reduction is made more difficult if you do not hasten to introduce them back into place immediately because they become inflated, opened and enlarged so their entry becomes hard.
Therefore, a medial disruption is better for ruptures because the reduction of the intestine is not difficult like it is with these two types.
Yes, I am aware that there are four methods of suture for veins.
Two common sutures are suitable for sewing the belly and other wounds of the body, and two other special sutures are specific to other types of wound. However, they are more specific for sewing the belly, and I will explain each of them to you with an explanation and a description.
I say therefore that when a wound is small and some of the intestines come out but it is difficult to put them back in, then one of two methods should be used. Either because of the small size of the rupture, as we have said, or because there is a swelling in the intestine due to cold air.
When this is the case, you should warm it up by submerging a sponge or a damp cloth in warm water alone. Or you could boil it in water with squills, rushes, barley grains, and embrocation until the swelling goes down.
Sometimes wine with an astringent effect will do this, and it is better than water alone at reducing the swelling.
Once you see that the swelling has gone down, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or malva have been boiled. As it soothes its entrance with a small amount of this preparation.
If, however, his recovery is impeded after this treatment, then seek a little more sharpness in the instrument with which you cut the haemorrhoids.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared than cutting; and there is no compulsion to cut after the need and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be the single curved sharp part, and let its other part not be sharp, and let its delicate tip not have the fineness of a scalpel, but rather be slightly more blunt; and it is that which resembles chalk, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged and it enters the intestine, then it should be reduced back to its natural form in its proper place on itself if possible over that; for it is better.
When, however, the rupture is wide in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on their back and put their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then put their head and chest higher than their lower parts.
And likewise if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then direct your attention and consider always that the part where the wound is should be higher than the other part.
And thus you ought to administer this for large wounds as well as medium-sized ones.
For small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then ensure that there is present between your hands a pious assistant who holds the entire rupture with his hand and brings its lips together. Then remove from it for him who follows a piece after a piece: and this is the way of single suture.
And it is, to take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound. Then withdraw from the edge of the wound a length equal to that of the ring finger of the physician; and fix one needle apart so as to introduce into it thread at both ends of the skin, with two ends of the suture which is under the skin on the inside until you have made it pierce through to the other side, then already will you have brought together the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the suture: and there are four threads.
Then tighten with double thread around the circumference of the needle several times from both sides at once until the two lips of the wound are brought together by a suitable arrangement.
Then withdraw the thickness of your finger as well, and fix into them another needle; afterwards straighten it with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not stop doing this with as many needles as you need until you have completed the binding of the whole wound.
And the needles should be medium between thickness and thinness; since thin needles very quickly pierce flesh and thick ones are difficult to introduce into skin; for this reason it is necessary that they should be of a medium size as regards both thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary to fix the needles in two edges of the skin close together so that flesh may not be quickly pierced and the wound opened up before it has healed; nor do they stretch out with the suture so as to hinder the wound from healing.
Then cut off the points of the needles so that they may not hurt the patient while he sleeps; and put over them little pads made of linen on every side which keep the ends of the needles in place: and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
This particular type of suture with needles is more suitable for small wounds. Since one needle or two or approximately that amount is sufficient for the suture of these (wounds).
However, the second type of suture is common, where you gather together with sutures four edges: two edges of skin and two edges of subcutaneous tissue, using a needle that has a thread wrapped around it evenly in fineness and thickness.
Then, when you penetrate with the needle these four edges, bring back the needle from the side where you started it so that the thread forms a loop on top of the wound; this creates a suture similar to the way cloths are tied together by string and place between each part of the suture a small finger's width.
This type of suture is known to people.
And with this type of suture, I closed the wound of a man who had been wounded with a knife in his belly; it was a large gash about one palm length and there were two palms' worth of intestines protruding from the middle intestine and the tear was located right in the center of his abdomen.
I replaced that after his intestines had been hanging outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
The wound therefore healed within about fifteen days; I treated him and he recovered, living many more years after that, going about his own affairs.
And the doctors had judged that it would not heal.
And it was by miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment or medicine could be found. I therefore placed an old poultice on the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water until it was healed.
I shall now describe two types of stitches that are specific to this method, according to the text.
And it begins with an acute stitch: you insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
When the needle has penetrated through the skin and is entering the flesh along the length and breadth of the entire body: leave one end on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it emerge at the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of mirach.
Once you have made it emerge, then make it pass through again at this same mirach end from the outside to the inside: and leave the sifac end that is on this side, and make the needle emerge at the other end from the inside to the outside: and make it go all the way in as you did with the mirach end that is on its part until it goes right through.
Then start again from this same side, and suture it with the end that is from sifac on the other side, and pull out the needle from the skin where it went through.
Then bring it back to that skin, and suture the sifac end which is on the other side with this mirach end: and pull it out from the skin that is on its part, and do one after the other, and another: and do it alternately, until you have stitched all of it completely according to this example.
And the essence of this suture is to stitch like that of tanning hides where they stitch skins together: so you must suture one end of sifac from one side with an end of the skin; and leave it on the other side alternately until you have completed stitching them all.
The second type of suture is specific, as Galen said.
His speech is told as if he compares it to the way some doctors practice medicine, so that they bring together every part to its corresponding match in nature: and they apply the tip of sifac to one end, and the other end to the tip of mirach. And it is done as I tell you.
You must pierce the needle into the tip of mirach near you from the outside and make it penetrate to the inside only; and release the two tips of sifac. Then turn the needle, and make it penetrate from outside to inside on both tips of sifac, namely on each side.
Then turn both of them as well, also make each one penetrate from inside to outside on the other two tips of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is a suture which came before the reminder.
This speech is therefore also that of Galen.
And some said so. The suture of the abdomen is corrected with two needles. Which is to introduce into both a single thread; and begin with the introduction of the needle from what is near you, and make it penetrate all the way to the other side, according to the suture of those who are equally skinned.
And you should know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen: then its suture is more difficult than that of other parts of the abdomen.
The judgement of a wound's treatment is the judgement of other wounds.
Which is because when you treat a fresh wound with its blood, before it is altered by the air; and you clean the intestines, and sew it up, and make it suitable: then place it on incarnantium powder.
If the air has already altered the wound, then apply some ointments that are available to you until they form pus; and the threads fall out, and the sifac and mirach are consolidated. Then treat it as you would other wounds until they heal.
If however you do not have the medicines available, then place an old rag on it immediately as soon as it begins to discharge pus; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not require any other treatment in many cases when the wounds are simple.
However if you fear that the wound will spread to main parts of the body by injury, then you must immerse soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil; and place it around the areas which are between the groin and armpits.
If they feel pain, or pricking sensations, or putridity in their belly (for this often happens), then administer an enema of astringent wine which is black and tepid; especially if the rot has already reached into the stomach, and is about to pierce through to their breast.
And note that that which comes from the large intestines is more easily healed; and that which comes from the small intestines is more difficult to heal.
The intestine which is called the jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is because of the multitude of its veins, and the size of them, and because of the delicacy of its body, and the closeness of its nature to nerves.
If however that which proceeds from the wound is fresh cirrhotic tissue, and you pursue it: return it according to your own reduction method, by which you reduce intestines equally.
If however time has passed and it has already turned green or black, then you must tie it with a thread above the spot that has darkened from it, so that there is no bleeding. In cirrhotic tissue there are veins and arteries.
Then cut off what is beyond that ligament and leave the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it; so as to alleviate the traction on you when extracting cirrhotic tissue, and discharging the pus of the wound.
Remembrance of the wound that occurs in the intestine.
When there is a rupture in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible for it to be restored in some people; because I saw one who was wounded in their belly by a lance blow, and the wound was to the right of the stomach, and the wound had become old: and a fistula formed, and there was an excretion and flatulence from it.
I therefore applied treatment until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to probe it until the site had healed and consolidated.
After I saw that the wound was already healed, I feared for my patient that something bad might happen to their abdomen; but nothing of the sort happened to them. And they remained in a better state of health, eating and drinking, having sex, taking baths, and following their regular routines.
And some medical experts have said that when there is a small wound in the intestines, it should be stitched closed in this manner. And you must take ants with large heads. Then gather the two lips of the wound and place one ant with its mouth open on top of the two lips of the wound. When it bites down on them and clamps its jaws shut, cut off its head as it will stick there and not let go.
Then place another ant near the first one, and keep doing this with ants after ants according to the size of the wound.
After that, bring it down, and close the wound. For those heads remain attached inside the intestines until they are eaten away by the intestine itself, and it heals up completely without any harm to the patient.
It is also possible to stitch the intestines together with a thin thread which has been taken from an animal's gut and attached to the needle beforehand.
And you must take one end of this thread from an animal's gut and strip off most of the flesh. Then tie to its end a thin linen thread wound around it. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then bring it back to ruin.
And this way of sewing with ants and intestines is only according to the path of trust and hope.
You know that a wound which is large and extensive, especially if it is on one of the thinner intestines: then there is no skill but deception, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of fistulae, and abscesses.
You know that any wound or abscess which becomes old and antiquated, turns into an ulcer, does not heal, and discharges pus always without abating: it is then universally called a fistula wherever the affected member may be, and we call this 'zuchemen'.
The fistula is in truth a hard, white, filtered knot with which there is no pain; it has the shape of a bird's feather: and because of this some call it 'risati', that is to say, 'feather'.
And there are times when it becomes moist with pus. Perhaps the moisture is discharged at certain times. And sometimes this moisture is large in quantity; and it is small; it is thick, and it is thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division when I spoke of wounds that any ulcer which does not heal and on which flesh is not formed - it is because of one of nine causes.
One reason is either due to a lack of blood in the body, or because of the malignancy of it, or because there is hard flesh between it and its lips, preventing the birth of flesh; or because it is full of dirt and filth, or because an ulcer within itself is putrid, and the material sent to it is of poor quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because the corruption falling in the region is a type of pestilence, or due to the properties in that region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, where diseases are difficult to digest, and nature delays the healing of abscesses; or because there is one bone in it, or a number of bones.
And I have already demonstrated all these causes and their signs, along with the cures and medications for each in the division; and here I must make you aware of the cure for Fistula and Zuken, which is done through surgical procedure with the hand.
Therefore, you should know that Fistula occurs in all parts of the body. For among them are Fistulae that reach major veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine; or the intestine, or the bladder, or any of the ribs; or any of the vertebrae in the back, or any composite joints made up of several parts, like the hand and foot; and among them are those which spread towards the proximity of the principal member and its likenesses.
Which of these Fistulae is in this condition, is among the diseases difficult to heal, and does not receive a cure, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorant work.
But if there is any Fistula whose depth does not reach these places that I have mentioned, and you hope, trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe made from brass or iron, if the fistula runs straight, and examine it with this.
If there is a twist within the fistula, then examine it with a fine lead probe. Because the softness of the metal enables it to bend and conform to twists.
If the fistula has multiple openings, you cannot mark it out with the probe. Flush out each opening with enemas.
For the fluid you use for the enema will drain towards other openings and flow out of them.
Then carefully determine how you can find out whether there is a bone or nerve inside and if the cavity of the fistula is deep or shallow; or if it is a single fistula with multiple openings and stand over all of these, using all your strength; by questioning the patient and observing the signs and causes of the swelling.
When you have stood correctly over all of these, then go forward with confidence to healing; and consider at that time whether the fistula is shallow close by or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein or any of the places I mentioned; then cut it open as previously described and remove what is inside it through filtration such as putrid flesh, which forms around its opening, made up of flesh and cellulite-like matter; heal it until it is cured.
If however the fistula is of great depth and runs straight then you must cut it open as deep down as possible. Then clean it from all its putrid flesh.
Then apply lint wrapped in sharp medicines; and insert them as far as the hollow of the fistula that you have grasped with an iron rod: do this a few times until the caustic medicine corrodes all that remains in the cavity of the fistula from putrefaction.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
If however it does not heal with that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and it is confirmed to you: then expose it, and cut into it as I have described to you; if nothing prevents you from a vein or nerve, or principal limb, as we said.
If therefore you detect bone, and there is something of putrefaction and blackness in it: then scrape it until its putrefaction is entirely removed. Then treat it with something that makes flesh grow in it, until it is healed.
If it does not heal, and emits pus as before; then know that you have not reached the complete removal of putrefaction.
Expose it again therefore, and make an examination of its scraping and cleaning with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it is healed, that is good; and if not, then know that the putrefaction is in a far-off depth of the body which you have not reached. You therefore have no skill in it but to leave it to nature.
If a small bone is corrupted and it is possible for you to do so, extract it with fine scissors that correspond to it.
If there are many bones, then carefully extract them all using your strength and do not leave any behind.
If any of the moistures cause you difficulty and there is no fear or mistake about opening them up: then open those moistures and flesh if they are present there, and free the bones.
If you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then place on the wound something that will cause it to rot: and leave it until those parts around the bones rot away from flesh and moistures.
And ensure that the wound does not heal. And the incision you made during your operation should be kept open. In my opinion, if you are worried then place on it a poultice of ground sulfur and oil that has been steeped in vinegar; or one steeped in Egyptian ointment, or any of the violet-based ointments.
For when those moistures rot as expected, and the bones are separated from them, the removal of said parts will be alleviated along with the extraction of the bones themselves.
But if it is one large bone, such as a shin bone or hip bone and those similar to them, and what has corrupted it is a large part: then scrape it with an ultimate rasp until the blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted in it is a large part and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it must be sawn through and cut away entirely up to where the corruption has spread; then treat it until it heals.
And I tell you this story as an example and a guide for your own surgical practice.
There was a young man around thirty years of age who experienced pain in his leg due to an internal cause, originating from within the body, until it reached his leg and developed into a large abscess; there was no external cause.
So time passed with the man suffering from medical error until the abscess opened up and a lot of pus came out. And his healing was delayed until his leg became fistulated and developed many openings which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
A lot of doctors tried to treat him for about two years but none were skilled in the surgical arts until I was called upon and saw his leg with the great flow of pus issuing from those openings; even now his whole body had wasted away and his complexion was sallow.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the openings and it reached bone.
Then I examined all the openings and found that they connected with each other from all parts of the leg.
I therefore made an incision and probed over one of those openings until I reached a piece of bone; upon examining it, I found that part to be damaged: already corroded, blackened, rotted, and perforated right through as far as the marrow.
I therefore sawed away what I had detected, preparing the remaining bone.
I appointed that there was no bone except for the corruption which I cut away and extracted. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied healing medicines to consolidate the restoration of the wound over a long period. And it has not consolidated.
Then I tried again, and found the bone broken a second time like the first break; and I cut away what appeared to me to be yet another part of this corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it has not been restored.
Therefore I tried again, and did not cease from cutting the bone in pieces and attempting its restoration; but it was not restored until I had cut away about a span of this bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicine and it consolidated quickly and healed; this repetition of the operation would not have been necessary had it not been for the infirm man's weak condition, his lack of tolerance, and my fear over him lest he die: for in every hour from an excess of evacuation he suffered evil swoonings.
He was therefore completely healed; hard flesh grew instead of the bone, and a good arrangement was made within his body so that his strength returned; he resumed his usual occupations and no injury resulted from this for him while walking.
But if a prominent bone in the body has already been broken then you must extract it like this; take a bandage and bind it at the end of the protruding bone, make someone lift that part upward while you apply another bandage made from thicker wool than the first one. Then tie it above the flesh that is over the bone; and extend its end so as to pull the flesh towards the lower part, and you uncover the flesh over the place where you wish to saw, so that the saw does not hurt (the flesh).
Place a piece of wood or board under the bone below at an angle; for when you do this, it is not prevented that it cuts the corrupted flesh.
And it must be that the saw is above the corrupted place for a short while, so that corruption does not occur in the cavity of the bone; and (the corruption) does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and forces (one) to use another saw in its stead.
If the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, but its part continues with another part; and the corruption is in its middle or on one of its sides: then uncover all the flesh from all sides completely. Then place a piece of wood below.
After that, saw it from the first side where the corruption is until the saw reaches the other side; and let the saw be according to the length of the corruption for a little while, as we said.
If the corruption is at the joint, cut that corrupted joint; then saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
But if the corruption is at the connection of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except abrasion.
If the corruption is at the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, then the matter with it is very difficult. But one must inquire about the corruption in what way does it appear to you? And cauterize it, and clean it as best you can according to how it is positioned, and with whatever skill you have: when neither the Vein nor Nerve opposes you.
And know that there are many knives and saws for cutting these bones, according to the location of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, fineness, size, smallness, hardness, and rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare all the instruments of every kind of operation suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations show you the type of instrument required for it when you have plenty of time. And the knowledge of the ways of this art is a sign of the understanding of illnesses; for then you will already find for yourself what suits each illness from among the instruments.
And I am confirming to you in this chapter's final section the number of instruments that you keep as examples for what you cut, and as a test by which you experiment on others.

And it is not appropriate unless the heads of the joined bones are rubbed with it when they become corrupted, or a large and extensive bone.
