The shape of the surgical instrument suitable for dealing with perforated bones.
Its tip is triangular, of sharp points.
It should be made from Indian steel. And likewise it should be made in the same way for all the surgical instruments, cutting tools, and saws that we have mentioned.
On the cutting of bone ends and the sawing of bones.
The limbs rot, either due to an external cause or an internal one.
When you have treated that corruption with medicinal cure and it does not respond to the treatment, and you see that the corruption spreads into the limb which nothing can save, then you must cut off that limb as far as the spread of corruption reaches so that the patient may emerge from death or a worse disfigurement.
And the sign that this has happened is when the limb turns black until it seems burnt by fire or rotten after turning black; it must be cut off where the rot reaches to prevent its spread throughout the body.
Therefore, prepare for the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures such as the marine scorpion, viper, rutela, and similar animals.
If the corruption or sting is at the tip of a finger, then cut off that finger and do not give space to the corruption so it does not spread into the rest of the hand.
Similarly, if it happens to the hand, then cut off that hand at its root and do not give space to the corruption until it reaches the arm.
And if it invades that part then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
But if the corruption has spread beyond the elbow, then the patient is doomed to death. And treating that besides with curative measures is an exercise in futility.
Similarly, if the corruption invades a toe then cut it off at one of its joints; but if it reaches the instep then amputate the entire foot. If it rises to the knee, then make an incision in the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill [to be employed] but to give up and leave the sick person to die.
The method of making an incision into a limb or sewing it up is to tighten the sinew below where you wish to make the incision; and then tighten another sinew above that place. And the attendant should stretch one of these sinews downwards; and the other attendant should stretch the upper sinew upwards. And you separate the flesh which is between these two sinews with a broad spatule until all of it has been uncovered. Then make your incision or sew it up.
And you should place a piece of linen cloth around all the parts [of the wound] and do not let the needle touch healthy skin; this will otherwise cause added injury to the sick person and hot abscesses.
If there is a gush of blood internally as you are working on it, then cauterise the place quickly; or put some styptical powders upon it. Then return to your medicating until you have completed the task.
Then bind up the wounded limb with suitable bindings, and take care of it until it is healed.
And I tell you that this same accident happened to a man in his foot.
That is because his foot was afflicted by a blackening like a burn which resembles the effect of fire; and this corruption first began on his toe before it spread to take over the whole foot.
The man therefore prepared himself, after seeing that the injury was affecting his limb with its severity which he found through pain and burning sensation; it affected him at the joint, but then he healed.
And after a long period of time passed, the same injury occurred to him in the index finger of his hand.
He came to me, and I tried to remove the excess with him by using medicines which I placed on his hand after cleaning his body; however, it did not drain away, and began to move into another finger until the injury affected his entire hand.
He therefore called for me to amputate his hand; I refused to do it, hoping that the excess could be drained by me, and also fearing death in relation to an amputation of his hand. Since the man's condition was advanced.
After he had lost hope in me, he returned to his land; and he reached out for help from me because he prepared himself and amputated his entire hand, and then healed.
I have not related this account except as a guide for those who suffer from this type of injury; and to indicate what can be indicated, and how it should be treated.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, and the nature of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy parts and the duration of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus; then it ruptures or perforates, and what is inside of it, from the pus, comes out leaving an empty space like a vessel, and the skin over it is like cloth that has been worn thin: if it does not reach its ultimate stage in terms of corruption where it affects bone, nerve or ligament, it should be called a hemorrhoid; but if prolonged until the infection reaches one of these parts, then it may be called an abscess or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut that skin completely as it peels off; especially when it has thinned and become like a cloth; and you trust that it will not stick to the wound due to its own decay.
But if you hope that the skin will stick and it does not follow from this ultimate attachment; and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with enemas; and it is necessary to pay attention to the abdominal cavity.
Then if it is large and the pus emitted from it has a foul odor; then enema it with Egyptian medicine, which consists of equal parts vinegar, oil, and ziniar, combined in a jar; boil them over fire until the medicine reddens; and becomes as thick as honey.
Then take as much of it as you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and enema the abdominal cavity with it; compress its opening, and leave the medicine in for about two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for as many days as necessary until the abdominal cavity is cleansed and the stench is removed.
And sometimes enema with water of ashes when this medicine is not available to you; and it is necessary to take the ash of grapevine wood, or the ash of holm oak wood; and throw it into the water. Then mix it well, and enema with it until you trust that the abdominal cavity is now cleansed.
But if the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, then enema them with water and honey mixed with a small amount of crushed ziniar. Or use an enema made with honey and wine mixed together, since the nature of honey is to cleanse and wash away impurities; and the wine makes the membrane adhere - especially when there is excess astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times and are confident that the corruption of the membrane has been removed, then use an enema made with whatever promotes flesh growth.
And it is good to take some dyaphinion plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or use an enema made with certain other medicines and salves whose properties I have described in the book on salves.
But if the membrane's opening is tight and will not admit the enema pipe, then stretch it a little with an iron instrument; or apply to it linen wrapped around a stick that has been dipped in Egyptian ointment, or in royal ointment, until it is stretched.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then draw its lips together with a stitch; and leave as much of it outside as the enema tube can enter without difficulty.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is raised towards the top, then cut it lower down on its own surface so that the pus can flow out downward; for when pus is confined within the depth of the membrane, it prevents flesh from forming there.
But if you cannot conveniently cut the membrane just below where you wish, then try to prepare the member in such a way that the pus will flow out easily as it is prepared for you. Try to ensure that no pus is retained in it at all; and take the consolidating medicines which are applied to wounds, suitable for what you wish. And extend them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it covers the entire bosom (breast). Then make an incision with the scissors along the edge of the breast opening slightly beyond the nipple. Then spread another soft ointment on the cloth according to the size of the nipple hole; and place it over the breast in a position away from the nipple opening so as to ease the outflow of pus from it: and do not remove the large cloth unless after some days have passed.
But the one that is over the breast opening, is the one you remove every hour, so that what has collected in it of pus may flow out: and so that you know whether the fluid flowing from it is a lot or a little; digested or undigested.
And when you re-examine the breast itself, consider whether its owner feels pain in it or not; and whether there is a boil on it or no boil.
When therefore your treatment has progressed thus and you see the material which comes out over the breast opening, then you know that the breast now adheres or is close to adhering; then therefore remove it in the morning or on the third day: and when you are removing the wound, then change to another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if a refined fluid emerges from the breast after a long time has passed, then do not despair of its adhering, for its healing is approaching; because there are people who delay the growth of flesh in their wounds, and some act contrary to it.
But if undigested pus emerges from the breast after many days, then you know that the breast is not adhering.
If the adherence of the sinus is delayed and its condition prolongs, then add to the drying medications, but let the physicians ensure that the medicines are moist in substance and dry in virtue, such as dyaphyinicon ointment when excess fat is added to it.
And from the very last part that is treated, it is such that you should take dyaphyinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and moisten over it an equal amount of old wine by substance.
Then prepare it with that, and use it.
And it now acts similarly to this procedure, when dyaphyinicon ointment is not present to you, use honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense: pound everything, and powder it over honey while it's over the fire. Then spread over it a cloth and bind it on the sinus: and spread over it the honey that you boiled until it thickens: and powder over it spices, and put over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes it is administered with long birthwort, and roots of heavenly lily, and lily: and herb flour, and centaurea. Use these simples, or aggregate them, pound them, and sieve them: and powder over the honey I described, and use it.
But if the condition of the sinus prolongs and it does not heal with this cure, then know that there is corruption within its depth: or it now causes an impression in the bones, or other hard tissues, or ligaments: then treat it with the cure for Fistulas, as stated before.
On treating panaritium, nail attrition, finger incisions and amputations.
A panaritium is an abscess that develops under the nail of the thumb or toe, and it may occur on other fingers as well.
If it is left untreated and the condition persists, the abscess may become an aposteme that fills with pus; or it may rot and discharge pus until it eats away at the nail bed, potentially corrupting the entire finger, reaching the bone, causing a foul smell, turning the fingertip wide and discoloured.
When you treat it as we mentioned earlier and the treatment does not take effect, then you must use a knife to cut away all of the remaining excess from the nail. Then cauterise the wound after that. Indeed, hot cauterisation is very beneficial.
However, if the bone is sound and both nail bed and root are healthy, but excess nail growth now presses against flesh deeper down, piercing it or hurting it, then you should place a thin probe under the corner of the nail that pierces into the flesh and push it upwards; cut this flesh away gently and apply to what remains of the flesh any burning, corrosive medicine until all is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If however the infection has already reached the bone, then you must cut away and remove that bone; for it will never heal so long as the infected bone remains.
If you see decay and deterioration progressing into the finger, then cut it off at one of the joints, according to what has been previously mentioned. Then treat the wound until it is healed.
However, if there is a nail injury or blow, and severe pain occurs, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first with cupping glasses. Then cut the nail with an acute spatule by making a cross-section from above downwards.
Watch out that the cutting does not reach the flesh underneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain to occur in the patient, and it may result in an addition of flesh at the site due to growth of new tissue.
Then treat the site with something that will relieve pain.
However, the extra finger that appears on some hands of people may be entirely fleshy; and there are bones in some of them, and a nail is present in some: and some of them appear at the base of the joint, or their origin lies in the joints of the digit. The one that appears in the joints of a digit does not move; but the one that arises at the joint of two digits may possibly be movable.
Therefore, the sectioning is easy for the one among them that is fleshy. That is, cut it at its base with a broad spatule.
However, the treatment of the one whose origin is at the base of the joint is difficult. Refrain therefore from cutting it.
However, in the case of one born with an extra digit near the last joint, you must first cut their flesh with a round incision until you reach the bone. Then saw the bone using one of those saws suitable for this purpose. After that, treat it until it is healed.
However, the joining together of fingers which happens either because of birth defects, or from wound healing, or burns from fire, and similar conditions.
It is therefore necessary to cut this joining together of fingers until they return to their original form. Then place between them strips of linen or cloth soaked in rose oil, so that they do not heal quickly and separate them; or place between them a thin sheet of lead until the wound has healed as it should.
And similarly if joining occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, then cut this joining as is prepared and appropriate for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the treatment of disfigurements by incision:
There are twisted, swollen veins full of melancholy humours which appear on many parts of the body; and their outcomes most often occur in the legs, especially those of walkers and workers, and bearers of heavy loads.
It is therefore necessary to administer several strong bleedings in order to remove the black bile.
Then use leeches on the patient's veins.
The treatment of these conditions with iron is done in two ways. One way is to make incisions and extract the black blood. Another method is to raise the vein and remove it entirely.
The incision is made in this way. Soak the leg first with hot water until the thick, dirty blood dissolves. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of their hip to just below the knee with a cloth. Then cut open the vein at one place or two or three places with broad incisions.
Then raise it, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg to above and let the patient's attendant first stop the bleeding with a cauterizer. Then cut the nail down to the quick until you see that enough blood has come out and as much as the patient can tolerate.
After that bind it and tell the patient to avoid foods which produce black bile; repeat the evacuation of the bowels and bloodletting when the veins fill up again and harm the patient.
Its raising is done in this way. Shave the patient's leg if there is much hair on it. Alternatively put the patient in a bath or else soak their leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins stand out or let him exercise vigorously with strong movement if no bath is available until his limbs are warm.
Then cut the skin with a straight incision along the vein's length: either at its end near the knee or on its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it becomes apparent to the touch; you will know that this is so when you see it become red, gradually turning black. As such, once it has come out of the skin, you see that it is white like a cord.
Then insert a rod under it until you see that when raised, it comes out of the skin; suspend it gently with a blunt hook.
Then make another incision close to that first incision, three fingers away from it. Then scrape the skin above the vein until it becomes apparent to the touch. Then raise it with rods as you did before, and then suspend it with another hook just like what was done first.
Then make another incision or more incisions if you need to do so. Then raise it up and then make the last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision above it.
Do this until you pull it out of the top incision or any higher ones until it comes out fully; then cut it.
But if it does not obey you when pulling and raising, then insert a needle with a strong thread twice through it and tie it, then pull it out; or put a rod under it: turn your hand in all directions until it comes out. And take care not to cut it. For if you do cut it, it becomes difficult for you to raise it greatly, and it enters the patient from that harm.
So when you have extracted it all: then place over the wound a piece of wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or just oil; and tend it until it is healed.
But if the vein is twisted, having a turn to the sides, and is not as we said straight: then you should cut over it on all sides of its twist, and the places of its folding.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it all; and take care at your cutting over it not to cut the vein or injure it: for it will be difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
The form of the occurrence by which a vein is healed.
Form of the blind incision.
It does not have the curvature of other incisions, nor is it sharp-ended so as not to wound the vein; and it is thick, curved, and soft: for if it were thin, its thinness would cut into the vein; rather let there be superfluous thickness to it, as we said.
On the extraction of the thigh vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Alizegi, and the land of the Arabs; and also in dry, hot lands with little moisture: that is to say, those where neither grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it generates in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is indeed from the putrefaction that occurs beneath the skin, just as it happens within the bodies of snakes and roundworms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this condition is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the area should be cauterized. Then the vein begins to emerge from that place of cauterization, like the root of a plant or an animal.
When its end becomes visible, you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing between one and two drachmas and tie it with string; then leave the lead hanging from the leg.
So whenever anything comes out of it to the outside, wrap that in lead and tie it up. But if it extends for a long time, then cut off that section and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it off entirely until it has completely emerged. For if you cut it off, it contracts and enters the flesh; this causes an abscess to form, as well as decay in that place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it should be handled gently and drawn out little by little until it has emerged completely, leaving nothing of it inside the body; this vein emerges from some people to a length of five and ten palms. He has now arrived with a wound that measures twenty palms in length.
But if it happens to you at the time of your treatment: then insert a rod into its hole and pierce it with a long hole, with a body, until all that is inside it has been evacuated; and apply the rotting of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with treatment for boils.
And indeed this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle joint or on the foot itself; and it happens to have multiple openings, and a branch comes out from every opening.
Treat it therefore as we have said above, and with what has been mentioned before.
On the operation for worms generated under the skin, which is called bovina disease.
This disease is called bovina disease in a certain region among us; because it often happens to cattle and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it penetrates the entire body, going up and down; and it can be felt during its movement from one limb to another until it bursts through wherever it ruptures a place on the skin and comes out.
And its generation is from the rotting of certain fluids. As happens with worms and snakes, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its evil. Because when it enters the body and is elevated to the head, and reaches its place, then perhaps it opens up there and exits, destroying the eye; and this happens many times.
How then do you want to cure him and extract it, then that won't happen unless during his walk and the appearance of its symptoms.
It is therefore necessary to tie up what is above him and what is below with a strong binding ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it is buried in the flesh and you do not find it, then place a hot iron on the spot until he dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we said.
But if you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind beneath it the eyelid with a good bandage. Then cut above it and extract it.
And the patient must use cleansing of his body with medicines that dissolve harmful putrid fluids.
And he should beware of foods that cause putrefaction.
On the incision for Nakir's disease.
This disease is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in some limbs, then shifts from limb to limb.
And I myself have indeed seen it, as I am telling you. I was called to a sick woman in some villages. And I found her arm swollen, and I saw slight inflammation in the vein of the biceps tendon.
And after I stayed for an hour, I saw one inflammation advancing with little sparks, like a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder faster than it is possible; as if it were live silver running from place to place.
The pain receded from that spot, and was fixed in her shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it rushed into the rest of her body until it reached her other arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving through her entire body, just as I saw.
I was amazed at the speed of its transition from limb to limb. And indeed, I had never before seen this illness with my own eyes in such a way. However, I have seen many who find that their pain shifts from limb to limb; and I have not witnessed it with my own eyes as I did with this woman.
And I do not think it is anything other than the fact that she was among those from dry villages whose veins are easily spotted; therefore, this shift in sensation appears windy to them.
And it is necessary that this not appear according to this reasoning for those who are luxurious and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
When you wish to cure her, and the patient feels this pain; then if it is visible to your eye as we have said, then squeeze firmly above it, and underneath it quickly; and cut over it until the windiness escapes, constrained; and cauterize the spot.
If you cannot see it with your own eyes: then treat it with a shaking of the body, and with that which dispels viscous substances, and let it purge them with foul pills, and with pills made from serapin, and similar substances.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types, and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is such that among them are some which have large barbs, and small ones: hollow, and solid. And there are those which have three edges. And from these some have barbs. And there are those which have stars.
Those which fall according to the limbs into which they fall are of two kinds. For either the limbs are from the hollow principal members, such as the brain, and heart, and spleen, and lungs, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar organs. When it falls into one of these limbs, and you see the signs of death that I will tell you about afterwards, then you must avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many situations. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the limb, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs from the brain when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and emerges from the fontanel which is above the brain, are: for violent pain occurs to him, and dimming of vision, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and a red tongue, and spasms, and clouding of intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood comes out through the nose, or the ears. And possibly speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And out of the place of the wound comes forth white moisture resembling pus. And from it emerges flesh of similar consistency.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then desist from the cure, and extract the arrow if it has not come out.
The signs of an arrow having struck the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and he feels with his hand as though it were fixed in something hard, not empty. And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the pulse; and black blood flows from the wound. And it is followed by coldness of the limbs, and cold sweat, and fainting. You should therefore know that death has surely set in.
And the sign of an arrow having struck the lung is the emergence of foamy blood from the wound; and the veins which follow around the neck become swollen, and the colour of the patient changes. And he emits a great, deep sigh; and seeks to draw in cold air.
If the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the chest, then it is near the lower ribs; and there is great panting with severe pain, together with a sigh; and all parts of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps undigested food comes out from the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and lodges there: some feces may exit from the wound, or a bladder, or intestines that have already been perforated. Therefore there is no hope of curing him, nor of extracting the arrow.
But if the arrow falls into the bladder and urine escapes from it: and something comes out to the exterior, and the pain is more intense above the wounded person: then you know that it is fatal.
The remaining body parts, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder blade, collar bone, spine, hip, leg and similar members can be saved according to many different treatments when the arrow has not struck an artery or nerve, and is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen regarding these arrows so that it may guide your treatment.
That is to say, an arrow fell into the eye of a man, at the root of his nose. So I extracted it from the other side, beneath his ear fat; and he was cured without any damage to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had fallen into the fat of his eye beneath his lower eyelid; and already it was hidden, but I could only grasp its small tip attached to its shaft: this arrow was large among Turkish archery arrows with a rounded head lacking ears.
So the Jew was cured without any bad accident happening to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
The arrow was Arabic, and it belonged to him who has ears. Therefore I secured the arrow between two guide wires above it, and by then it was already submerged in his throat. I thus operated with care until I extracted it; and the Christian was cured.
And I extracted another arrow from the man who had fallen on it; and we thought he would die from that.
After thirty days had passed and nothing in his condition changed, I therefore secured the arrow above it; and I applied ingenuity to this [arrow] and extracted it; he was cured, and no misfortune befell him.
And I saw the man who had fallen on an arrow in his back, and the wound over it was healed. After seven years had passed, the arrow came out at his hip's root.
And I saw a woman who had fallen on an arrow in her belly; and the wound over it was healed, and the arrow remained inside of her: and none of her conditions changed, nor did she find any harm to herself by any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man who had fallen on an arrow in his face; and the wound was healed but it remained there without causing him much pain; and many similar things like this.
And I extracted an arrow from the chief among the king's men which had fallen on him at the middle of his nose and already it bent a little bit towards the right; and all of the arrow was hidden.
I was called to treat him after the arrow wound for three days. I found therefore that the arrow wound was very tight.
So I examined it with a thin probe, and did not feel it; yet he himself discovered the puncture and pain under his right ear. I hoped therefore that it was a puncture from the tip of the arrow.
So I dressed the place with a plaster which had an attracting and digesting power, hoping that the spot would become sore and reveal to me the sign of the arrowhead so that I might cut over it; but nothing happened in this place to indicate that the arrow-head was there.
I kept dressing it for many days with the same plaster; and nothing happened, but meanwhile the wound healed up; and he remained weak from the extraction over a space of some days until one day he felt an arrow inside his nose. And he told me about it.
And he told me about it. I therefore placed a sharp corrosive medicine on the wound for many days until it was opened; and with the probe I probed inside it, and felt the thin end of the arrow-head which was sticking into some wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound by means of that sharp medicine until the end of the arrow-head became visible to us; and this process took about four months.
Then after the wound had been made wider and it was possible for me to introduce a pair of forceps into it and move them about without success; I did not desist from contriving and planning means of extracting it with various instruments until one day I began upon it with a specially-made pair of forceps, which will be described at the end of this chapter, until I extracted it. Then I healed the wound.
And indeed doctors judge that the nose cartilage does not heal. I therefore healed it, and the wound was consolidated, and the patient was cured completely; nor did anything harm him at all.
And I tell you the process of extracting certain arrows, so that you may place that rule and its significance over what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not covered together with the narration; nor does a book comprehend it, and a prudent artisan compares little things to great ones: and what is present, to that which is absent; and finds new methods, and new instruments for the problems of unfamiliar cases in this art when they arise.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of the members in which they are fixed unless according to two methods. Or by pulling it from the place where it entered. Or by pushing from the opposite side of the other part.
And that which comes out where it entered is either an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place; and it is pulled, and comes out.
If it does not obey and come out of the hour that it fell, then you must leave it for a few days until the flesh around it becomes rotten; and its pull is eased, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed into a bone and does not obey you to come out, then leave it for some days too; and repeat pulling on it and moving it every day until it comes out.
If it does not obey you with an exit after a day, then you must perforate around the arrow on the very bone from all sides using a fine trephine until the arrows are enlarged. Then pull it out, and extract it.
But if the arrow is already concealed within some part of the body where it escapes the sense of feeling, then search for it with a probe. But if you feel it, then pull it out using some instruments that are suitable for pulling it out.
But if you cannot manage it because of the tightness of the wound and because of the length of the arrow being deep inside; and there is no bone or nerve or vein in that place to prevent you: then wait over it until the wound widens, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If there are ears to which it is attached, then free the flesh fixed on them from every side and by all means you can do so, and use tricks if you cannot without tearing away the flesh at the fracture of the ears, and twisting them until they come out.
And when you perform the arrow extraction in any place it is located, then use your hand to twist and scissors for all parts until you make it come out: and with extreme caution so as not to break the arrow, which would make its retrieval difficult.
If you cannot remove it within an hour, then leave it for a few days until the tissue around it becomes rotten. Then try again to remove it: the pain will have subsided by then. If the bleeding is a problem for you, then perform what we said regarding treatment in its own chapter. And take care with your skill so as not to cut an artery or nerve.
And apply every trick you can to remove the arrow as easily and calmly as possible, with slowness and strength just as I have described it for you.
And you must make sure that the patient is positioned during arrow extraction in just as they were when shot by it. Because it will be of greater benefit to them. If that is not possible for you then apply what is possible according to the positioning of the patient.
If the arrow is exiting from another part of the body or something has already emerged to the outside, or if you see that a bulge is forming at the end of the arrow through your senses on the upper side of nearby skin: cut above it and make an incision large enough to fit scissors into.
Then pull it out; for it now becomes easy to remove.
If it is retained within the bone, then turn your hand according to its curvature until the arrowhead embeds itself into the bone and widens; subsequently, pull it out.
And if not: leave it for several days; then try again until it exits.
If the arrow shaft is within her, push it out with that too.
If the shaft has fallen out but you wish to administer a push: then introduce into it an implement with a concave end so that its cavity may encompass the tail of the arrowhead; subsequently, push it along with the latter.
If, however, the arrowhead is concave: push it along with an instrument entering into its concavity. Indeed, for the latter will alleviate the pain of the arrowhead's exit.
If the arrowhead is poisoned, it is necessary to scoop out all the flesh containing the poison; if you are able to do so. Then treat it with whatever is suitable for its healing.
If the arrowhead is lodged in the chest, or abdomen, or thigh, or side; and it can be reached with your probing fingers, and you are able to cut above it: then do so, being mindful of any veins or nerves that may lie nearby, and extract it.
Then sew up the wound if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrowheads may be extracted.
The extremities are like a bird's beak, which have been made like iron: when they grasp the arrowhead, they do not let it go.
And they come in many shapes and sizes: large, small, and medium, according to the size of the arrowhead, its smallness, the width of the wound, and its tightness.
The shape is that of an impelling concave.
And the shape is that of a deafening impulse.
This is the deaf extremity like a wedge, so as to ease its entry into the concave arrowhead and drive it with itself.
On venesection of veins.
The veins that have become accustomed to venesection in the body are thirty in number.
Of these, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsatile veins that are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries that are visible in the two temples; and two veins that are in the two lacrimals of the two eyes, which are called Watchers; and a vein that is prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Guidegies, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins that are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
Veins however that are phlebotomized in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic vein, and it lies on the side of the wrist; and the common people call this the head vein. And the second one is the Basilic vein. And the third one is the median vein, which branches from both the Basilic vein and the Cephalic vein; this common people call it the body vein. And the Basilic vein is located on the inner side of the arm; it is also called the ulnar vein. And the common people call this belly vein. And the Brachial vein is situated on top of the forearm; this is the one that is phlebotomized and it appears above the thumb as a prominent bulge.
And the superificialis vein; this is situated between the ring finger and little finger, and it has two branches.
And there are three veins in the leg and foot. One of them is located underneath the curvature of the thumb on the outer side. The second is the saphenous vein, which lies near to the heel on the inner side. The vein Ness: and its location is by the heel bone on the side of the silvester.
And in the other calf there are three veins similar to those.
Regarding bloodletting of two veins behind the ears.
Bloodletting of the two veins behind the ears is beneficial for chronic catarrh, headache, and old head ulcers.
And the quality of performing bloodletting is as I describe it. And the patient's head should be shaved, then their occipital region is rubbed thoroughly at the site of the two veins with a coarse cloth and thorough friction. Then the patient should tighten their neck with a cord until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears at two depressed areas of the head. So probe for them with your finger, and wherever you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark it with the cauterizing iron.
Then take up the lancet-type bloodletting tool, which is called the "Incising" one. Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the bloodletting cup reaches the mouth of the incision. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein and skin in an incising motion. Let the length of the cut be about two joined fingers in quantity or around that; and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then compress it with cloths and leave it until it heals.
And now we have already discussed the incision of these at the beginning of the book, as well as their cauterization.
The bleeding of two arteries in the temples is beneficial for old headache; bad eyesight; and persistent red eyes caused by a flow of excess humours.
And the quality of bleeding these arteries is as I tell you. The patient should firmly press their neck until two veins become visibly apparent and you can feel the pulsation of them under your finger. Then mark these with an iron cautery.
Then lift the skin above the vein with your index finger and insert a bleeding-lance from underneath, then raise the vein upwards. And cut it as you did with the other two veins, and let flow as much blood as your need requires.
Then release the patient's compression and place your finger over the vein for an hour. Afterwards place a poultice over it and tightly compress the patient from above with strong binding; leave it until it heals.
And now we have already revisited the discussion of these, their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of this book.
The remedy for venesection of the frontal vein is after that of the cephalic vein, for old facial ailments: and for hemicrania, and redness: and for foul ulcers.
And the quality of its venesection is, as I tell you. The patient should strain his neck with a towel until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called the "burrower".
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the burrower over the vein itself, and strike above it with a comb or some other thing in its manner: and let the blood run according to the quantity you want. Then loosen the patient's piercing, and squeeze it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet. However it ought not to be that the lancet is of a sharp point like other lancets, but rather of a broad point. And let the lancet be passed across it according to its direction: for the bone is near: and perhaps therein the lancet will break when it is thin.
The remedy for the two veins that are in the two lachrymals of the two eyes is for eye scab and redness: and a sty in the eye, and facial ailments.
And the quality of their venesection is that the patient should strain his neck with a towel. Then perform bloodletting on them: and you should stand over their head.
And the bloodletting should be done transversely along a small length with a small, somewhat wide, lancet because there is no flesh in that place. For if the lancet is fine at its point, it may break.
Then draw as much blood as you need to make [the patient] run. And put a bandage on them, tighten it one night, then loosen it.
Bloodletting from the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever, severe inflammation, and facial ailments such as headache and facial redness, especially when it is longstanding.
The nature of this bloodletting is such that the patient should tighten their neck. Then hold their nose with your left hand and take a fine, long lancet; fix it into the middle of their nose, between that which divides the nose lengthwise. For the vein does not appear there to the sense [of touch], but because blood starts flowing immediately.
And you should keep your hand [on the lancet] to some extent; draw as much blood as is necessary for you. Then bandage it one night. For it heals quickly.
The two bloodletting instruments of Guidegi are beneficial for treating shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, melancholy ailments, itching rashes, and malignant sores, as well as corroding herpes-like conditions.
And the quality of those bloodletting instruments is such that they should be tightened on the patient under both armpits around their neck with a bandage; and the practitioner should stand over the patient's head while the patient sits on a chair.
Next, cut open the blood vessels themselves with an appropriate length-wide bloodletting knife. Then draw out an appropriate quantity of blood, or according to the necessity that you see is required. Afterwards, do likewise with the other vein.
Then untie the bandage and tighten both veins with moderate pressure to prevent the patient from being overly weakened. And leave it until morning. The wound will heal.
On the bloodletting of the jugular veins, by Algaberich. Algaberich's bloodletting instruments for the jugular veins are useful because they help with bad breath and gum infection; malignant sores; and lip cracks as well as malignant sores on the nose or around it.
The quality of those bloodletting instruments is such that you should make the patient sit before you and tighten their neck with a cord. Then roll back their lip and look at the two veins - one from the right side of their lip, and another from its left; you can see both by the blackness they display on either side. And it is true that around them are small, black veins. Make an incision on both sides with an incising instrument.
But if there is doubt about which one it is, and you do not know which one it is, then direct your attention to the greater of them and the more obvious ones.
And likewise do the same with regard to the two veins that are in the upper lip, and it is indeed customary for this to be done; and make an incision on both of the veins that are in the lower lip.
On bloodletting from the vein beneath the tongue after bloodletting from the cephalic vein. Bloodletting from the two veins beneath the tongue is beneficial for relief of swellings that are in the throat, and diseases of the uvula, and disorders of the mouth.
And the manner of bloodletting from them is that the patient should sit between your hands in front of the sun; and lift their tongue, and look beneath the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for another vein: and note that the colour of each has turned towards blackness.
Bloodlet from both; and be careful not to press your hand too hard when bloodletting from them. For beneath it are arteries, and perhaps there may be a gushing of blood from those arteries.
Now the three veins from which blood is customarily let in the elbow are those from which bloodletting has become customary among all people.
And venesection on these can be done in two ways. Or by pricking with a broad myrtle-stick venesector; or with an oil flask inclined to fineness; or by cutting with the knife type venesector which is called alnesil. And this is its form:
And from them are broad types, and fine ones according to the width of veins and their constriction. The form of the myrtle stick venesector is broad as you can see. And it is suitable for opening hollow veins, full of blood, protruding and visible ones; thick veins too those which contain thick, murky blood.
And this venesector is of lesser breadth and finer at the tip.
And it is suitable for venesection of fine veins which contain thin yellow blood.
And the basilic vein, which is one of these three venesection veins. Because it extracts blood from illnesses under the throat and neck, of what follows after breast down to the belly; and it is suited for a skilled venesector with their instrument to be careful about it, and be above fear of it because underneath it is an artery. So if they make an error and add to the venesector's incision, they will cut that artery, and there results a gushing of blood. Therefore, one should not perform a venesection on her with a sharp lancet; instead, let it be done by cutting with alnesil.
If it does not appear as a clear manifestation in the basilica, then one must remove others from it and change to another location; or seek some offshoot of it, or perform a venesection at its place on the arm. For it is indeed manifest, and cut it with the lancet anesil, as we have said.
But if you wish to locate the vein itself, then one must feel around for a spot in front of the tightness [?] of the arm until you identify the pulse. Then mark the spot with a cauterizing iron; afterwards cut the arm and cut [?] the vein with a concave incision using the lancet alnesil, as we have said. And be careful that no percussion falls through space from the place of the artery.
And when you see that, as you tighten the binding, there is swelling at the spot marked by the cauterizing iron, then this swelling is of the artery; therefore avoid it.
But if you see blood rising during the venesection like a child's urine and it is thin, red blood; then you know that it is from arterial blood. Then hurry and place your finger over that long space, then remove it. If the flow of blood is arrested (for it often is), then bind the arm and let it be; and caution the patient against moving; and reassure them about their fear.
And it should not move for several days until healed.
But if the blood is not cut off and it overpowers you: and there is no medicine available for you at that moment, then open the artery if it appears to you. For its ends contract, and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of pistachio and pound it: take one half, and bind it over the place where you are binding the vein appropriately, with a bandage and lint until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with what preceded the mention of the place where the removal of the flow of blood originated: and its cutting off of blood is not difficult in many cases because of the small location of the wound, and possibility of binding from the arm.
As for the dark vein of phlebotomy, its function is to draw blood from the upper part of the head and lower part of the body: because it consists of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist to be above fear from its phlebotomy, because beneath it is a nerve. Therefore if he adds to the puncture of its phlebotomy and finds a nerve, numbness occurs in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it may not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to sensation. If it is hidden in certain people and is subtle, and does not show itself, then you must make an incision with your phlebotomy tool where you want to remove the abscess, along with a cut using alnesil, and avoid nerves with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then open up the vein lengthwise.
Regarding the cephalic vein. The cephalic vein phlebotomy is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and helps alleviate eye ailments.
In this vein specifically, if you wish to perform a phlebotomy, it is best to do so by pricking with an olive-wood or myrtle-wood phlebotomist tool, as it is safer than all other veins since there are no arteries or nerves underneath.
However, when performing a phlebotomy, it is advisable to avoid the start of the forearm with your phlebotomist tool and look for a softer spot; if not found on the first try, repeating the procedure multiple times will not harm them.
However, perhaps an abscess forms in some people when not lanced on the first strike but this abscess does no harm to them.
On the usefulness of phlebotomy and precautions to be observed during it. The quality of phlebotomy and its complications from which you must think about remedying it.
First and foremost, you must know that a phlebotomy is not administered unless it preserves health, sustains its endurance, and protects from illnesses.
And to perform a phlebotomy on one of the three veins that are found at the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Thumbs, and the Basilic vein. And to perform a phlebotomy at the beginning of spring while fasting and also during summer; likewise autumn, and winter after a meal when signs of fullness appear.
And to perform a phlebotomy on the first day or third day after three hours have passed since the last meal was taken.
A phlebotomy administered for illness does not have a set time; rather it is done when the situation and necessity call for it to be performed at night or day, and during any hour of the day and every season.
And it is not necessary for boys to undergo the procedure until they reach fourteen years of age. A pregnant woman should not undergo the procedure from her fourth to seventh month of pregnancy. Neither should elderly people who have reached or passed sixty years of age undergo the procedure.
When a person wishes to undergo the procedure for any reason, they must cleanse their intestines beforehand with an enema; if there is hardened faeces in them, so that during the procedure, the veins will not draw from the intestine harmful impurities which damage major organs.
Nor should the procedure be performed on a patient who is afflicted with nausea and discomfort following a meal; or one suffering from inebriation or hangover until these conditions have passed. Nor should it be performed on a patient suffering from dropsy (edema).
And the procedure must not be performed repeatedly after a case of colic pain, vomiting, abdominal flux, excessive sexual intercourse, labour or other physical exertion; nor during a period of restlessness, fasting, and any circumstance that causes depletion of one's vitality from either body or mind.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if blood is thick with food and drink, and medicines: if it is possible for him to do so.
Then he should enter the bath, if nothing prevents it; or engage in some form of exercise to refine the blood.
And place the phlebotomy on his chest around the third hour of the day, as we have said; and try to evacuate from his chest that day all bad animal incidents: such as distress, anger, fear; and all bodily incidents, such as labor, pain, excessive sexual intercourse, and similar things.
And he should make available to himself those things from which custom has driven away the use of men on account of their bitterness, smell and touch, and similar things as far as possible in quantity.
Then the bloodletter should sit on a cushion that is higher than the patient's mattress; afterwards he should extend his arm and rub it twice or thrice with his own hand. Then bind the ligature with straps and roll them around twice.
The binding should be moderate, because when the binding is uneven or excessive it prevents the flow of blood; likewise with too little binding.
After the binding it is necessary for the patient to rub his hands together until the veins fill and become apparent to sight.
Then the bloodletter should wipe the phlebotomy with a little oil, which is old and his own.
Then the bloodletter should place his left index finger on the vein beneath the site of the intended phlebotomy, lightly; so that the vein does not escape and the strike is not broken. Because from the veins you find one like a string that escapes during bleeding; and of these is one which is full of windiness: and when you place the lancet upon it, it contracts under the lancet, and deceives the blood-letter, and the lancet does not open the vein; and if it opens it, it is tight: therefore, for this reason it is necessary that the blood-letter be made firm, and be solicitous in all these matters.
Then put down the lancet. If therefore the vein is opened with one thrust, then it is good. And if not, then repeat that thrust with another a little below or above the same place quickly; if the spot does not blanch.
But if it blanches or the patient grows faint, then let him rest one day, or two days; and do not bind up tightly. For perhaps it will draw out heat as a pustule. Nor should one enter the bath.
Then repeat bloodletting if he desires it.
But if the opening is small or blood flows slowly; and you fear that not enough blood will come out: then repeat the lancet in the same hole according to its straightness, and add a little to the procedure.
And do it quickly before the spot blanches; for indeed a blanched spot is found in many men during small procedures.
If therefore you see that it is already blanched, then do not repeat on it at all; for nothing excuses you. Place on it a bit of oil residue: for it eases the flow of blood. And honey is better in this place than oil itself, and than other oils.
Likewise, apply oil residue to all your bloodlettings where you cut veins in order to prevent the flow of blood; and make that with alfarah ointment and sagzene when one of them is placed on a spot.
For blood becomes thinner and dissolves when it's thick.
But if a large abscess occurs at the bloodletting site - which often happens, especially for those who haven't been bled before or have a small vein opening - then hurry and place over the spot a sponge soaked in warm water and salt; press it there for an hour: since it dissolves.
And you must do this after the blood's stopped flowing from that vein itself or from another.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the spot after a few days that's not harmful; and if you wish to apply over it something of aloe or dissolved myrrh; or anything of mint juice, which is called calamint or similar.
And indeed abscesses and swellings often occur near the Basilica's bloodletting.
Place your hand over it; then if you find softness under your pressure on it, that swelling is bad. Therefore take care to place over it something of what we've said. For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it.
But make a plaster for him with that which has astringency, so that the area hardens. Then treat himself with the remaining treatment until he is healed.
And you must take from everyone blood according to the quantity of their strength. And that which appears in color over the blood is victorious.

On Cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping glasses are made of horn, and of brass, and of glass.

And this cupping that is without scarification, is done in two ways. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the cupping sites that are applied with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen places on the body. One of which is cupping on the forehead, and it is at the back of the head; Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; and Alhagdahim cuppings, and they are two layers of the neck from both sides together; and mentum cupping, and it is under the jaw below the mouth; and two spatula cuppings; and Alachosos cupping at the end of the spine; and two bowl cuppings, and they are in the middle of two arms; and two leg cuppings; and two spoon cuppings.
And cupping does not extract blood from the small veins scattered throughout the flesh, and because of that, there is no loss of strength from it as happens with phlebotomy. Nor is it appropriate to apply cupping with fire or without fire for any illnesses that arise from congestion until the entire body is evacuated.
If necessity arises to apply cupping because of illness or custom, we do it at any hour at the beginning of a month, at its end, and in its middle, and at whatever time is required.
This occurs because there are men who, when blood multiplies within them, require its extraction by means of venesection; they find heaviness in their head and a stoppage.

And among them there is one who sees blood and redness, killings, injuries, and things similar to these in their sleep.
