This chapter too is often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is about restoring fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, O Sons, that fools among the doctors and laity boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined an old book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is neglected among the sciences in our land. And I myself never encountered any good in it at all, and did not achieve from it what I achieved except through the length of reading old books and my study to understand them, until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I clung to experiment and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains on the subject: and above what has passed by way of experiment with me, after having shortened it for you, and relieved it of the burden of prolixity, and abbreviated it by way of concise brevity, and shown it by way of decisive declaration: and I have depicted in it many figures of the forms of instruments that are employed in it, since it is a matter of supplementary statement, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter 2.
We must say at the beginning of this chapter that we state in summary form and briefly the issues whose understanding is required first by a surgeon regarding any operation: namely to know or learn this noble skill for yourself as well as teach others.
I say therefore that when a person has a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall then you must go quickly to perform bloodletting and give laxatives, or both together if nothing prevents it such as weakness of constitution or the patient is either a child or a decrepit old man, or it's a time of extreme heat or severe cold.
Next you should restrict them to their diet, cold vegetables and the meat of birds or kids and forbid wine and coarse meats and allow filling foods and any food that fills the veins with blood. Until you are sure that the swelling has cooled and there is no fear of pus spreading to the limb then the patient can return to their normal diet with which they are accustomed.
When the broken bone begins to heal then the patient should be fed nourishing foods with plenty of nutrients that are weighty and heavy with a degree of viscosity such as cooked wheat-based dishes in water or rice and heads and feet and stomachs of fowl and eggs and fresh fish and thick wine and similar. When this regimen is followed, fracture healing occurs faster and better.
Also know that fractured bones, when they occur in men who are in their old age and decrepit, cannot always heal and consolidate according to their nature due to the desiccation of their members; instead, they heal and consolidate as if they were those of small children.
However, nature binds to the fractured bone from all sides a substance similar to glue that has some thickness and adheres to it and holds it together until they adhere to each other and bind with one another; until in time, strength and stability return as before, so that nothing hinders its function anymore. For this reason, it is necessary for you to provide the patient with nourishing food that has weight, viscosity, and thickness, such as we have mentioned.
Also know that the types of fractures are diverse, depending on the different members of the body; for a leg fracture is different from one to the head bone, and a chest bone fracture differs from a back bone fracture, and likewise all other members differ from each other. And I have come with an exposition in its own chapter of every type of fracture set apart from another. And the types of bone fractures also differ within themselves.
Because its fracture is curved, other than when pieces fall out of it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone; and there are stepped fractures within it, that is, pieces, and projections proceeding from them as in a reed when it is cut with a knife to write with. They do not proceed: it is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head; and it is a small crack fracture.
And for every type of fracture there is a specific tool or instrument to repair it, as will be recalled later and explained at its proper place.
And from what is known of bone fractures, there are its twisting and prominence; and their appearance to the sense and sound when you press them with your hand.
When, therefore, there is no apparent twist in place nor sound at your touch when you feel the bone with movement and the patient does not find great pain; then it's not a fracture but rather likely a twist or small crack: thus, one should not move it with hand and pressure but apply over it from medicines which will be recalled later, after some time appropriate to that place and then bind it with fine bandages.
And know that when the place is bent and split into two parts but without any other accident to it while all its parts are removed from their fellow; then you must begin your treatment of it from the start by equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs.
However, if an hot abscess occurs then leave it for several days until the abscess subsides. Then equalize it according to whatever way you can from its softness and nature.
And know that its repair and equalization is easier than that of a bone with other accidents.
Then bind it according to what will be recalled to you.
If there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, if it is a hand or foot; or with your own hand, if it is a small limb; or with two cords, or with one cord and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb over an equal spot, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended: then try to bring back those additions with all skill, and in whatever way you can to their places; and beware, according to your strength, lest there happen upon the sick person due to your operation pain, or injury; and try, according to your strength, to fit together one of two bones with its fellow, according to the best shapes.
And it is necessary at that hour that you attempt to grasp and touch it with your own hand. If therefore you see something awry there, correct it, and equalize it according to the measure of your strength.
And beware of violent stretching and strong compression, as many fools do. For many times they cause to happen through their operation a hot abscess or contraction in the limb, as we have often seen from their operations.
Then bind up after the equalizing and proper operation, and the setting of that limb, and rest, and tranquility; and advise the sick person regarding it. And make him beware, lest he move it in the hour of his watches and sleep regarding its revolution and agitation and at excretion and all his movements with his utmost strength. And he should take care that there is no position of the limb where pain follows. Which is because when he feels pain or injury in a particular limb position, then it should be changed to another where there is no pain.
And you should advise him to ensure that his position is equal and straight so as not to cause any curvature when the limb is restored.
And the quality of the fracture in the broken limb is as I describe it.
You should be aware that broken limbs vary according to their smallness or largeness, and shape.
So those that are small, like the arm, finger, toe, and similar, then a soft, fine linen bandage should be used. But those that are large, like the thigh or leg, a wide, firm bandage should be used.
Because the wide bandage joins together a large limb and tightens its edges equally on all sides of the fracture so that nothing protrudes.
Then you should start putting on the appropriate ointment after your cleaning from the centre outwards with soft lint over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to bandage over the place of fracture itself with three or four turns as is appropriate for the limb, and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with him to the upper part from where you bandaged the fracture site and tighten less than the first bandage. Then extend yourself with a roll from the fracture site for a while, and gradually loosen the structure until you grasp something substantial from the healthy spot.
Then take another strip of cloth and roll it again over the broken spot with rolls. After that, go down with the roll to the lower part below the fracture. And let your procedure be in the tightness of the roll and its loosening as we said for the first upper roll.
Then place between the rolls some soft linen or cloth to even out any curvature of the fracture if there is one; and if not, do not put anything into it.
Then roll another strip over the same spot. After that, arrange warm compresses on these rolls immediately if there is neither swelling nor a hot abscess at the site.
But if there is swelling or an abscess, then place over it what sits on the abscess and removes the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then bind compresses tightly over them at that point.
And let the compresses be made of half-sized hollow reeds, scraped and shaped with skill; or they can be made of wood used for making sieves which are made from pine or gerahd i.e., palm branches, or calingi; each compress should have equal breadth, according to this shape and figure.
However, the compress that is placed directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and broader than the other compresses; and its length should match the limb according to its size and smallness.
Then tighten it over the aster with another piece of thread, according to the same first binding. Then bind it from above with off-centre threads, as we have said about binding: and [ensure] that your binding is over the site of fracture; the more you elongate [the wound] away from the fracture, make the binding tighter.
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and they are to be made from soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the work of fools who use their own threads made from twisted linen cord, it is a great mistake; for the binding fails with them outside the realm of effectiveness.
And fine threads also are not suitable; for you will not achieve with them the binding that you wish to attain.
And it is not necessary for there to be less than a finger's breadth between the aster and the next one along.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the asters after binding them to healthy sites; then place under them soft tow or crimson wool until nothing hurts from it.
When there is a fracture with an overlying wound, and it happens that there is disruption of the skin, I will come to this in chapter two with particular reference to its uniqueness.
And you should know that not every broken limb must be bound with asters from the first day onwards. This is because when a limb is large, you need not place asters upon it unless after five or seven days, or more, according to your judgement about the hot abscess-like development.
This is the way of making dressings by which the ancients restored those bones that are broken and dislocated.
A method for making an abbreviated common plaster that restores broken bones and is suitable for many different constitutions but especially women and children since it does not succumb to heat or cold.
It is made from the flour dust of the mill and the flour dregs that cling to the walls of the mill near the motion of the millstone: and you mix it as it is, except that you sieve it with an egg white sieve: and lay its mixture neither thick nor thin. Then apply it.
A method of plaster that restores fractures, dislocations, and twists. Take almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugwort; of all sucks take 10 oz., of myrrh and aloe each take 5 oz., and of althel take 20 oz.; grind everything together and sieve it and mix with althel water: or with egg white if the patient's constitution is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and suits many people.
A method of plaster that also suits fractures and twists. Take mugwort, mess, and white lilies; of all take 10 oz., of myrrh and aloe each take 5 oz., acacia takes 6 oz., and Armenian bole takes 20 oz.; grind everything together with great care and sieve it and mix with water or egg white: and apply.
The method for applying a plaster to joints and bones separated from their places; it relieves the pain that occurs in them, [treats] bone dislocation and fracture. Woolen cloth should be soaked in vinegar and boiled oil, then applied to the site.
This plaster has no restorative properties but is good for soothing hot abscesses; it specifically alleviates pain.
Method of a plaster that restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree and wild poplar; grind them all together and make a plaster from both.
Method of applying an abbreviated plaster for the restoration of a bone fracture; when you want to resolve residual abscesses. Take one portion each of marshmallow root, chamomile, violets, and the herb plantain; grind them all together then mix with wine unless the limb is hot [inflamed].
If the limb is hot [inflamed], mix it with moist coriander water or water instead and use as required.
Another method with a stronger plaster for resolving [hard abscesses] than the previous one, when an abscess develops during limb restoration. Take one portion each of marshmallow root, linseed, balsam, honey locust, water-plantain, violet flowers and chamomile; grind them all together then mix with willow water or fresh water or wine. All of this should be done according to the temperature of the limb and calming its heat [inflammation].
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything other than a poultice made of clay and oil should be placed on a broken limb during its restoration; he described it as being of moderate thickness.
Galen considered that substances with drying and warming properties, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, along with similar substances from those we have mentioned, should be applied to a broken limb during its restoration.
Recipe for an ointment that relieves injury and pain. Take equal parts of mugwort, chickpeas, human hair clippings or bird feathers, nettle, and salt; grind them up, sieve the mixture, and process it into an ointment.
The length of time for which a dressing should remain in place before being removed depends on considerations such as the absence of pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its position; if these do not occur, it may be left in place for many days. If the patient experiences severe itching, disturbing pain or swelling at the site of the dressing, it should be removed immediately; take a soft cloth or a moistened marine sponge and soak it in warm water to gently wash the area until the itching subsides and any pain is soothed.
If the patient experiences severe itching, disturbing pain or swelling at the site of the dressing, it should be removed immediately; take a soft cloth or a moistened marine sponge and soak it in warm water to gently wash the area until the itching subsides and any pain is soothed. After that, allow the limb to rest for an hour. Then apply a piece of woollen felt soaked in vinegar and oil or rosewater over the limb and bandage it there for one night until you are assured that the heat blister has subsided, any swelling of the limb is quieted and its pain has been relieved.
Then repeat this on a fine structure, and apply a small plaster; do not tighten it with your first compression, and keep the fine bandages with it until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all other symptoms have now completely disappeared, and you need a plaster and bandage, then repeat this process as you did initially.
If none of the things we have mentioned happen to the limb, then do not remove it unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when you remove it after twenty days, depending on how the limb appears upon its release, as we have mentioned, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approaches over it: then also add in a bandage greater than your first compression altogether; also add to the enlargement of the patient's diet, according to what has been previously recalled.
However, if you see that the place of fracture is already becoming dried out and more weakened than it should be, then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it. Therefore immerse it with warm water at every release you make of it, and let this occur over the course of three days; also ease off on the bandage somewhat.
By this process therefore nourishment reaches the limb and it heals quickly.
But that which unskilled surgeons do when restoring a broken limb with another bone instead of the original one, because it is not restored properly at first and then has to be restored crookedly: this error arises from their procedure, and causes great fear; and if it had been correct they would have said so in ancient texts, and acted upon that.
And I have not seen any traces of them there at all, and it is better that it should not be done.
Regarding the treatment of head injuries:
Types of fractures vary widely, with many different shapes and forms, and they have diverse causes.
One type of fracture occurs due to a strike with a sword; the strike removes the entire bone up until it reaches the marrow beneath the bone, similar to how an axe splits wood; hence this particular kind of fracture is known as axial.
Or the sword cuts into a bone and grazes its surface without penetrating all the way through; this kind of fracture is called *kala'an*, or absolute levation.
And it is an injury with two fractures, either large or small.
There is another kind of fracture that involves crushing or grinding; this occurs due to a strike with a stone, a fall onto one, or similar incidents.
This fracture can also be penetrating, approaching the marrow beneath the bone; or it can occur on the surface of the bone itself. It is likewise an injury with two fractures that are either small or large.
There is yet another type of fracture that is hidden within the bone itself, as thin as a hair; it is a small split and thus named *capillaris*.
Another fracture occurs from a fall or strike with a stone and similar incidents that cause a piece of bone to enter the body cavity; it creates a hollow space there, like what happens in bellows when they receive a blow, allowing some air to pass into their insides.
And it happens most frequently in the heads of moist bones, such as those of children.
And all these types of fractures occur separately, and I will come with a recollection of that whole thing in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by examination upon them and investigation with probes, as well as removal of the corrupted flesh that is above them.
The capillary type is recognized by this: that which is detected over the bone and wiped away and lined above it with cauterization; for the fracture appears black.
However, in treating a fracture, consider first the patient's condition: then if you see from their conditions that they indicate clearly by vomiting of bile and fainting, as well as prostration, loss of speech, syncope, acute fever, giddiness, flushing of the face, and similar symptoms in many situations, certainly it will not heal; and if you see non-frightening conditions, and hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
That is, if the wounded comes to you when first wounded: and it occurs during the winter days, then you must aim at removing bone before the fourteenth day according to every situation.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is beneath the bone from the flesh decays and those symptoms that we described occur.
If the bone fracture has already reached the membrane touching the brain and is with shreds and stripping: then you must cut away the comminuted and stripped-away part as I tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person; and expose over the bone according to whatever manner is possible for you; and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light on the sick one.
If it should happen to you during the exposure of bone that there is a flow of blood or a hot abscess: then oppose it with what is appropriate. And it is necessary that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil; and let the wound rest until the abscess settles down, and be assured of no more flow of blood. Then begin with the cure of bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is that you cut the bone with an Incisory of a sharp iron: and this is its form.
Then apply another Incisory, after the narrower one, somewhat wider: and this is its form.
Then apply yet another Incisory that is even wider than the last one.
The second method is that you have a number of different Incisories with some being wider than others and some shorter than others; and they should be very sharp at their ends, and made from iron or steel: and strike gently on the Incisory so as not to shake the head and hurt it.
But if the bone is hard and strong: then it is necessary that you drill around its edge before applying Incisories with Terebras which are called Non-deepening Drills; they are not so named unless because itself does not penetrate through the end of the bone to what is beyond it, since the Drill has a round tip on top of which is like an edge and small circle underneath prevents sinking in, and passing through the thickness of bone.
You should take many of these drills from those bone chisels, each suitable for the thickness of a bone, until you have a drill for every part of the skull; the length and width of the pointed end of which corresponds to the thickness of that part of the skull.
These are three forms of drills or trepans.
The technique of drilling around the circumference of a broken bone is to place the drill on top of the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place and put in between any hole the thickness of a finger or about that much.
Then cut with knives what is between any hole in the bone and make it as thin as you can at that end of lightness, as we have said, until you scrape out the bone, either by hand or with some other tool which you prepared for that purpose such as forceps and fine scissors.
And you must take every precaution that the drill or knife does not touch any of this soft matter.
When you have therefore drilled around the bone and separated it from that soft matter, if they are still joined together then you must cut and scrape equally the end of what remains in the bone with another similar knife-like instrument. However it must be thinner and finer than the other knives.
If there remains any small pieces of bone and gristle then take them out carefully with the tool you have at hand for that purpose. Then treat the wound afterwards with dressings and ointments as we have described.
Another method of operation is easy, completely removed from fear: which Galen said and praised it with great praise: and these are his words. It is necessary for you first of all to begin to expose the bone in that place where the fracture is more violent and apparent: until when you have exposed this part and placed under it one end of this Lenticular Knife whose form is thus.
And the Lenticular part which is blunt does not cut anything: and its sharp part on both of its sides tapering to a point as you can see: so that the lenticular part may be applied against the bone with its cutting edge acute towards the bone.
Then strike upon the Knife from one side with a small mallet until you have cut through the entire bone as it turns: and be sure to stand clear of the knife so that no accident happens there which you greatly fear.
And if the physician were not a more ignorant and stupid person he would not be asleep now: then if any portion of this membrane still remains attached to the bone in certain places he should cut it away from that with one end of the same lenticular knife and free it from there easily. It is freed from this without injury and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not extend through to the membrane and that which is torn away from the bone is its surface while roughnesses and fine ridges remain in it: then you must scrape off this roughness and remove those ridges with very delicate scrapers from which you have prepared a number of different sizes so as to be able to apply from these those most convenient and suitable for each place according as your operation leads you and the fractured bone itself and its shape. You must sharpen your blade more than those of the other razors. Next, use the finer one after that.
You must sharpen your blade more than those of the other razors. Next, use the finer one after that. And do not stop doing this until you reach the stage of sharpening the thinnest and finest of them all.
However, for each of the other small hairline fractures and fine breaks, you must take care to sharpen according to what leads to their correction.
And this is something that cannot be hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and they will have stood over what we have written and shown regarding large breaks.
But if a piece of membrane is found and retained when you remove the bone: then you must take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Next, take another piece of cloth, doubled or trebled, immerse it in wine and rose oil, place it over the first piece of cloth, and make sure you do this as easily as possible so that it does not rub against the wound.
Then apply over this a wide bandage and do not tighten it unless the amount is such that it just holds the wound together; leave this for one day or two until you are sure the hot inflammation has gone. Next, remove this bandage and apply afterwards medicines that are appropriate for excessive dryness such as lilies roots, herb flour, incense powder, aristolochia, and similar things; and it is best to make a powder of these medicines which you can sprinkle over the wound like a dry dusting.
And generally administer to it all medicine that has the property of cleansing and not stinging. And ensure that according to your virtue, the wound is clear during its treatment; there should be no grease stains or dirt.

Therefore, consider that if the patient experiences any of the conditions we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur unless it is caused by the medicine that is applied to it and if it is in the virtue of that medicine to cause such blackness, then you must take one part honey, three parts rose oil, and line them with a cloth. Then apply it to the syphilitic sores; afterwards treat it with curative spices that are suitable until it is healed.

You should know that only the two superior parts of the nose are broken at once or one of them; for it is composed of two bones: since its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break and nothing happens to it other than abrasion, twisting, and indentations.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you must insert a small finger into the nostril hole, and with your index finger and thumb, realign that fracture until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully, with ease. And beware, lest by your operation that pain upon the patient occur.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you must use the end of a rod, one that is a small thickness.
If however the fracture is in both parts, then do likewise; and be quick to restore it on the first day of the break, if possible. And if not, then after seven or ten days when the swelling has cooled.
Then insert into the end of the nose a linen thread if the break is in one part; or insert two threads if it is broken in both parts; and have the thread as thick as to fill the nostril hole.
And some of the ancient restorers said to immerse the threads in butter, changing them every day. That however does not seem right to me; instead the thread should be soaked in the white of an egg, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the thread until the bone hardens and the cartilage strengthens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of the thread, after it is wrapped in soft cloth; this holds the broken nose more firmly so that the patient can still breathe.
This thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do Licinius's work.
If it happens that, while operating on a hot swelling, the nose of Naso becomes inflamed again, apply to his nostrils either a mustard plaster or a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil, or daiquiri.
If the inflammation does not occur then it is necessary that you apply plasters made with sesame flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white. Next put a soft dressing on top of that; and do not bandage his nose at all.
However if Naso's bones are broken or shattered by a small fracture then you must cut open above the break and extract those bits with suitable instruments. Then sew the wound shut; and care for it with whatever promotes healing and consolidation of the flesh such as suitable plasters or ointments.
If ever there is an injury between the nostrils then you must treat it with strips of linen; and insert a lead pipe until healed.
On restoring the lower jaw when it is shattered:
When the lower jaw is shattered and there are no other injuries; then if its fracture is external only and not split into two parts, then identifying it should be straightforward.
If the fracture is on the right side then insert your left index finger into the injured person's jaw; likewise if it is their left jaw that is fractured then use the index finger of your right hand instead: and with your other hand push inwards from behind towards the outside at the swelling of the fracture, pushing gently until you feel the bones meet.
If the fracture of the jaw has already separated into two pieces, you must perform an extension from both sides according to its straightness until it can be equated.
If there is already damage to the teeth or they have been displaced, then fasten those you hope will remain with gold wire, silver, or silk thread. Then place pulp on the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and on the cloth put a large unevenly shaped tile or a piece of leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above according to how you have prepared the bandage for him and its application is suitable until the jaw is no longer broken; advise the patient of calmness and rest; and give him soft, easy-to-swallow food.
If you think the shape has changed to some extent, then be quick and remove it on the third day. Then correct that which has been changed and make a plaster with flour of barley mixed with egg white or sesame seed flour after you have removed the pulp; and place on the plaster some soft wool.
While that plaster remains attached to it without changing the position of the bone, leave it in place and do not remove it until healed or until the fracture is secure. This fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If there is a warm abscess in the meantime, then apply that which we have said many times for its reduction until it is removed.
And if there's a fracture with a wound, consider it. Now when the piece of bone has been separated from the limb, either by sawing or cutting; then you must make use of the appropriate fine instruments to remove those pieces of sawdust which suit your purpose for their removal.
If however the wound opening is tight, then widen it with a lancet according to the extent of your need.
Then when you have removed the sawdust and none remains of it, sew up the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on top of it a plaster that suits this purpose, and apply flesh to it until it heals.
On restoring the fork when broken.
The fork is indeed most often broken on the anterior part of the arm; and its fracture occurs according to one of three modes.
For it is broken either into two pieces without any sawdust getting caught in it; and thus its restoration is easier.
Or that sawdust gets caught up within the bone; and its restoration is difficult.
Or that there's a fracture with an open wound alongside it.
When a fork is fractured without an open wound, the procedure is as follows: you must have two assistants; one of them holds the broken fork while the other extends their arm towards the side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct and even; and there should be neither prominence nor hollows.
If you need more extension, then you should place underneath the patient's heel a pad made of cloth or wool; its size should be according to your needs; extend and raise the fork, and shorten the pad with your hand until the fracture is reduced as required.
If you cannot draw the end of the fork outwards because it has come out too deep, then the patient should be made to lie face down over his own neck; place under his shoulder a pillow of medium size and have an assistant press his shoulder downwards until the bone of the fork that is buried deeply is raised up; then reduce the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel slippage of the fork and it is moving, then you should cut open above it and remove that displaced part with ease.
If it is retained by the bone, then introduce into its incision one of the knives you have prepared for this purpose; after you place under the fork a protector, and the protector is either wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no hollow.
Its width should be according to the size of bone you need it for and its fragility. Its length should also be according to what is possible for you in the procedure.
It should have two ends as you see it: one end is wide, the other narrow.
If however the wound you opened when removing that displaced part of the bone is large and you are afraid of abscess because it is warm, then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the rupture is small or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and plugs according to the size of the injury's tear.
If a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of the abscess.
However, if there is a bone fracture without any wound or break, you should apply a plaster made from flour and egg white to the bone. And place soft rags on top of it. Then put a ball under their armpit if they need it. Next take a hemmeter, which is the wheel that the Saracens use and which is very long; its width should be one palm or around that size. Then place a doubled plug on top of the rags and plaster. Next take a thin board whose width is three fingers wide and its length likewise. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the board in it around the place of the hemmeter which lies over the fracture and site. Then place the hemmeter over the fracture as it was rolled up and wrap it around their neck, under their healthy armpit, and then under their injured armpit; bring it back to cover all parts where you see that the bone has been misaligned. It should not be concealed from view above and should cover everything so that the board is not removed from over the broken bone. And for this reason, you should look after the sick person every day. Whenever you see the bone already starting to move away; then straighten it out and tighten the ligament.
Then place the patient's pillow under his back and put another small pillow at night under their head to elevate it on that side. Raise the fracture of the forearm by raising up the shoulder.
Or bind the patient's arm to their neck and do not release this bandage unless there is an accident at that site due to itching or from a sore until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster if you see its surface has become exposed. And add to the bandage and leave it until it is healed and hardened.
The fracture of the forearm is most often bound up and strengthened within twenty-eight days, sometimes sooner for some patients.
### On healing a Fracture of the Jawbone.
The jaw is rarely broken except over its widest part; but if it does break then only the edge of it breaks off.
When the jaw is broken at this site and breaks right through its middle so that it cannot be seen from outside; then according to where exactly the break lies, you must try to set it in place. And restore its shape as much as your ingenuity allows you to do so.
Then place over the wound powder from a mill with egg white, and soft lint, and cover it all with a little pad made of doubled cloth. Then place over it a flat splint made of thin wood according to the size of the whole spatula, or slightly larger.
If there is any hollow under the splint in one of the spatula's places, then fill that hollow evenly with soft lint until the splint sits level.
Then bind it tightly from above with a long bandage of the proper tightness and firmness to hold the splint in place until it does not move.
And check the binding every day. Whenever the binding becomes soft, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it has moved from its place; also have the patient lie on their healthy side.
The splint will heal within twenty or twenty-five days.
When that number of days is complete, then you may remove the binding; and you will be safe. For it is made of bones whose fracture or dislocation need not be feared.
If however, a piece of bone projects from it and pierces the skin, then split it open over that spot and remove the projecting piece. And do as we said with the fracture of the collarbone for a warm poultice from an aposteme if something similar happens.
On restoring fractures of the ribs.
The chest sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and this rarely happens. Its limbs indeed break and shatter the most from its accidents.
When its middle breaks, which slopes downwards to the lower parts, and it suffers severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and a cough; perhaps vomiting blood, with concavity evident on the broken bone, and not hidden from perception, its restoration is for the patient to lie on their back with a pillow placed between their shoulder blades.
Then press their shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides with your hands; and carefully align it as evenly as possible and appropriate until the shape of the bone returns as it should be.
Then place a plaster and a poultice on them, and put above it a star made of thin wooden board from willow or hazel, and similarly light ones, after you wrap it in cloth. Then carefully bind it above the broken bone so that it is not moved; and go around with the binding according to the roundness towards the back several times, tightening it with a suitable bandage.
Then check the bandage every hour and whenever it loosens, tighten it; and if its need provokes its removal due to itching, or corrosion which occurs in that place, or pain, or abscess: then hurry, and remove it, take off the plaster, and address what happens with an appropriate treatment method.
Then bring back the plaster if you see its face; and attach to it a bandage until it is healed.
The restoration of ribs when they are broken.
You know that a rib fracture does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the back and at their ends from the front part. And they do not suffer dislocation because of this, since they are made of cartilage. Recognition of it is not hidden from the sense of touch during examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in this way: equally align the fracture with your fingers as best you can until the shape is equalised as it should be. Next apply plastering, and bind the broken bone with a splint if it needs one.
And if the rib fracture is towards the inside of the body, then the patient experiences severe pain and piercing, like that which occurs in pleurisy, because the bone pierces the lung, and they also experience difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting a lot of blood: and this condition is difficult to treat.
Ancient physicians have devised many techniques for this condition, and among them are those who say that you should feed the patient foods which cause inflammation and wind, so as to inflame the abdomen and push it outwards, thus forcing the fracture towards the outside.
We however refrain from doing anything which might cause a quick-forming hot abscess if it does not happen. But if it has already occurred, then an addition is made to it.
Some of them said to put something windy on the place, then suck it with strength, and this is more similar to logic. However, there is a fear that it may draw superfluous humors to the site due to its weakness-inducing properties.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the site should be covered with wool already soaked in warm oil; and let wads be placed between the ribs until full, so as to have an equal binding when you wrap it around according to its roundness.
Then the patient should be treated with treatment for pleurisy from food and medicine.
If the illness afflicts the patient severely in a way that is not tolerated and the wound stings with a harmful jab, and we fear for the patient; then it is necessary to cut open above the site, and expose the broken rib. Then we should place above it the instrument that preserves healing, whose description preceded; and cut the rib with ease, and also cut said instrument.
Then we should bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with stitches; and we should treat it with plaster until it heals.
If meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then hurry and insert wads into rose oil, and place it on the site; and let the patient be treated with what is appropriate for an abscess.
Meanwhile they should lie on their side of the wound until it heals.
On the restoration of spinal fractures in the back and neck.
The bones of the neck sometimes suffer fracture, and rarely do they undergo attrition; similarly with spinal vertebrae in the back. When it happens to someone and you want to know whether they will be healed or not, then consider the following... If you see their hand already softened and numb, lifeless; they cannot move it or stretch it out nor clench it, and when you squeeze it with your fingers and prick it with a needle they feel nothing and experience no pain: then know that this person will not heal, and in most cases this is fatal.
But if they can move it and feel the squeeze of your fingers and the prick of a needle then know that the bone's neck is still intact and this patient will heal with treatment.
If it happens in the vertebrae of their back and you want to know if they will heal or not then consider their feet; if they are also soft and lifeless like their hands were, then when this person is lying on their back air escapes them without control, and when they are on their stomach waste comes out without control: when they turn onto their back again they cannot urinate although wanting to: know that this is fatal. Therefore do not trouble yourself with treating them.
If none of these things happen to this person then the case is less severe.
To treat this patient you should try to reduce the hot abscess's inflammation by putting rose oil alone on a rag over the affected vertebra or with roast lamb entrails.
Do this several times a day until the hot abscess is reduced; then put one of the tightening, drying plasters on it and bind it with a bandage, and instruct the patient to rest and keep calm. He must not sleep on top of the side where he finds pain until it is healed.
If this happens when there is erosion of the bone or something which has already come away from it: then one must cut above the skin and remove it. Then draw together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitches. Then treat it with some red ointment until it heals.
But if the last bone of the leg is broken and it's the end of the tailbone: then one must put their left thumb into the anus, and with the other hand equalise the broken bone as far as possible according to what alignment you achieve.
Then place something over it and remove it; and treat the wound with whatever treatment came before until it heals.

On the setting of fractures in the arm.
The splint is that which lies between the elbow and the head of the spatula.
