If the fracture is close to the shoulder joint then a ligament should be placed under the armpit and another beneath the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is close to the elbow then a ligament should be placed on that spot and over the actual elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture easily without difficulty until it is aligned as it ought to be and heals well; then tighten it if no hot inflammation occurs.
But if hot inflammation does occur then release the tight bandage until the seventh day. And place over it a dressing of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the inflammation quiets; then tighten it again with an ointment on the fracture.
Then place a bandage made from new cloth over the plaster. Then bring the arm alongside for support, and place their open hand over their shoulder.
Place a cloth and bind it tightly over the supporting arm, so that the arm stays in place as splints: if nothing prevents you from doing this, nor will anything shift within your broken bone.
If you fear that something might shift within your broken bone, then use splints: and it is best to place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between the width of all splints a finger's breadth: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an additional three finger-breadths on each side.
Then bind tightly over the splints using the method I described at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is important that your binding be tighter over the site of the fracture. And the more extended the fracture is, the less tight should your binding be.
If therefore you see that the placement of the splints and binding is as we have described, then do this when restoring the limb immediately: and if you fear a hot abscess, then loosen the binding and remove the splints until the seventh day, as I said before.
Then check the binding on all three days, so that an itching or swelling does not occur at the site, nor is food prevented from reaching the limb due to excess binding. Therefore correct it all according as I have said.
If, however, it is not certain that something will happen, then do not remove the ligature until after three days or more.
And if the patient is sleeping on his back with his hands over his stomach, place under the support cushion full of wool evenly.
And consider every hour of the night or day to ensure that the position of the broken limb is not altered nor the ligature loosened. Therefore, correct all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier; let the first foods be soft until his jaw begins to tighten, and then you must thicken his diet.
For it is the custom that a splint and crutch should be worn for forty days; at this point, it must be removed, he should have a bath, and be anointed with ointments that straighten the limb.
But if the fracture is foul and crushed, do not remove from it the splint and wooden wedges until fifty days or even two months.
On the restoration of the broken limb of the arm.
The arm is made up of two bones which are called the forearm bones.
One of these is small, being the one that follows the thumb. The other is large; it lies beneath the smaller bone below.
Perhaps only the larger forearm bone is broken, or perhaps the smaller one, or maybe they are both broken together.
When therefore the lower jaw is broken in a minor fracture, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower jaw is broken in a major fracture, its injury is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it gets worse when two bones break at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the lower jaw in a minor fracture, then it behooves the physician to attend to its restoration by applying gentle traction until it is even.
If however it is the large lower jaw that has been broken, then he must apply a very strong traction to restore its position.
And he must place the palm of his hand in position for its restoration, with an extended stretch above the pillow. And let the thumb of his hand be higher than all the fingers; and let it act as a joint to all the other fingers, or at least some of them: and let the sick person sit square before himself, with the pillow in front of him, or directly beneath him, at such a height that he incurs no effort.
Then the attendant must extend the arm from below with either his hand or a cord. And another attendant must likewise stretch it from above.
Then the physician should level the bone until he reduces its position to as good a figure as possible.
If there is bending to the bone fracture, then strive to bring every piece back into its place by your might.
If however you find the dislocation to be separated and it punctures through the skin, with no hope of restoration for you, then bind it up, and remove it according to what we have described above.
If there is a fracture with a wound, I have already done that in a separate chapter, so take the cure from there.
If at the beginning of your restoration a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil, and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth onto it with a tight bandage until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared with flour of millstones and egg whites. Then apply splints, and let the splint that is placed over the fracture itself be a little wider, and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases: whether there be a fracture in one shaft or two shafts together.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site more tightly and forcefully, and somewhat tighter again with the bandage above or below the fracture: or apply a softer bandage to it slightly looser than its tightness as mentioned at the start of this chapter.
And let the cloths that wrap around the fracture be soft and moist: and not hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be linen, and medium between thin and thick, as we have described.
And consider the limb and its ligaments after a day. If there is something that requires correction, such as itching of the limb: then you must embrocate the limb with warm water until the itching subsides, and release the limb from its bandage until it calms down, then repeat the binding.
If the tightness has already softened and the bone is removed, and similar matters to these: then straighten it all with your skill, and consider again.
If nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, then you must soften it somewhat and leave it for a few days until the nourishment reaches it, afterwards tighten it.
If none of the things we have mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days or thereabouts.
Then the patient's hand should be hung from his neck. And let his neck be straight, and it should be kept steady by his skill to avoid violent movements. Make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture heals within thirty days or thirty-two days and perhaps it is healed after twenty-eight days. All of this is according to the dispositions of one's constitution and their skill.
On the healing of broken bones in the hand.
Know that broken bones rarely happen in the knuckles of the hand or at the joints of the fingers. It does not happen to it unless there is repeated attrition (i.e., rubbing together).
So when there is a fracture or attrition of the heel bone (i.e., calcaneus), then the patient should sit square and opposite him/her should be placed an equal seat. Then place his hand on it, extended.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones, and the doctor should align them until they join with a good connection.
At this point, it is necessary to apply the plaster cast and bandage, unless a hot abscess occurs. Place the splint over it according to the size of the area, you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
However, if the fracture is in the lower part of the foot near the sole, then make a ball out of cloth and ask the patient to press his broken foot against it; afterwards it should be secured with a long piece of linen.
The splint should be made of soft leather so that it conforms to the shape of the foot; tighten it as required.
If the fracture is on the outside, you should place a splint over it and another underneath at the sole of the foot; make sure that the hand is extended and open. Then place bandages as you would for a hand injury; extend the binding between the toes.
If any of the toe joints is broken, if it is the big toe then align as required. Afterwards it should be secured with the foot; if necessary, apply a straight splint to prevent any elevation of the fracture and ensure that there is no movement.
If the other toes are broken (such as the middle, index, little or ring toes), then they should be aligned and bound to the next healthy toe; alternatively all of them can be bound together which is better. Place a small asterisk on it as we mentioned before, on the thumb.
Consider the restoration process and following your arrangement of the warm poultice: address any complications that arise as described in his account.
On the restoration of hip fractures.
The hip bone often breaks and is easily detected because it twists towards the inner and back part of the body.
To restore this injury, bind a bandage above the fracture and another beneath it. Let the patient lie prostrate on their face.
The assistant then stretches every bandage to its limit on each side evenly if the break is through the middle of the bone.
However, if the break is near the hip bone's root, a bandage should be placed at the ilium so that its stretch goes upwards and another beneath the fracture.
Similarly, if it is close to the knee, place the bandage near the knee so its stretch goes downwards.
The doctor should then align the bone ends with his hands until it returns to its original shape and join them in a good union.
Apply a plaster cast and bandages if no infection develops in the limb. If an infection develops there, you should leave it for a few days until the warm swelling subsides before returning to your treatment.
Its stricture should be such. You must roll up the bandage over the fracture of the dura mater; that is, you should double it twice or three times, and there should be a remainder from its surplus. Then roll up the leg until you reach the heel at the root of the buttock.
And introduce a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee to the underside, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper part of both parts. Then roll up over the leg and thigh what remains from the other bandage.
Then place over the thigh at the site of its fracture some splints, and put one splint above the leg bone.
Then fill the gaps between the leg and thigh with soft cloths until you equalize the tightness.
Then start with a strong bandaging from the middle over the site of fracture by three or four turns. And the more you extend with the bandage from the site of fracture, let your binding be tighter and softer, and gentler.
Then reach the two ends of the thread that you introduced between the thigh and leg. Tie with both [ends] three splints which are above. Then go with both ends of the thread to the underside until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both, bind also the ends of the splints from the other side so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Release the constriction over it while it remains, so that there is no corrosion of the limb, nor abscesses, nor inflammation, and similar things to them. If anything should happen as a result of this. Wash it with its remedy and correct whatever happens as a result entirely, just as we have said many times.
But if there is a splinter of bone from the bone that is protruding: then you must cut it out equally, as much as you can. And if not, place something over it, and remove it; and treat the person whose symptoms have occurred before, until they are cured.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is compressed, besides which splints are added to it, as we have said about the arm and shoulder.
However, when this fracture is restored, there is no limp in the patient. And if it is restored alone, apart from the fact that another leg is applied to it, then there must always be a limp in the person whose it is.
And know that the hip is compressed within fifty days, or a little more, or less; all of this is according to variations in constitutions and other conditions.
### On the restoration of the fracture of the knee cap.
Know that the knee cap rarely experiences fracture but is subject to wear many times.
If it does experience fracture, then there is no other than either a crack or a hole in its parts. And it happens with a wound and without a wound. And he stands over it whole with his senses.
And its restoration is such that what has been broken of its parts will be levelled with fingers until it is united and joined as well as possible by levelling and smoothing, and shaping.
Then place a plaster of sesame flour or mill flour with egg white: and put over it a round wooden board, if you need one: and press down upon it with a binding suitable for that purpose.
Then consider all the treatments we described for other fractures: such as hot inflammation and similar conditions; so that you apply to every incident whatever is suitable for it until it heals.
 On restoring the fracture of a leg.
Know that there are two leg bones, one of which is thick and named the shin bone; and another fine one, called the calf bone.
And it happens to both of them by type as what happens to the two bones of the arm; and because of this its restoration is equal to that of an arm, and the operation is one and the same.
If both bones break at once, the leg turns into all pieces.
And if the finer bone breaks, the leg turns forward.
And if the thicker bone breaks you will find it lower down; and it is one of those that are not hidden from view.
Then administer extension, equality and binding of the stars equally according to that.
However, if the leg fracture is a severe one, with many ligaments involved, your extension should be smaller and gentler.
And in the leg there is something from the operation over the arm, and it is such that when you equalize the stars and complete all your work, then take two pinewood splints used to make roof tiles placed under the rafters; choose those which are neither too thick nor too thin, and let their length be according to the leg's length from knee down.
Then wrap each splint with two layers of bandage according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the opposite; let them be from knee down to foot.
Then bind the two splints together at three places: both ends, and midway. For with the binding in place the leg sags to both sides and maintains good position.
Now let there be made a cradle of wood according to the leg's length; place the leg in it so that it may keep itself from moving.
Indeed one must make sure to do this for the fracture when there is an open wound as well.
Then check the leg every day, and pay close attention to any infection, inflammation or other complications. If any of these occur, then treat it with what is needed until the leg heals, God willing.
On fractures of the bones of the foot and toes.
The heel bone is not fractured at all.
The bones of the foot sometimes become fractured, and also there is a slight fracture of the toes; but they are never broken except by attrition in various places.
But if the bones of the foot are fractured and you see these bones raised one above the other then let the patient put his/her foot on a prepared piece of ground as when walking.
Then stand yourself and place your foot on that which is raised up from these bones; afterwards press it, or stamp on it; and equalise them until they return to their places.
Then place on it a plaster cast and some cloth above that; then place under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat ends.
Then bind it together with appropriate bandages after you have wrapped it up with cloths and other things as required.
When therefore three or four days have passed then untie the bandage. For you will find that the bones have become level if they were broken or dislocated. So therefore there is treatment for them as you see.
But if some of the toes are broken then restore them and equalise them as I have described to you for restoration of the fingers of a hand. Then place a splint alongside the toe according to its length and let it be slightly wider than that; then put under this foot the board which I described to you before and bind them with appropriate bandages.
If the bone that is broken consists of two pieces or three or more, then place a splint of appropriate size over each finger wrapped in linen cloth, and bandage it to a board underneath the foot; let one end of the board extend beyond the stomach of the foot so as to hold [the splints] firmly.
And it is necessary for you not to forget that you should consider all the things I have said about other types of fractures from the accidents we mentioned; and apply them as appropriate in each case.
Regarding the fracture of a woman's collarbone and a man's ribcage...
When a woman's collarbone is broken, make her sit on a square-shaped seat. Then tilt her slightly towards the side of her back; let someone hold her from behind.
The midwife should then fill her collarbone with cotton wool until it is completely full; the collarbone will become like a sphere as a result.
The woman should then be shaken slightly; her back must be raised gradually afterwards. The cotton wool will emerge from this side until it becomes a sphere in front of the collarbone; the broken bone will be rejoined as a result.
Then place a little pillow on her back. When she wants to urinate, the cotton wool should be removed gently; let her go to the toilet and then replace it as you first put it inside; afterwards, she can return to her pillow.
She should do this for seven days or so; the bone will be restored in that time.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and bind over its opening a reed tube, and insert the entire bladder into his anus. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside his rectum, for it will burst again.
Then fill it with cottage cheese and let it remain there for several days, as we have described, until he is healed.
And when the bone of a man's or woman's pelvis breaks, then apply what we have described for the repair and equalization of the pubic bone.
And the anus is not concealed by these nearby fractures which are of little consequence, especially to someone who has some interest or use for it.
And understand this book of mine very well because the fracture itself shows you, in many cases, a straight path to repairing and tightening it: so understand it.
When a man's penis is broken, take the gullet of a goose: and insert the penis inside it. Then place over it a wrap made of cloth, and it should be bandaged up and left for three days or thereabouts, and he will recover if God wills it.
On the repair of bone fractures when they are accompanied by wounds.
It is appropriate for someone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large like the bone of the hip, or the supporting bones, or something similar to them, that you hasten and immediately have him bled by a phlebotomist; if he meets the conditions for bleeding as we described above.
If the wound discharges blood, then you must promptly proceed to its excision, applying crushed zege powder onto it, if no other [powder] is available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not postpone it unless an abscess occurs.
But if there is a hot abscess, then postpone the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess settles down. And do not approach it on the third day or fourth day at all, because you would cause bad things to happen to it.
However if the broken bone is protruding through the skin then it must be attempted to reduce it by aligning it with your hands gently and with minimal extension.
If however its extension is not prepared for, nor its alignment with your hands then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron with a length of seven or eight fingers and its width according to the size of the wound. And for this reason it is necessary that the physician take three or four according to how many he needs of them for each type of fracture treatment.
And it is round with a small thickness so as not to double up during compression on it at the time of operation and has sharp edges with bevelled ends. And its upper half is thickened from the middle to its lower end.
And this is its shape.
In this operation it is necessary that the curved sharp end be placed over the protruding bone and push with it in one motion until the bone returns to alignment.
Attempt to align the fracture's ends with each other.
If its end is broken too finely and does not take the tool well, then you must cut down that bone's end until the tool has a good purchase on it.
But if you cannot reduce them with what we have described, then cut it open with one of the knives that suits it best, as we said before; or saw through it with any one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then smooth out what remains of roughnesses and small splinters from the bone.
When the bone has been put back and the patient afterwards experiences severe pain, you know that it is not in its proper place again.
Do so, then. For you give the patient great relief when your restoration of the bone is complete.
Then immerse some cloth in black styptic wine. And especially if it is summer. And do not apply any sort of poultice to the wound nor anything oily; so that it does not cause rot and corruption there.
Then give your patient aster leaves an hour after you have finished restoring the bone. And leave the wound open; so as to cut an opening with scissors into the wrappings according to the size of the injury.
And take every care not to bind up the wound tight around the fracture. Many times indeed have these foolish physicians done it: they caused to happen over their patients either death or decay, or suppuration, that is, pus. And if your fracture is soft, gentle, different from other types of fractures.
But if the wound is bad or a large one, and you fear some of the complications we described might occur, and you find pain in the suspended site; then it should not be necessary to put weights on top. And make for them rolls of hard cloth at the place of the weights, and tighten it with these.
However, after one or two days when you see the wound begin to generate pus; then take away from it the cloth which you placed on top with wine. Then apply liniments and ointments with which we are accustomed to treating wounds: such as the four-ingredient ointment, and similar ones.
It is appropriate for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every morning and evening until it is healed and consolidated.
And the limb should be prepared in such a way that pus may flow easily from it downwards.
But if many days pass by the wound and it does not consolidate nor is the pus separated from it; then you may know there are small splinters of bone within. Why you must probe the wound with a tent. How many of those small splinters have been removed, take them out and extract them. And those that are not separated from them and pierce the limb and cause pain, try to cut into them, and remove them in every way possible for you.
If a wound occurs with an abscess or ulceration, that is, with a tumor, or some other sign of corruption and putrefaction: then you should apply to each one of them what corresponds to it regarding its treatment, the description of which has been narrated earlier in its own chapter.
And from this, in what my speech should stand on, and to which you should direct your attention when a bone is broken and protrudes beyond the skin, like the bones of the hip, the arm or similar large members: it is that you must not presume its traction, for many times death occurs from it; rather leave it alone until it rots.
Indeed, perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound if it seems appropriate to you; otherwise, leave it be.
On the treatment of swelling in the track of a fracture.
This swelling often occurs along the track of healing from a fracture. Especially when it is close to the joints; and the shape of the limb is distorted by it. And perhaps prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Therefore, consider that if the swelling is recent, you should apply medicines with astringent properties to it, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, gum Arabic, acacia, and similar substances.
Take some or all of them; prepare them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the swelling wrapped in lint, securing them firmly with a good bandage.
Leave the bandage in place and do not remove it for many days. Then loosen it and apply another one, until the swelling is gone.
Or bandage over it a piece of lead foil. For lead has the property of drawing out any noxious matter from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone and is bound up, and there's a pressing need to remove it quickly: then make an opening above its upper part, and cut off any protruding mass; or scrape it with some scrapers until it is gone; and treat it until it heals.
Now concerning treatment when the limb is restored but remains thin, not according to its nature.
When the fracture of bone is healed but the limb remains thin and weak: this happens for many reasons. One reason is an excess of the solution used for bandaging and tying it up not being as it should be; either through over-tightening of the bandage so that nourishment cannot reach the limb; or too much ointment applied at the wrong time; or because there's little blood in the patient's body, and weakness.
Treatment is to feed the patient nourishing food, and make his body soft so that the blood multiplies within him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness to him.
Then place a peg on the limb: so that it will draw pus towards itself in great quantities. And frequently anoint him with warm water until the pus runs out and his limb returns to its natural form.
On curing broken bones when they have healed crookedly.
When it happens that a limb which has already healed after its cure of crookedness and projection of the broken bone or knotting, and is disfigured by it, but nevertheless the limb does not prevent from its natural movement: then you ought not to listen to him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who did that in our country.
And this operation is truly blameworthy, leading to death or great fear so that the patient be terrified of dying.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that he be anointed with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as althea leaves, and its root, and *mellilot*, and those like them. And make for him a plaster with softening plasters, such as *dyachylon* done decently.
Or take the mucilage of althea root, and mix it with goose fat or sesame oil: and plaster him with it.
They should take plump figs and mix them with pigeon dung and similar substances which are called among medical men bone-softening agents.
And sometimes the knot unties itself with steady, ancient, gentle friction which is done with moist hands. One should move the limb to every side at all hours of the day.
But if the curvature has already advanced and become strong and turned into a stone-like hardness, and it is necessary to treat it with iron, then one should cut above it from the top and detach the connected bone, and cut away what is superfluous of the knot or bone with delicate Incisors; and ease in that be applied with care and diligence.
On dislocation (lit. 'joint-displacement').
A dislocation is the displacement of any joints from its place. Because it is prohibited from movement and defaces the figure of the limb. And it causes severe pain and injuries to occur in the weak person.
Therefore when it happens to someone that a dislocation has occurred, one should rush to restore it and not delay at all.
For if the treatment is delayed, then the place becomes calloused and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
For this reason it should not be moved nor stretched at the time of its callousing. For it often causes spasms and painful injuries to occur.
Indeed, when that happens, one must hasten to perform a bloodletting on the patient. Then they should be discharged until the abscess subsides.
Afterwards, the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then they should be discharged until the abscess subsides.
And every limb should be treated with whatever comes to mind when recalled in its proper place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocations, as well as those that came before regarding fractures, from the upper to the lower parts of the body.
On the treatment for dislocation of the mandible.
Two jaws rarely dislocate unless they do so to a slight degree.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. Either they are removed slightly from their places, or they become somewhat softened. Or they are completely dislocated until the parts soften towards the chest; when it reaches the patient's saliva and they cannot hold onto that or close their mouth, and their speech is wrapped up with their tongue.
But when its dislocation is slight, it returns to place by itself in many positions with little trouble.
If however it is completely or fully dislocated: then one must hasten to its reduction and not delay at all; the minister should hold the patient's head and the physician insert the thumb of their hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between their teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. He should insert both his thumbs at once if the dislocation is from two sides. And the rest of his fingers should be kept outside, with which he tests (it).
And tell the patient to clean his jaws and thoroughly rinse them out for eating on all sides. And the doctor should test (the jaw) and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its repositioning is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved: then apply a hot fomentation with water and oil until the repositioning of both becomes easy; and do not delay the repositioning of both at all, as we have said.
When they therefore return and are tested, and the patient's mouth is closed, and there is no softening: then place over them pads of cloth with a cerate made of wax; and rose-scented oil.
Then it should be bound gently with soft bandages.
And the patient should sleep on his back; and his head should be made to rest between two pillows so that he cannot move it from side to side; nor should he attempt or be forced to chew anything: rather, his food should be given by gentle sucking until such time as the pains subside and the jaw is healed; then let him eat what he wishes and give it to him without difficulty.
Let him not work on the task of opening his mouth at mealtimes and drinking, nor yawn, until the jaw is healed and restored to health.
But if the repositioning of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places; then from this many fevers often occur with a continuous discharge, and perhaps the patient's stomach becomes relaxed, or perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When you see that, then know it is a lost cause, and many times the one afflicted dies within ten days.
On the reduction of dislocations of the fork-bone and shoulder joint.
The fork-bone does not dislocate from the inner side due to its continuity with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outwards, which is evident by the sense of touch.
And its restoration is for the patient to lie on their back and stretch out their arms. Then squeeze the area with your palm firmly, for it will return to its place.
After that, apply a plaster and splints on top of it, and bind them tightly.
The extremities that follow the elbow, and which are connected to it, do not dislocate unless rarely. If it has dislocated at any point in time, then it must be reduced and aligned as we have said, and as is prepared for you.
After that, apply a plaster and splints on top of it, and apply tight bandaging. And instruct the patient to adhere to rest and tranquility until they have healed.
And with this same treatment the shoulder joint is reduced when it too has become separated from its place.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder joint.
You know that the humerus is dislocated only in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the side of the coracoid process on the lower side. The second is that it is dislocated toward the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated towards the upper part of the humerus, which happens rarely.
It does not dislocate backwards because of the shoulder blade. It also cannot be dislocated forward due to nerves.
It indeed tends to be most often dislocated and exits towards the lower side of the coracoid process, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. Since it exits quickly from them and also enters rapidly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it happens contrary to that, namely because it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps there are some people who suffer a blow or fall and the humerus becomes swollen with a hot swelling, which is thought to be because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore it is necessary that you examine this until you are certain of its cause, and then proceed to treat it.
A dislocation is recognized when it is towards the lower side of the coracoid process, and you must distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. For you find an obvious difference between them and discover in the head of the dislocated humerus concavity, and under the coracoid process, near where the head of the humerus should be, as if it were an egg; and the sick person cannot lower their hand to their ear nor move it with all its motions.
Similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, then you will find a clear indication by touch, and it will not be hidden.
The reduction of this particular dislocation is easily achieved when it is recent or the patient is a weak child.
The reduction method involves the assistant lifting their hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under their armpit and lift the joint with strength upwards to its original position; meanwhile, the assistant lifts their hand and stretches it upward. Then lay them down; for they return quickly on their own.
However, if it does not return as described and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred; then it is necessary for the patient to bathe in warm water and be given an embrocation that softens and soothes, such as when Althea roots, fenugreek, and meadowsweet are boiled in water and administered.
Then the patient should lie on their back; and under their armpit should be placed a sphere of wool of equal parts softness and firmness.
Then the physician should place their heel on top of the sphere and lift the patient's shoulder with strength while pulling their arm downward.
If you wish, reduce it in this way; and that is for a taller man to be present who stands on the patient's side and inserts his shoulder under their armpit: then he should lift the patient's armpit upward until they are suspended in midair. The other assistant should pull the patient's hand toward the lower part of their abdomen. If the patient is slight in build, then something else must be hung from them to weigh them down; for otherwise the dislocation will recur immediately.
It can also be done in another way: by fixing into the ground a long pole whose head is round like a pestle handle; this should not be either too thick or too thin. Then the patient should be placed on top of soft cloths that have been laid out under them, with their body aligned along the length of the pole.
Next his hand should be pulled toward the lower part of his body from one side, while on the other his whole body should be extended with force; for in this way the joint quickly returns to its proper place.
If all the above-mentioned methods prove ineffective for reducing it, then try this treatment: take a pole whose length is equal to twice your forearm's length, its width equivalent to four fingers, and its thickness two fingers; this should have a rounded head like the one shown in the diagram below.
Then soft cloths should be wrapped around its rounded head to prevent it from injuring the patient.
After that the pole should be placed under the patient's dislocated joint so as to extend their hand or arm down onto it and thus reach its lower end; then tie the pole firmly in place, securing both the assisting hand/arm and the injured limbs extremity.
Then the patient's arm should be placed along the width of one step in a ladder, their hand extending down to its lower edge; while on the other side their body remains suspended as before. The junction enters immediately.
Therefore when its opening is complete in any possible way, you must place underneath the needle a woolen sphere of equal quantity. Next place the prepared plaster made of flour powder with incense and egg white all over the shoulder like it rolls down from above.
Then bind it underneath the needle with a suitable bandage, and roll its strap over the plaster from above; his hand is tied to his neck, and [the patient] is left without moving their hand for seven days.
The patient's diet should be restricted until the limb regains strength, for this expedites its healing.
Then after seven or five days an elastic bandage should be put on, and movement exercises are performed. If the limb is firm and not soft then it is already healed.
If the joint is dislocated repeatedly due to moisture or for another reason then a three-hole caustic should be applied as described above regarding caustics.
If all this is done and the bandage removed after seven days but the joint isn't firm and plaster and bandages are reapplied several times without it becoming firm and falls again and cannot raise itself above then you know that its nerves near the head of the shoulder have been severed or stretched or softened, and you will know that the joint never be set in place.
As for a dislocation towards the chest and breast or one that is reduced to the back then it is done with an impulse and stretching using hands until it returns; then the rest of the bandage and treatment are applied until healed.
If, after recovery, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use a bath frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original nature.
On the cure for dislocation of the elbow.
You should know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty too; it can be dislocated in all directions, but especially forward or backward.
And its dislocation will not be hidden from you; for it falls under sight and touch, whichever way it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with a healthy one, it will be evident to you. For the joint will be bent, and it cannot bend its arm or touch its shoulder with it.
And you must hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess occurs in it.
For if an abscess occurs in it, its reduction will be difficult; and perhaps it may not heal at all, especially when it is at the back. It is worse than all other forms of dislocations and causes greater pain; and frequently death comes with it.
And when its restoration is possible among those that can be reduced, it should be done by extending the patient's hand with both hands; and their arm should be spread out, and the doctor's hands are above and below the elbow; and he pushes on the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is forward: then he returns so that he doubles up his hand with its own hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which has been dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a strong and violent extension: it is for two assistants to extend the arm; and let two assistants also hold it so that it cannot be moved during the extension.
Then the arm is turned around to every part after a folded cloth or a wide bandage has been wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should grease it with oil so that it is one of those things which assist in lubricating the joint with ease. Then he should push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
And after its reduction it must be placed over it a plaster which contains an astringent and a drying agent with egg white, and it should be bound with a suitable bandage. And the arm is to be suspended from the patient's neck, and left there for several days.
Then it is released. So if the joint remains firmly in place then remove the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not properly bound then repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it binds. Then release it.
If however there is weight and stiffness after the reduction of the joint and it is fixed in movement: then apply moistening in bathing and gentle friction, and compression until it is eased or place over the joint fat from the rump, that is, rich lamb. Then it should be bound and left in place for a day and night. Next remove the splint, and immerse it into a bath. Once it has sweated, then rub the affected area with tempered friction liniment. Then repeat the process with an eased voice once, twice, thrice with immersion in a bath until it softens.
And if you wish, apply cow dung that is moist, warmed with butter, and wrap it around the affected area.
Do this several times. For it will soften and return to its natural state.
 On the treatment of dislocations by Al-Mahas.
The hand (which is connected to the arm by way of joint) often becomes dislocated.
And reducing its dislocation is easy. Unlike that of the other joints. However it is necessary that the speed of its reduction be done within the hour in which it was dislocated before swelling sets in; otherwise a hot abscess will occur.
And reducing the dislocation is done by placing the weak hand of Al-Mahas on a table and having an assistant extend their arm; and then the physician will place their palm over the joint's prominence and push it until it returns to its normal position.
But it should be considered that if the palm has already receded towards the belly of the hand then place the weak person's back (of their) hand on the table for its extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding toward the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be internally on the table, i.e., the inner part of the hand should be placed on the table so that the physician's hand falls upon the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing poultice to draw out the inflammation, and leave it be without further interference.
If however it does not tolerate this and you are unable to achieve the reduction because time has passed, yet the joint remains according to its original deformity; and nothing is harmful to an infirm person except that the hand becomes weak, and they cannot grasp anything: then know that nerves have been severed or damaged.
It is thus not to be treated with anything but the caustic near its origin. And perhaps nothing will benefit it.
Thus if Almahassem does not return, then apply over it the poultice which we have described. It should then be bound, and left for five days; afterwards it may be unbound and left, while the hand is exercised.
If however it loses its motion, and something heavy happens to it; then treat it gently with warm water and frequent massage until it is soothed.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
When any of the fingers are dislocated towards either side, or towards the back of the palm or its base; then extend the finger and push at the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the finger's head, and suspend it towards the side to which it is dislocated; and leave it thus for two days.
Then hang him up and stretch him until he equals his usual straightness.
So when it's night, bind him according to the same account: and do not cease unbinding him by day: and exercise him with movement: and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until it becomes firm.
And do likewise with it if the wrist is dislocated towards the belly side; bind him to that part and perform your first procedure on him until he is healed.
And do the same when it's dislocated towards other parts.
 On the treatment of spine dislocations in the back.
When someone has a complete spinal or neck dislocation, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places, then there is no cure for it since death approaches the sick person.
And its sign is that the sick person's ejection happens without their will and they cannot hold it back; and often some of his limbs soften from this, or both legs, or both arms, or one of them; or a single vertebra moves out of place.
For often it is moved that which is often dislocated towards the four quarters.
So what moves backwards is called gibbosity.
However, the cure for it is to consider then that if gibbosity happens from childhood, there is no cure and absolutely no recovery.
In those cases that occur due to accident, or impact, or similar causes, the ancients have already spoken of the methods of treatment in great detail. Of which little returns with profit.
I, however, have already abbreviated that which has little excuse for its abundance due to my close proximity to the intention: and by my exposition in which I explained it, and formed his instrument in a manner different from that in which they presented it, and exposed it.
I say, therefore, that in the hump which occurs from the front part, there is neither a remedy nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which appears on two sides as well.
And they are not cured of them, except for that which occurs on the back itself with what I relate: and it is such that the invalid should be extended on his face onto a level platform near to the wall: and a moist covering should be spread beneath him so as not to injure his chest.
Then let a straight stick be placed fixed into the ground at the end of the platform near his head: and another stick about his feet at the other end of the platform: and the assistant should hold the stick, which is not firmly anchored into the ground. And another assistant should hold the other one.
Then roll over his head and underneath the patient's armpits a soft but strong belt or strap: and extend the end of the strap to the stick which is by his head: and tie him to it.
Then tighten the other strap over his waist, and over his knees, and near to his ankles. Then gather all the ligaments and tie them to another piece of wood that is by his feet.
Then each assistant should pull on a piece of wood from the side of the ligaments and not remove the two pieces of wood from their original places where they are fixed; however, as we said before, they must not be secured.
And the doctor should place their palms on top of the vertebrae with strength until it returns to its original position or they can put a plank over them instead. Then the doctor should attach their feet on top of the plank until it returns to its original position.
If however it does not return with this cure then you should take a plank whose length is about three arm-lengths. And the hole we spoke about earlier that is located on the wall near the weak spot should be where the end of the plank fits into it. Then place the middle part of the board over the vertebrae. And then the doctor should put their feet on top of the other end of the plank. And pull hard until the vertebrae is tightened back into place and returns to its original position.
Then if you like, use the drill that is powered by hand and it is as though you are pushing into the ground at the head of the patient two pieces of wood whose length is one arm-length each; and again at his head with another two pieces of wood at the other end of the plank.
And the distance between each piece of wood should be one hand-span; and now it is made so that there are holes at every place where the drill will pass through them and all the pieces of wood must be very well secured into the ground to ensure they do not move at all.
A round piece of wood is introduced and there are two pegs on which a cord may be twisted that go through two holes of the two pieces of wood.
At its end is a hole into which a piece of wood the length of one's palm is fixed to be wound around; similarly there are two other pieces of wood on each side for this purpose.
Then tighten the cords you have tied around the patient's chest with a peg by their head, and those that are binding their legs with a peg at their feet.
A helper should stand by each peg and turn the handle with which it is wound; the physician meanwhile adjusts the bone as we have described.
This is the form of the pegs that follow.
Then, once the bone is returned to its place and adjusted, it must be fixed there with a drying plaster and egg white. Next, apply lint. After this, place above the plaster a splint of wood that is three fingers in width or thereabouts. Its length should be sufficient to support the place of prominence as well as the healthy portion of the bone above it.
Then bind it with the cord we have described. The patient should be fed soft food until they recover.
If there is any remaining prominence at the end of healing then softening and easing medicines should be administered with a long-term course of the tablets we have described. And now a sheet of lead is applied to it.
And sometimes there is a lump at the very end of the spine; it is thought that this is due to a dislocation and indeed this bone already protrudes. Why it is not appropriate to presume that he will be healed by this medicine; for perhaps it may cause death.
 On the treatment of a dislocated shoulder.
You should know that a shoulder or arm joint can only be dislocated; unlike other joints they do not become slightly displaced or concave.
Indeed, the shoulder joint becomes dislocated in four ways. That is to say it may be displaced either forwards or backwards.
Most often the shoulder becomes dislocated inwards; it is rarely displaced either forwards or backwards.
The treatment for a shoulder dislocated inwards is as follows: when you join the healthy leg of the patient with their injured leg, it will be longer; the knee of the injured side will also protrude more than on the other side and they cannot bend that foot up to the groin area. There will be a place below the groin which has become swollen due to the head of the thigh bone now protruding there.
A shoulder that is dislocated outwards will present the opposite of these symptoms.
For someone whose shoulder is dislocated forwards the symptoms are as follows: they will stretch their healthy leg fully but cannot bend it at the knee without pain; when attempting to walk they are unable to take steps forward and instead drag that foot along the ground, even stepping on their own heel in doing so. Their urine is retained and the groin area becomes swollen.
And the sign of dislocation to the rear is that they cannot straighten their knee nor bend it until they have bent their hip; and their leg is also shorter than the other; and their hip is softened, and the head of the thighbone is by the groin.
The reduction of these types of dislocations is as follows: consider then that if the dislocation is old and has existed for some time, the one who has it. Then if you administer the reduction of it, and it remains as it was before: then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore it should not be assumed that an operation should be performed on them.
But in the case of one whose dislocation is recent and is among one of four types of dislocation: then hasten to this, that you roll the joint, and extend it towards the inside and outside, and move it right and left; perhaps it will go back into place, and no further treatment will be needed.
If however it does not go back in this way, then you should prepare a strong assistant, and have them stretch their leg from below: either with their hands or with a strap which is tied above their knee; another assistant stretches out their hand on top, so as to put their hands under their toes. Then bind with a soft strap above the hip joint, and hold one end of another strap.
And let the direction of the stretching be either from the front of the hip to the side of the pelvis, or from behind towards the back.
And let all of them stretch at the same time until the patient is raised from the ground with their body and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among the four types.
If then the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then for every type it is necessary that I speak of the proper cure.
In its proper reduction, when the dislocation is inward, the patient must lie on their healthy side. Then place a bandage over the root of the hip bone, at the point between the hip joint and the area beneath the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to the higher part of the body, toward the fork.
Then let another strong assistant take hold of his arms, reduce the thick area of the affected hip, and extend it outward with a vigorous extension. Now it has returned to its place.
And this type of treatment is easier than the other types for reducing this limb.
But if it slips away from you and does not obey your attempt with this type of treatment, then the two feet of the patient must be bound together with a strong yet gentle bandage over the two heels and above the knees; each length should measure four fingers' breadth from its corresponding point; and let the affected leg remain extended two fingers' length beyond the other.
Then let the patient be suspended from the head of a wooden beam within the house; its length should measure two arms' lengths from the ground.
Next, instruct a strong youth to secure the hip joint; let another person be present with the patient; and have this other youth push against the one securing the hip with strength because the joint returns swiftly to its place.
In his own reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary for the patient to lie on a bench as we described for one with a hunchback. And the strap should be tightened over his leg, that is, the patient's, and over his chest.
Then place two sticks, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then put an additional stick in the middle of the bench, fixed well; over which are wrapped wet cloths so as not to hurt the patient; and let this stick be between his thighs, so that he is not pulled down during the extension.
Then let each assistant pull on their side, while the physician adjusts the reduction with his hand.
If it therefore yields to its return, it will be good.
And if not, place him on the same board again, and press it as we said for hunchbacks, evenly. However, the patient should lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg must be extended quickly; and it follows according to this same narrative on the bench: and the physician should place the palm of their right hand over the patient's groin. Then grasp it with the other hand, and while grasping, place him extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is backwards: then the patient need not be extended downwards and raised off the ground; instead, he should be placed on a hard surface; just as it must also be done when his hip is dislocated to the outside, according to what we said about his lying on two boards: and he is on his face, with straps extended; as we recently stated.
The compression must be applied with a board over the area where the joint extends when reduction is completed as required.
Its sign of recovery will not be hidden from you; this is when you extend the patient's two legs and observe them as equal in length, and the patient tightens their leg muscle, flexes it without assistance; then you know that already the limb has returned to its proper position. Then bring together the patients two thighs, apply a bandage, and tighten with an imemati, that is, a pulley-wheel, so that no movement of any kind occurs at the joint; and the patient should rest for three or four days.
Next, release the ligature and bandage, compare the patient's leg with their other one; then if you observe them as equal length in this position, know that already the joint has been set fast; therefore discharge the patient to walk.
And if you observe any degree of softening; then reapply a bandage and tighten it according to your original technique; release the patient after three more days.
Then release it again and make the patient slow down their walking over both legs for a few days until they strengthen well.
On dislocations of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards - that is, backwards.
And it does not dislocate forwards.
The sign of its dislocation is when you instruct the patient to bring their leg up against their hip. If it does not adhere to the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all types of dislocations is to make the patient sit upright so that they can now stretch out their leg if possible over it and attach your back to their face; then place their foot between your feet.
Then attach their knee to your palms and straighten them between your fingers above their knee. Then press with your palm the two sides of their knee firmly. And have another assistant stretch out their foot until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of recovery is when the leg adheres to the hip with ease without exerting effort.
Then bandage it, and make sure that the leg adheres to the hip. Afterwards bind them together with thread for three or four days. Then untie them.
The patient should not walk for several days until they have fully healed.
If however it resists reduction, then apply strong traction with the bandages described earlier in treatment for hip dislocation until it returns.
# On the Treatment of Heel Dislocation #
The heel bone is sometimes slightly displaced, and at other times completely dislocated posteriorly. And its dislocation occurs either towards the inside or outside.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see an inflamed heel bone proceeding towards the side to which it has been dislocated.
The cure for its removal is an easy return, and it is to be stretched with ease by hand until it returns into place.
In its cure when it is dislocated according to the limb, you must have the patient sit upright and have a strong assistant hold them firmly from behind at the middle of their back.
Then you hold his foot with your right hand from its upper part and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot where the instep is located.
Then draw the foot towards you with your right hand. Then pull it with two fingers of your left hand.
Then stretch it out with the left hand, and push the sole of the foot towards the leg without effort.
Do this twice as we have described.
Then push the sole of the foot towards the leg for a third time, you then pull on the instep.
If it then returns by the first or second finger according to this method and you see the foot aligned, it is good; if not, then repeat the procedure on it since it has returned.
If however the reduction is prevented with what we have described, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground and fix a pole firmly into the earth so that it falls between their hips; you should already have wrapped a cloth around him so he isn't hurt.
Then let one assistant take hold of his hip; afterwards let another assistant stretch out the foot, either with their hands or with a strap that is tied around the ankle. Then let all the assistants stretch in opposition to each other's stretching: and a stick should be held between the patient's two hips, preventing their body from being pulled down towards their feet during the stretching.
Then let the doctor reduce the dislocation with their hands, while another assistant holds the patient's healthy leg down towards their feet. A dislocation returns swiftly.
When the dislocation returns and you are informed of its return: then apply a plaster and bandage, binding it tightly with strips, and tie the foot to the leg with a strap.
You should also be wary of nerves that are above the back of the heel so as not to place a strap over them tightly and damage them.
Then let the patient rest for two or three days. If the strap loosens, tighten it; then release it on the third or fourth day and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. Should the patient attempt to walk before this time has passed, it is not safe as the strap may come undone and the joint will be damaged beyond repair with no hope of future recovery.
If the patient develops a hot swelling then it is necessary to apply cold compresses as described previously at another point of this guide, and an ointment, until it resolves.
On the dislocation of the toes of the foot and its treatment.
It is necessary that what has been dislocated from them be reset with a little extension without effort: and this indeed is not difficult, but rather easy.
If however the dislocation is in certain joints of the foot's back; then it is necessary that you make the patient sit their foot on an even spot on the ground or on a board: and they stand like walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from those joints. Then push it with your foot with strength until it returns, and you see it now evened out, and the protrusion is no longer visible at that spot.
Then place under their foot's belly a board with two ends that fits the whole foot. Then bind it tight with an appropriate binding for three days. Then loosen it and have them rest from walking for many days until they are bound fast and secure by the resetting.
Regarding types of dislocation with injury or fracture...
When something of this sort happens and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often ensues; because of that it is not appropriate to presume upon curing one such as this unless you are wise in the art through long practice, merciful, pious, cautious, not rash or presumptuous; and so you should use at first only medicines which cool hot swellings: and leave the patient to divine judgement. Unless you perceive, or hope for recovery from ruin with mildness of illness, and where there appears something of hope. Try therefore to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before hot pus forms.
If therefore the member returns as we wish, then administer a regime that settles the pus: and treat the wound with what suits it from drying ointments.
If the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture, and separate splinters occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and make in it what we said earlier regarding simple illnesses that precede them in those places.
And beware according to your virtue, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: as my earlier admonition to you stated.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and safer for your body among them during your time.
